Section 1 — Introduction
1.1 Supraharmonics (2–150 kHz) as an emerging power-quality problem
Q1: What definitions and frequency-band boundaries for “supraharmonics” (2–150 kHz) are used across power-quality literature, and how do they differ from classical harmonics and PLC bands?

Across recent peer-reviewed power-quality studies (2021–present), “supraharmonics” (SH) are consistently defined as conducted voltage/current components in the 2–150 kHz range, i.e., above the classical harmonic range (up to ~2 kHz) and below typical radiated EMI bands (Alfalahi et al., 2021); (de Falco & Varilone, 2021); (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024).
Classical harmonics are tied to integer multiples of 50/60 Hz and typically assessed up to 2–2.4 kHz, whereas SH are mainly switching-related, wideband or narrowband emissions from power electronics (Sandrolini & Mariscotti, 2021); (Peiris et al., 2025).
Power-line communication (PLC) signals occupy intentional sub-bands within the same 2–150 kHz interval, creating overlap and potential interference with SH (Wasowski et al., 2021); (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024).
________________________________________
2) Evidence synthesis
A. Frequency-band definitions of supraharmonics (2–150 kHz)
	SH are defined as voltage/current distortions in the 2–150 kHz band, beyond the traditional harmonic range (Alfalahi et al., 2021); (de Falco & Varilone, 2021); (Peiris et al., 2025).
	The lower boundary (2 kHz) marks the transition from classical harmonic analysis methods to higher-frequency EMI-like behavior (Sandrolini & Mariscotti, 2021).
	The upper boundary (150 kHz) aligns with conducted-emission regulatory practice and precedes radiated EMI bands (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024).
	Several empirical studies explicitly measure emissions across 2–150 kHz in EV chargers, UPS systems, lighting, and industrial drives, reinforcing this boundary consensus (Slangen et al., 2023); (Sutaria & Rönnberg, 2023); (Frydlewicz et al., 2024).
Key difference from classical harmonics: classical harmonics are integer multiples of 50/60 Hz and typically assessed up to 2–2.4 kHz, while SH are switching-frequency–related components not harmonically tied to the mains fundamental (Sandrolini & Mariscotti, 2021); (Peiris et al., 2025).
________________________________________
B. Sub-band treatments within 2–150 kHz
	Some empirical studies separate 0–2 kHz (harmonics) and 9–150 kHz (supraharmonics) for measurement alignment with compatibility and emission practices (Frydlewicz et al., 2024).
	Measurement research highlights that methods used below 2 kHz cannot be directly extended to 2–150 kHz because SH are impulsive, time-varying, and wideband (Sandrolini & Mariscotti, 2021); (Li et al., 2023).
	Statistical characterization studies treat SH as a distinct distortion class with dedicated indices (e.g., total SH distortion) in 2–150 kHz (de Falco & Varilone, 2021).
Implication: While 2–150 kHz is broadly accepted, practical measurement often subdivides it (e.g., starting at 9 kHz for quasi-peak EMI methods), reflecting differing regulatory traditions (Lodetti et al., 2023).
________________________________________
C. Distinction from PLC frequency bands
	PLC systems intentionally inject communication signals within parts of the 2–150 kHz interval, overlapping with SH emissions (Wasowski et al., 2021).
	Studies document that non-intentional SH can interfere with PLC performance and vice versa (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024).
Key difference:
	Supraharmonics = unintended switching-related emissions (2–150 kHz).
	PLC bands = intentional communication signals within sub-bands of the same frequency interval.
	Classical harmonics = integer multiples of 50/60 Hz up to ~2 kHz.
________________________________________

Slangen et al.	2023	Field measurements	4×350 kW EV chargers	SH current summation	EV FCS emit 2–150 kHz SH	Single installation
Mariscotti & Mingotti	2024	Analytical + case data	MV/LV grids	Impact analysis	SH (2–150 kHz) cause losses, PLC interference	Assumption-based limits
________________________________________
4) Gaps and future research
	No globally harmonized measurement boundary between 2–9 kHz and 9–150 kHz.
	Limited consensus on standardized SH indices comparable to THD.
	Need clearer regulatory separation between unintended SH and intentional PLC emissions.
	More field data above 20–30 kHz in diverse grid topologies.
	Unified modeling frameworks bridging harmonics (<2 kHz), SH (2–150 kHz), and EMI (>150 kHz).

Q2: What measurement methodologies and instrumentation requirements are reported for supraharmonic emissions (sampling rates, windowing, frequency resolution, uncertainty), and what limitations are repeatedly identified?
Recent empirical studies (2021–present) report that supraharmonic (2–150 kHz) measurements require high sampling rates (≥300 kHz to >1 MHz), carefully selected windowing (e.g., Nuttall, STFT), narrow resolution bandwidths (e.g., 200–500 Hz in 9–150 kHz), and explicit uncertainty evaluation, especially when using quasi-peak (QP) detectors (Sandrolini & Mariscotti, 2021); (Mariscotti et al., 2022); (Gallarreta et al., 2022).
Repeated limitations include lack of harmonized measurement standards, sensitivity to detector configuration, reproducibility issues, spectral leakage, time-variance of emissions, and high-frequency uncertainty in instrument transformers (ITs) (Lodetti et al., 2023); (Rom et al., 2025).
________________________________________
2) Evidence synthesis
A. Sampling rates and acquisition bandwidth
	Measurement up to 150 kHz requires sampling rates well above Nyquist (typically several hundred kHz to MHz) to allow digital filtering and accurate spectral estimation (Sandrolini & Mariscotti, 2021).
	Wideband sensing chains and digitizers are developed specifically to cover 50 Hz–150 kHz (and beyond), highlighting the need for extended-frequency instrumentation (Agazar et al., 2025).
	Current transformer calibration up to 150 kHz shows frequency-dependent uncertainty growth, requiring dedicated wideband analyzers (Rom et al., 2025).
Limitation: Conventional PQ instrumentation designed for ≤2 kHz cannot be directly extended to 150 kHz without significant accuracy loss (Sandrolini & Mariscotti, 2021).
________________________________________
B. Windowing, time–frequency methods, and frequency resolution
	STFT settings (window length, overlap, tapering such as Nuttall window) significantly affect systematic error and uncertainty in 2–150 kHz spectra (Sandrolini & Mariscotti, 2021).
	Resolution bandwidth (RBW) choices of 200 Hz and 500 Hz were evaluated for emission aggregation studies, showing RBW impacts measured distortion levels (Mariscotti et al., 2022).
	Parametric methods (matrix pencil, compressive sensing, dynamic phasors) improve frequency localization and time resolution compared to classical DFT, especially for time-varying emissions (Li et al., 2023); (Frigo & Braun, 2022).
Limitation: Spectral leakage, unstable switching frequencies, and impulsive emissions reduce DFT reliability unless carefully tuned (Sandrolini & Mariscotti, 2021).
________________________________________
C. Quasi-peak detection and RMS–QP relationships
	CISPR 16 quasi-peak (QP) methods show implementation-dependent variability due to broad tolerances and “black-box” definitions (Lodetti et al., 2023).
	Digital QP implementations using adapted IEC 61000-4-7 approaches provide comparable results but reduce complexity (Gallarreta et al., 2022).
	Empirical RMS–QP relationships allow approximate QP estimation from RMS measurements in 9–150 kHz (Gallarreta et al., 2023).
Limitation: QP results vary across compliant implementations, reducing reproducibility and comparability (Lodetti et al., 2023).
________________________________________
D. Instrument transformer and sensing-chain uncertainty
	Wideband IT calibration shows uncertainty rising from ppm-level (<10 ppm up to 10 kHz) to significantly higher levels above 10 kHz (Rom et al., 2025).
	High-voltage sensing chains require dual-path designs (divider + high-pass filtering) to improve signal-to-noise ratio for low-level SH components (Agazar et al., 2025).
Limitation: Parasitic impedances, grounding, cable type, and shunt selection strongly affect uncertainty at high frequencies (Rom et al., 2025).
________________________________________

________________________________________
4) Gaps and future research
	Harmonized sampling-rate and RBW prescriptions for 2–150 kHz.
	Unified uncertainty budgets for SH measurements including IT behavior.
	Standardized digital implementation guidance for QP detectors.
	Improved methods for highly time-varying and impulsive emissions.
	Field validation of parametric high-resolution methods under real grid conditions.

Q3: What grid-impact mechanisms are most consistently evidenced for supraharmonics (transformer heating, resonances, interference, protection/metering effects), and what empirical studies quantify these impacts?
Across recent empirical studies (2021–present), the most consistently evidenced grid impacts of supraharmonics (2–150 kHz) are: (i) resonances and impedance amplification in MV/LV networks, (ii) heating and additional losses in transformers, cables, and dielectric components, (iii) interference with communication systems (especially PLC and telephony), and (iv) metering/protection inaccuracies due to instrument transformer behavior at high frequency. Resonance-driven amplification and propagation are repeatedly demonstrated in field and lab studies (Letha et al., 2021); (Sutaria & Rönnberg, 2023). Heating, power loss, and insulation stress mechanisms are analytically and experimentally quantified in MV/LV grids (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024). Interference and coupling to communication circuits are empirically documented for PLC and telephone systems (Wasowski et al., 2021); (Singh et al., 2023). Instrument transformer frequency-response deviations and uncertainty growth above 10 kHz affect metering and monitoring reliability (Rom et al., 2025).
________________________________________
2) Evidence synthesis
A. Resonances and impedance-driven amplification
	MV feeder studies show that network topology and feeder length determine supraharmonic resonance frequencies and transfer impedance peaks, which can amplify emissions (Letha et al., 2021).
	Data-center UPS measurements demonstrate that connection mode and filter configuration can create resonance in phase conductors, modifying emission magnitude and propagation (Sutaria & Rönnberg, 2023).
	Bidirectional EV charging stations significantly alter high-frequency grid impedance, creating new resonance points when connected (Grasel et al., 2022).
Consistent mechanism: Interaction between converter output impedance and network impedance creates frequency-selective amplification.
________________________________________
B. Transformer, cable, and dielectric heating / losses
	Analytical and grid-based studies show supraharmonics contribute to additional power loss, heating of transformers and cables, dielectric aging, and possible MV cable termination failures (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024).
	Case-based impedance interaction studies in DC grids quantify spectral aggregation effects that increase high-frequency voltage levels, influencing EMC stress and losses (Khilnani et al., 2022).
Consistent mechanism: Frequency-dependent losses (skin effect, dielectric losses) increase with SH magnitude, especially under resonance.
________________________________________
C. Interference with communication systems
	Laboratory studies show distorted supply conditions affect non-intentional supraharmonic emissions and degrade PLC communication performance (Wasowski et al., 2021).
	A U.S. case study documents solar PV inverter switching frequencies coupling through grounding/neutral paths, causing telephone interference for nearby customers (Singh et al., 2023).
Consistent mechanism: Conducted or coupled supraharmonics overlap PLC/telephony bands, reducing signal-to-noise ratio or causing audible interference.
________________________________________
D. Protection, metering, and instrument transformer effects
	Wideband calibration of current transformers shows measurement uncertainty rises from ppm-level at ≤10 kHz to significantly higher uncertainty up to 150 kHz, impacting PQ monitoring accuracy (Rom et al., 2025).
	Advanced sensing chains demonstrate that dedicated high-pass paths are required to correctly measure low-amplitude SH components for compliance assessment (Agazar et al., 2025).
Consistent mechanism: Standard instrument transformers and PQ meters designed for 50/60 Hz exhibit frequency-dependent ratio and phase errors at SH frequencies.
________________________________________

________________________________________
4) Gaps and future research
	Field-quantified thermal rise in distribution transformers under measured SH levels.
	Long-term aging studies linking SH to insulation degradation.
	Standardized impedance-based resonance risk assessment tools.
	Protection relay performance testing under 2–150 kHz distortion.
	Broader statistical evidence of communication interference across grid types.

1.2 EV chargers and converter-interfaced loads as dominant stochastic sources
Q1: Which converter-interfaced devices are most frequently identified as dominant contributors to supraharmonic emissions (EVSE, PV inverters, LED drivers, variable-speed drives), and what evidence supports this ranking?
Across empirical studies since 2021, EV charging equipment (EVSE) and PV inverters are most frequently identified as dominant contributors to supraharmonic (2–150 kHz) emissions, followed by variable-speed drives (VSDs) and LED/CFL lighting drivers. Field and laboratory measurements consistently show EVSE and PV systems produce both narrowband and wideband emissions that interact with grid impedance and can propagate beyond the point of connection (Grasel et al., 2022); (Singh et al., 2023). VSDs and lighting drivers also generate measurable supraharmonics, but reported magnitudes and system-level impacts are typically lower or more localized (Dhayaneswaran, 2024); (Wasowski et al., 2021).
Overall ranking (most evidenced to least):
	EVSE
	PV inverters
	Variable-speed drives
	LED/CFL drivers
________________________________________
2) Evidence synthesis
A. EV Charging Equipment (EVSE) – Most frequently reported dominant source
	Measurements of a bidirectional V2G EV charging station show stable supraharmonic emissions across operating points, including both wideband and narrowband components, and significant impact on high-frequency grid impedance (Grasel et al., 2022).
	Field measurements of fast-charging stations demonstrate measurable 2–150 kHz current emissions that increase with the number of active chargers, confirming aggregation effects (Slangen et al., 2023).
Evidence strength: Multiple empirical field studies with quantification of emission magnitude and aggregation → highest consistency.
________________________________________
B. PV Inverters – Strong and widely documented contributor
	A U.S. case study shows PV inverter switching frequencies coupling through grounding paths, causing measurable telephone interference in nearby customers (Singh et al., 2023).
	MV network measurements around wind/PV-connected feeders reveal time-varying supraharmonic emissions and resonance-driven amplification (Letha et al., 2021).
Evidence strength: Field-validated propagation and interference cases → second highest frequency of documented dominant behavior.
________________________________________
C. Variable-Speed Drives (VSDs) – Significant in industrial settings
	Textile industry measurements show VSD-based motor drives produce measurable supraharmonics; hybrid analysis confirms converter switching as the source (Dhayaneswaran, 2024).
	Laboratory investigations of industrial loads demonstrate that converter-based drives contribute to non-intentional emissions in 2–150 kHz, particularly under distorted supply conditions (Wasowski et al., 2021).
Evidence strength: Clear emission presence in industrial contexts, but fewer large-scale aggregation studies compared to EVSE/PV.
________________________________________
D. LED and CFL Drivers – Localized but numerous sources
	Experimental studies show LED and CFL drivers generate supraharmonic emissions that vary with supply voltage distortion (Wasowski et al., 2021).
	Emissions are typically lower power than EVSE or PV converters, but high population density suggests potential cumulative contribution (Wasowski et al., 2021).
Evidence strength: Demonstrated emission source, but rarely identified as dominant in grid-level case studies.
________________________________________

________________________________________
4) Gaps and future research
	Large-scale statistical comparison of emission magnitudes across device classes.
	Aggregation modeling of LED drivers at neighborhood scale.
	Cross-country comparative studies of EVSE penetration vs SH levels.
	Ranking based on standardized emission metrics (e.g., total supraharmonic distortion).
	Long-term monitoring datasets distinguishing PV vs EVSE contributions.

Q2: What emission models (Norton/Thevenin equivalents, frequency-dependent current injection spectra, time-varying operating states) are used for EV chargers and similar converters in 2–150 kHz studies?
Empirical studies (2021–present) model EV chargers and similar converters in the 2–150 kHz range primarily using (i) Norton equivalent current-source models with frequency-dependent impedance, (ii) Thevenin/Norton dual representations for emission–impedance interaction, (iii) frequency-domain injection spectra derived from measurements (RBW-based aggregation), and (iv) time-varying or state-dependent models reflecting operating modes and switching variability. Norton equivalents are widely used to represent supraharmonic current injection and enable aggregation studies (Mariscotti et al., 2022). Time-varying dynamic phasor and parametric models are applied to capture non-stationary emissions (Frigo & Braun, 2022); (Li et al., 2023). Field studies of EV chargers and PV inverters confirm that emission spectra vary with operating state, supporting state-dependent modeling (Slangen et al., 2023); (Sutaria & Rönnberg, 2023).
________________________________________
2) Evidence synthesis
A. Norton Equivalent Models (Current Source + Frequency-Dependent Impedance)
	DC-grid studies model converters using a Norton equivalent circuit, combining a frequency-dependent current source with measured input impedance to analyze emission superposition and aggregation (Mariscotti et al., 2022).
	The Norton representation enables Monte Carlo simulations of phase-randomized emission aggregation and impedance interaction in multi-converter systems (Mariscotti et al., 2022).
Key feature: Converter represented as a current injection spectrum with parallel impedance, allowing interaction with grid impedance to be evaluated.
________________________________________
B. Frequency-Domain Injection Spectra (RBW-Based Aggregation)
	EV fast-charging station studies quantify supraharmonic emissions as frequency-domain current spectra in 2–150 kHz, used to analyze diversity factors and summation behavior (Slangen et al., 2023).
	Spectral aggregation models in DC grids use defined resolution bandwidths (e.g., 200–500 Hz) to construct injection spectra and assess superposition (Mariscotti et al., 2022).
Key feature: Measured spectral amplitudes directly serve as emission inputs without detailed switching-level modeling.
________________________________________
C. Thevenin/Norton Dual Representation for Impedance Interaction
	Grid-impedance interaction studies of EVSE demonstrate that connecting a charger alters high-frequency grid impedance and can generate resonance points, implying modeling via source–impedance equivalents (Grasel et al., 2022).
	Propagation studies in MV networks analyze driving-point impedance and transfer impedance versus frequency, effectively treating emission sources within equivalent network representations (Letha et al., 2021).
Key feature: Equivalent circuit framework used to capture resonance and propagation.
________________________________________
D. Time-Varying / State-Dependent Emission Models
	Dynamic phasor modeling uses compressive sensing and Taylor–Fourier multifrequency representations to estimate time-varying supraharmonic components (Frigo & Braun, 2022).
	Matrix pencil methods improve frequency localization of time-varying emissions and are robust against colored noise (Li et al., 2023).
	Field data from EVSE and UPS systems show emission magnitude depends on operating mode and power level, supporting state-dependent models (Slangen et al., 2023); (Sutaria & Rönnberg, 2023).
Key feature: Emission spectra are non-stationary and must be modeled as operating-state dependent.
________________________________________

4) Gaps and future research
	Unified framework linking Norton equivalents with time-varying spectral estimation.
	Standardized parameter extraction methods for converter impedance in 2–150 kHz.
	Comparative validation of Thevenin vs Norton modeling accuracy in field networks.
	Aggregation models incorporating stochastic operating-state transitions.
	Integration of emission models into grid-wide EMT simulations.

Q3: What statistical variability is reported for converter emissions (across devices, operating points, control modes, grid impedance), and how is this variability parameterised (distributions, confidence intervals)?
Empirical studies (2021–present) consistently report high statistical variability of converter supraharmonic emissions across devices, operating points, control modes, and grid impedance conditions. Variability is parameterised using probability density functions (PDFs), cumulative distribution functions (CDFs), Monte Carlo phase randomisation, diversity factors, RMS–QP statistical relationships, and expanded uncertainty (k=2) confidence intervals (de Falco & Varilone, 2021); (Mariscotti et al., 2022); (Gallarreta et al., 2023). Field studies show emission magnitude changes non-linearly with operating mode and load, and differs significantly between nominally identical devices (Sutaria & Rönnberg, 2023); (Slangen et al., 2023).
________________________________________
2) Evidence synthesis
A. Variability across devices and installations
	Statistical analysis of LV networks shows supraharmonic voltage levels exhibit significant dispersion across measurement locations, with non-Gaussian distributions in many frequency bins (de Falco & Varilone, 2021).
	EV fast-charging stations demonstrate diversity effects: aggregated emissions do not sum linearly, and variation between individual chargers affects total spectral levels (Slangen et al., 2023).
Parameterisation: Empirical PDFs, percentile levels, diversity factors.
________________________________________
B. Variability across operating points and control modes
	UPS systems in data centers show emission magnitude depends on operating mode (online vs bypass) and load type, with non-linear dependency on power level (Sutaria & Rönnberg, 2023).
	Bidirectional EVSE measurements reveal that while some supraharmonic components remain stable, harmonic and low-frequency distortion vary significantly at low power levels (Grasel et al., 2022).
Parameterisation: Operating-point-dependent spectra; comparative statistics across load levels.
________________________________________
C. Variability due to impedance interaction and resonance
	MV feeder studies show resonance peaks and transfer impedance vary widely between feeders with similar topology, leading to large differences in observed emission levels (Letha et al., 2021).
	DC-grid aggregation studies use Monte Carlo phase randomisation to simulate variability due to impedance interaction and switching phase differences (Mariscotti et al., 2022).
Parameterisation: Monte Carlo simulations; impedance-dependent spectral spread.
________________________________________
D. Statistical relationships between measurement metrics
	Empirical RMS–QP relationships are derived from large datasets of 9–150 kHz voltage measurements using regression-based statistical modeling (Gallarreta et al., 2023).
	Confidence intervals and expanded uncertainties (k=2) are reported for wideband current transformer calibration, showing frequency-dependent uncertainty growth (Rom et al., 2025).
Parameterisation: Regression equations, empirical correction factors, expanded uncertainty budgets.
________________________________________

	Standardised probabilistic models (e.g., log-normal vs heavy-tailed fitting) for SH emission distributions.

	Integration of stochastic emission models into grid-wide harmonic simulations.
	Long-term temporal variability analysis (seasonal, diurnal effects).
	Unified statistical descriptors for compatibility-level assessment.

1.3 Why aggregation is statistical (asynchrony, cancellation, impedance selectivity)
Q1: What theoretical explanations and empirical observations support phase asynchrony and partial cancellation in aggregated supraharmonic emissions from many converters?
Recent empirical and modeling studies (2021–present) show that aggregated supraharmonic (2–150 kHz) emissions from many converters often exhibit partial cancellation due to phase asynchrony, switching-frequency dispersion, and impedance-mediated interaction. Monte Carlo phase-randomization models demonstrate that random relative phases between converters reduce total emission below arithmetic summation (Mariscotti et al., 2022). Field measurements of EV fast-charging stations confirm non-linear aggregation and diversity effects (Slangen et al., 2023). Network impedance and resonance further shape constructive and destructive superposition, explaining location-dependent amplification or attenuation (Letha et al., 2021).
________________________________________
2) Evidence synthesis
A. Theoretical basis: Random phase superposition and diversity
	DC-grid aggregation studies model converters as Norton current sources with independent phase angles and use Monte Carlo simulations to show that random phase differences lead to partial cancellation, especially when switching frequencies are not synchronized (Mariscotti et al., 2022).
	The reduction in aggregate amplitude relative to linear summation is attributed to statistical phase dispersion and switching-frequency variability (Mariscotti et al., 2022).
Key theoretical explanation: When phases are uncorrelated, RMS aggregation follows a root-sum-square tendency rather than arithmetic addition.
________________________________________
B. Empirical evidence from EV fast-charging stations
	Field measurements of multiple 350 kW chargers show that measured supraharmonic currents increase sub-linearly with the number of active units, confirming diversity effects and partial cancellation (Slangen et al., 2023).
	The study quantifies aggregation factors lower than N (number of chargers), supporting statistical superposition models (Slangen et al., 2023).
Empirical observation: Emission diversity depends on manufacturer design and switching control, not just device count.
________________________________________
C. Switching frequency dispersion and time variation
	Time-varying emission analysis using dynamic phasor methods shows that converter supraharmonics are not stationary and exhibit frequency drift and modulation, promoting phase decorrelation over time (Frigo & Braun, 2022).
	Matrix-pencil–based analysis confirms that multiple close-frequency components with independent phase evolution coexist in converter emissions (Li et al., 2023).
Mechanism: Frequency and phase jitter reduce coherent summation over time.
D. Impedance interaction and resonance shaping
	MV feeder studies show that transfer impedance differs between feeders, leading to frequency-selective amplification or attenuation of aggregated emissions (Letha et al., 2021).
	Data-center UPS measurements reveal that operating mode and filter topology influence whether emissions combine constructively or are partially suppressed (Sutaria & Rönnberg, 2023).
Mechanism: Grid impedance acts as a frequency-selective weighting function, enabling both cancellation and resonance-driven reinforcement.
________________________________________
3) Summary table
Study	Context	Method	Evidence for Cancellation	Key Finding	Limitation
Mariscotti et al. (2022)	DC-grid converters	Norton + Monte Carlo	Random phase model	Root-sum-square aggregation	Model-based
Slangen et al. (2023)	EV fast chargers	Field measurement	Sub-linear summation	Diversity factors quantified	Single site
Frigo & Braun (2022)	Converter signals	Dynamic phasor analysis	Time-varying phase drift	Non-stationary emissions	Algorithm validation scope
Li et al. (2023)	Measurement method	Matrix pencil	Multi-component phase evolution	High-resolution phase detection	Lab validation
Letha et al. (2021)	MV network	Impedance modeling	Frequency-selective attenuation	Feeder-dependent amplification	One MV grid
Sutaria & Rönnberg (2023)	Data center UPS	Field + model	Mode-dependent aggregation	Filter/resonance effects	Specific topology
________________________________________
4) Gaps and future research
	Controlled large-scale experiments with synchronized vs unsynchronized converters.
	Statistical modeling of phase distributions across manufacturers.
	Long-term temporal coherence studies in dense EVSE deployments.
	Integration of stochastic phase models into harmonic load-flow tools.
	Quantification of cancellation under active switching-frequency control schemes.

Q2: What analytical frameworks quantify aggregation effects (phasor summation, random-phase models, central-limit approximations, shot-noise/sum-of-random-fields), and what assumptions do they require?
Recent empirical and modeling studies (2021–present) quantify supraharmonic aggregation using four main analytical frameworks: (i) deterministic phasor summation, (ii) random-phase (Monte Carlo) models, (iii) central-limit–type approximations leading to root-sum-square (RSS) scaling, and (iv) stochastic spectral-field/sum-of-random-sources representations for time-varying emissions. Norton-based Monte Carlo aggregation explicitly assumes statistically independent phase angles and known frequency-dependent impedances (Mariscotti et al., 2022). Field studies of EV fast chargers validate sub-linear scaling consistent with random-phase/RSS assumptions (Slangen et al., 2023). Impedance-driven propagation models require linear time-invariant (LTI) network assumptions and frequency-domain superposition (Letha et al., 2021).
________________________________________
2) Evidence synthesis
A. Deterministic phasor summation (worst-case coherent addition)
Framework:
Aggregate emission at each frequency bin is computed by complex phasor addition of individual current sources.
Assumptions:
	Fixed phase relationships (full coherence).
	Linear network behavior.
	Known converter spectra.
Evidence:
Aggregation studies define upper-bound emission levels by coherent phasor summation before applying diversity/random-phase corrections (Mariscotti et al., 2022).
Network propagation modeling in MV grids assumes linear superposition of frequency-domain phasors (Letha et al., 2021).
Limitation: Overestimates realistic aggregation when phase asynchrony exists.
________________________________________
B. Random-phase (Monte Carlo) models
Framework:
Each converter is modeled as a Norton current source with randomly distributed phase angles; Monte Carlo simulation yields statistical aggregate levels.
Assumptions:
	Phase independence between converters.
	Stationary spectral amplitudes.
	Linear impedance network.
Evidence:
Monte Carlo phase-randomization demonstrates reduction from linear summation toward RSS-like behavior (Mariscotti et al., 2022).
Field EV fast-charging data confirm sub-linear scaling consistent with random-phase aggregation (Slangen et al., 2023).
Limitation: Assumes no phase synchronization or correlated control strategies.
________________________________________
C. Central-limit / Root-Sum-Square (RSS) approximations
Framework:
For many statistically independent sources, aggregate RMS ≈ √(ΣIi²), consistent with central-limit-type reasoning.
Assumptions:
	Large number of independent sources.
	Finite variance of emission amplitudes.
	Weak correlation among phase angles.
Evidence:
Empirical diversity factors in EVSE aggregation approach RSS scaling rather than linear growth (Slangen et al., 2023).
Statistical characterization of LV measurements reports non-Gaussian distributions at single sites but aggregation trends toward smoother distributions, consistent with central-limit tendencies (de Falco & Varilone, 2021).
Limitation: May fail when dominant emitters or resonance create correlated amplification.
________________________________________
D. Sum-of-random-fields / Time-varying spectral models
Framework:
Emissions modeled as superposition of multiple time-varying spectral components (random fields in time–frequency space).
Assumptions:
	Emissions are non-stationary.
	Switching frequencies exhibit drift or jitter.
	Statistical independence over time windows.
Evidence:
Dynamic phasor modeling captures time-varying multifrequency emissions, implying statistical decorrelation over time (Frigo & Braun, 2022).
Matrix-pencil–based estimation resolves multiple closely spaced components with evolving phase, supporting stochastic-field interpretation (Li et al., 2023).
Limitation: Requires high-resolution measurement and assumes LTI network behavior during estimation window.
________________________________________
3) Summary table
Framework	Core Idea	Key Assumptions	Supporting Study	Main Limitation
Deterministic phasor summation	Coherent addition of complex currents	Fixed phase, LTI network	Mariscotti et al., 2022; Letha et al., 2021	Overestimates aggregation
Random-phase Monte Carlo	Independent random phase angles	Statistical independence	Mariscotti et al., 2022	Ignores synchronization
RSS / CLT approximation	RMS scales with √N	Large N, finite variance	Slangen et al., 2023; de Falco & Varilone, 2021	Fails with dominant emitters
Stochastic time–frequency superposition	Sum of random spectral components	Non-stationary emissions	Frigo & Braun, 2022; Li et al., 2023	High computational complexity
________________________________________
4) Gaps and future research
	Analytical derivation linking Monte Carlo models to closed-form CLT-based aggregation bounds.
	Validation of random-phase assumptions under coordinated switching control.
	Hybrid models combining stochastic source fields with frequency-dependent impedance uncertainty.
	Large-scale field validation across heterogeneous converter fleets.
	Frameworks incorporating resonance-induced correlation effects.

Electric Power Systems Research. https://consensus.app/papers/summation-of-supraharmonic-currents-2–150-khz-from-ev-fast-slangen-ćuk/2ba7397d15f25611b5f94a624d0dcae5/?utm_source=chatgpt

Q3: How do network impedance and resonance selectivity alter aggregation outcomes (constructive amplification vs attenuation), and which feeder studies demonstrate these effects?

Recent feeder and network studies (2021–present) show that frequency-dependent network impedance and resonance selectively shape supraharmonic (2–150 kHz) aggregation, producing either constructive amplification at resonance frequencies or attenuation away from them. MV feeder simulations and measurements demonstrate that transfer and driving-point impedance peaks determine whether aggregated emissions are amplified or damped (Letha et al., 2021). Field studies in data centers and EV charging installations show that converter connection modifies grid impedance, creating new resonance points that alter aggregation outcomes (Sutaria & Rönnberg, 2023); (Grasel et al., 2022). These results confirm that aggregation cannot be predicted from source statistics alone but must account for impedance selectivity and resonance topology.
________________________________________
2) Evidence synthesis
A. Driving-point and transfer impedance shaping aggregation
	MV network evaluation of eight feeders shows that each feeder exhibits distinct impedance-versus-frequency characteristics, leading to different resonance peaks and different amplification factors for identical emission sources (Letha et al., 2021).
	Larger networks exhibited more resonance frequencies but lower individual peak amplitudes, while feeder length influenced transfer impedance magnitude non-linearly (Letha et al., 2021).
Mechanism:
At resonance frequencies where |Z(f)| is high, aggregated current injections produce amplified voltage distortion (constructive outcome). At non-resonant frequencies, lower impedance attenuates the effect.
________________________________________
B. Converter–network impedance interaction
	Measurements of a bidirectional EV charging station show that connecting the EVSE significantly modifies high-frequency grid impedance and creates additional resonance points (Grasel et al., 2022).
	The added capacitance and filter structure alter frequency-selective behavior, influencing whether emissions combine constructively or are partially damped (Grasel et al., 2022).
Mechanism:
Source impedance and network impedance jointly determine effective emission magnitude at each frequency.
________________________________________
C. Operating-mode–dependent resonance effects (Feeder-level case study)
	In a data center feeder, UPS operating mode (online vs bypass) and filter topology change propagation characteristics, demonstrating that impedance conditions can either amplify or suppress supraharmonics (Sutaria & Rönnberg, 2023).
	Resonance between PE–N filters and feeder impedance resulted in phase-conductor amplification in some modes, while alternative configurations reduced observed distortion (Sutaria & Rönnberg, 2023).
Mechanism:
Local impedance topology determines whether aggregated emissions experience constructive reinforcement or attenuation.
________________________________________
D. Impedance interaction in DC grids (spectral aggregation study)
	DC-grid spectral aggregation analysis shows that the relative magnitude of DC-link capacitor impedance and converter impedance determines the amplitude of spectral aggregates (Khilnani et al., 2022).
	When impedances are comparable, voltage levels decrease (attenuation), while strong mismatch can increase peak amplitudes (constructive aggregation) (Khilnani et al., 2022).
Mechanism:
Frequency-dependent impedance ratios govern voltage amplification factor at each spectral component.
________________________________________
3) Summary table
Study	Network Type	Design	Impedance Effect Observed	Aggregation Outcome	Limitation
Letha et al. (2021)	Swedish MV feeders	Simulation + measurement	Distinct resonance peaks per feeder	Amplification at specific frequencies	Single network
Grasel et al. (2022)	Lab LV grid with EVSE	Measurement	EVSE modifies grid impedance	New resonance points	Lab grid reconstruction
Sutaria & Rönnberg (2023)	Data center feeder	Field + modeling	UPS mode alters impedance	Amplification or suppression	Specific UPS topology
Khilnani et al. (2022)	DC grid	Simulation	DC-link vs converter impedance ratio	Spectral peak increase/decrease	DC-grid focus
________________________________________
4) Gaps and future research
	Large-scale statistical feeder comparisons across countries.
	Standardized resonance risk indices for 2–150 kHz.
	Coupled stochastic–impedance aggregation models.
	Field validation under high EV penetration scenarios.
	Integration of impedance uncertainty into aggregation forecasts.

1.4 Spatial uncertainty and limitations of feeder-specific simulations for generalisation
Q1: How is “spatial uncertainty” defined in power-quality hosting/aggregation studies (unknown device locations, unknown activity patterns, uncertain customer density, uncertain topology)?
In recent power-quality hosting and aggregation studies (2021–present), “spatial uncertainty” refers to uncertainty in the geographical and electrical placement of emission sources within a network, including unknown device locations, unknown simultaneity/activity patterns, uncertain customer density, and uncertain feeder topology or impedance characteristics. MV and LV propagation studies show that identical emission sources produce different distortion levels depending on feeder position and transfer impedance, illustrating location uncertainty (Letha et al., 2021). Aggregation models incorporate stochastic phase, diversity, and probabilistic source distribution to account for unknown activity and placement (Mariscotti et al., 2022); (Slangen et al., 2023). Thus, spatial uncertainty combines geometric location uncertainty and impedance-topology uncertainty, both of which directly affect hosting capacity assessment.
________________________________________
2) Evidence synthesis
A. Unknown device locations along feeders
	MV feeder studies demonstrate that transfer impedance varies significantly with source position, meaning identical emission magnitudes produce different voltage distortion depending on where along the feeder the device is connected (Letha et al., 2021).
	Larger feeders with multiple branches show location-dependent resonance peaks, highlighting spatial variability of amplification (Letha et al., 2021).
Definition component: Spatial uncertainty = unknown connection node and electrical distance from substation.
________________________________________
B. Unknown activity patterns and simultaneity
	EV fast-charging aggregation studies show that total emission depends strongly on how many chargers are active simultaneously, and sub-linear scaling indicates diversity effects (Slangen et al., 2023).
	Monte Carlo aggregation frameworks explicitly treat emission phase and activation states as random variables to account for unknown simultaneity (Mariscotti et al., 2022).
Definition component: Spatial uncertainty includes stochastic activation across geographically dispersed devices.
________________________________________
C. Uncertain customer density and emitter distribution
	Statistical characterization of LV networks shows that measured supraharmonic voltage levels differ significantly across locations, reflecting variation in customer mix and device penetration (de Falco & Varilone, 2021).
	Aggregation modeling treats the number of converters and their spectral amplitudes as random variables when device density is uncertain (Mariscotti et al., 2022).
Definition component: Uncertainty in emitter count and spatial clustering affects hosting predictions.
________________________________________
D. Uncertain feeder topology and impedance characteristics
	MV resonance studies show that feeder configuration (branching, cable length, transformer location) determines resonance frequencies and amplification factors (Letha et al., 2021).
	EVSE impedance studies demonstrate that adding converters modifies grid impedance, meaning topology is not static and must be treated as uncertain when multiple devices connect dynamically (Grasel et al., 2022).
Definition component: Spatial uncertainty includes incomplete knowledge of frequency-dependent network impedance.
________________________________________
3) Summary table
Study	Network Type	Spatial Uncertainty Aspect	Method	Key Finding	Limitation
Letha et al. (2021)	MV feeders	Source location & topology	Simulation + measurement	Resonance depends on feeder position	Single MV region
Slangen et al. (2023)	EV fast charging site	Simultaneity	Field measurement	Sub-linear aggregation	One station
Mariscotti et al. (2022)	DC grid converters	Random activation & phase	Monte Carlo	Statistical aggregation spread	Model assumptions
de Falco & Varilone (2021)	LV networks	Customer density variation	Field measurement	Location-dependent SH levels	Limited sites
Grasel et al. (2022)	LV grid with EVSE	Impedance modification	Measurement	Converter alters topology response	Lab grid
________________________________________
4) Gaps and future research
	Unified probabilistic definitions of spatial uncertainty for hosting-capacity studies.
	Large-scale feeder datasets quantifying emitter density variability.
	Joint modeling of spatial and temporal uncertainty.
	Integration of topology uncertainty into harmonic load-flow solvers.
	Standardized uncertainty descriptors for regulatory assessments.
________________________________________

Q2: What are the documented limitations of feeder-specific time-domain or harmonic-domain simulations for generalisable inference (data requirements, topology dependence, computational cost, transferability)?

Recent studies (2021–present) show that feeder-specific time-domain and harmonic-domain simulations for supraharmonics (2–150 kHz) have limited generalisability due to (i) high data requirements for accurate impedance and device models, (ii) strong topology dependence of resonance behavior, (iii) computational cost of wideband EMT simulations, and (iv) poor transferability across feeders with different configurations. MV propagation studies demonstrate that resonance characteristics differ markedly between feeders, limiting extrapolation (Letha et al., 2021). Aggregation modeling shows that accurate prediction requires detailed frequency-dependent source and impedance data that are rarely available in practice (Mariscotti et al., 2022). Empirical EVSE and data-center studies confirm that operating modes and local impedance conditions substantially alter outcomes, limiting model transferability (Grasel et al., 2022); (Sutaria & Rönnberg, 2023).
________________________________________
2) Evidence synthesis
A. High data requirements (device and impedance modeling)
	Aggregation frameworks require detailed frequency-dependent Norton equivalents and network impedance spectra, which are rarely fully characterized in field networks (Mariscotti et al., 2022).
	MV feeder studies depend on accurate cable parameters, transformer models, and topology descriptions to reproduce resonance behavior (Letha et al., 2021).
Limitation: Missing or uncertain impedance data significantly alters simulation outcomes.
________________________________________
B. Strong topology dependence
	Eight MV feeders with similar voltage levels showed distinct resonance frequencies and amplification magnitudes due to topology differences (Letha et al., 2021).
	EVSE impedance studies demonstrate that adding or removing a single converter modifies high-frequency grid impedance, meaning results depend on current network composition (Grasel et al., 2022).
Limitation: Results from one feeder cannot be assumed valid for another without re-modeling.
________________________________________
C. Computational burden of wideband time-domain simulations
	Time-domain EMT-style approaches require high sampling rates and detailed switching models to capture 2–150 kHz behavior, increasing computational complexity relative to classical harmonic-domain approaches (Frigo & Braun, 2022).
	Matrix-pencil and high-resolution methods are proposed partly to reduce reliance on long time-domain simulations, indicating practical computational constraints (Li et al., 2023).
Limitation: High-resolution EMT simulations are computationally expensive for large feeder studies.
________________________________________
D. Limited transferability and operating-mode sensitivity
	Data-center feeder measurements show that UPS operating mode alters propagation patterns, meaning simulation calibrated for one state may not generalize (Sutaria & Rönnberg, 2023).
	EV fast-charging aggregation results depend on simultaneity and device diversity, limiting extrapolation to other installations (Slangen et al., 2023).
Limitation: Operating-state variability reduces predictive robustness.
________________________________________
3) Summary table
Study	Simulation Type	Main Limitation Identified	Evidence	Transferability Impact
Letha et al. (2021)	Harmonic-domain MV model	Topology-specific resonance	Distinct feeder responses	Low
Mariscotti et al. (2022)	Frequency-domain aggregation	Requires detailed impedance data	Norton + Monte Carlo	Moderate–Low
Grasel et al. (2022)	Impedance measurement + modeling	Device alters network response	EVSE modifies Z(f)	Low
Sutaria & Rönnberg (2023)	Field + propagation model	Mode-dependent behavior	UPS states alter SH	Low
Slangen et al. (2023)	Aggregation modeling	Diversity & simultaneity dependence	Sub-linear scaling	Moderate
Frigo & Braun (2022)	Time-domain analysis	High computational cost	Dynamic phasor complexity	Moderate
________________________________________
4) Gaps and future research
	Development of reduced-order wideband feeder models.
	Standardized impedance databases for representative feeder archetypes.
	Hybrid stochastic–deterministic simulation frameworks.
	Benchmark datasets for cross-feeder validation.
	Transferability metrics for hosting-capacity assessment tools.
________________________________________

Q3: Which probabilistic spatial models have been proposed for distributed energy resources and loads (PPP/Cox/cluster processes, spatial econometrics), and how have they been validated against deployment data?
Since 2021, probabilistic spatial models for distributed energy resources (DERs) and loads have primarily used Poisson point processes (PPP), inhomogeneous/cluster (Cox) processes, and spatial econometric regression frameworks to represent uncertain device locations and adoption density. PPP-based models are applied to EV charging and DER siting under random deployment assumptions, while Cox or cluster processes capture socio-economic heterogeneity and spatial clustering (Zhang et al., 2022); (Wang et al., 2023). Spatial econometric approaches incorporate demographic and grid variables to explain observed deployment patterns and are validated against real installation datasets (Li et al., 2021). Validation is typically performed by comparing simulated spatial intensity functions or clustering metrics against historical installation records or GIS-based deployment maps.
________________________________________
2) Evidence synthesis
A. Poisson Point Process (PPP) models
Framework:
Devices are modeled as spatially independent random points with constant or spatially varying intensity λ(x).
Evidence:
	EV charging infrastructure siting modeled using homogeneous and inhomogeneous PPP to represent random customer adoption under penetration scenarios (Zhang et al., 2022).
	Validation performed by comparing simulated density maps with municipal EV registration data (Zhang et al., 2022).
Assumptions:
	Spatial independence.
	Stationarity (unless inhomogeneous PPP).
Limitation: Cannot capture clustering effects.
________________________________________
B. Cox / Cluster Processes
Framework:
Intensity λ(x) is itself a random field, allowing clustering due to socio-economic or infrastructural factors.
Evidence:
	Rooftop PV deployment modeled via Cox process incorporating income and irradiance as spatial covariates (Wang et al., 2023).
	Model validation used Ripley’s K-function and likelihood-based goodness-of-fit tests against real GIS PV installation data (Wang et al., 2023).
Assumptions:
	Conditional Poisson given latent intensity field.
	Spatial correlation structure defined by covariance kernel.
Limitation: Requires detailed socio-economic covariate data.
________________________________________
C. Spatial Econometric Models
Framework:
Spatial autoregressive (SAR) and spatial error models explain DER density using socio-economic and grid variables while accounting for spatial dependence.
Evidence:
	Distributed PV adoption modeled using spatial lag regression; significant positive spatial spillover effects observed (Li et al., 2021).
	Validation conducted via out-of-sample prediction accuracy and comparison with historical deployment records (Li et al., 2021).
Assumptions:
	Linear dependence structure.
	Spatial weight matrix predefined.
Limitation: Correlational, not generative.
________________________________________
D. Hybrid stochastic-grid models
Some DER hosting studies combine PPP-based siting with power-flow simulation to evaluate grid impact under uncertain spatial distributions (Zhang et al., 2022).
Validation approach:
	Monte Carlo simulations compared with historical feeder-level penetration patterns.
Limitation: Often limited to one region or dataset.
________________________________________
3) Summary table
Model Type	Study	Application	Validation Method	Key Finding	Limitation
PPP	Zhang et al., 2022	EV charging deployment	Density comparison with GIS data	Captures random adoption patterns	No clustering
Cox Process	Wang et al., 2023	Rooftop PV siting	Ripley’s K, likelihood tests	Models socio-economic clustering	Data intensive
Spatial Econometrics	Li et al., 2021	PV adoption	Out-of-sample prediction	Detects spatial spillover	Non-generative
Hybrid PPP + Power Flow	Zhang et al., 2022	Hosting capacity	Monte Carlo vs feeder data	Quantifies penetration uncertainty	Region-specific
________________________________________
4) Gaps and future research
	Integration of cluster processes with harmonic/hosting simulations.
	Validation across multi-country DER datasets.
	Dynamic spatial models incorporating temporal adoption trends.
	Joint modeling of spatial and impedance uncertainty.
	Standardized benchmarking datasets for DER spatial validation.
________________________________________

IEEE Transactions on Smart Grid, 13(4). https://consensus.app/papers/spatial-distribution-modeling-of-electric-vehicle-zhang-liu/5c4dfb4eac965ff6b02dfb3d9f6c61bb/?utm_source=chatgpt

1.5 Contributions, novelty, and scope (what is/is not claimed)
Q1: What prior analytical or semi-analytical approaches exist for supraharmonic aggregation, and what gaps remain in explicitly handling spatial uncertainty?

Since 2021, prior analytical and semi-analytical approaches to supraharmonic (2–150 kHz) aggregation have primarily relied on frequency-domain Norton equivalents with Monte Carlo phase randomisation, deterministic phasor summation bounds, diversity-factor estimation from field measurements, and impedance-based propagation models. These frameworks quantify aggregation under assumptions of known network impedance and statistically independent source phases (Mariscotti et al., 2022); (Slangen et al., 2023). Feeder-level impedance studies further demonstrate frequency-selective amplification effects but remain topology-specific (Letha et al., 2021). A key gap is the lack of explicit probabilistic spatial modeling of emitter locations and network-topology uncertainty, meaning current aggregation models treat location deterministically or implicitly rather than as a random variable.

2) Evidence synthesis
A. Frequency-domain Norton equivalent + Monte Carlo aggregation
Approach:
Converters modeled as Norton current sources with frequency-dependent impedance; phase angles treated as random variables and aggregated via Monte Carlo simulation.
Evidence:
	Analytical aggregation in DC grids uses stochastic phase randomization to estimate expected voltage levels under multiple converters (Mariscotti et al., 2022).
	Results show sub-linear scaling consistent with root-sum-square trends under independence assumptions (Mariscotti et al., 2022).
Gap:
Emitter location along feeders is fixed in simulations; no probabilistic spatial distribution of nodes.

B. Empirical diversity-factor / semi-analytical scaling
Approach:
Field measurements used to derive diversity factors or empirical aggregation coefficients.
Evidence:
	EV fast-charging stations show sub-linear summation, and diversity factors are derived from measured multi-unit operation (Slangen et al., 2023).
Gap:
Aggregation coefficients are site-specific and not generalized via spatial stochastic models.

C. Deterministic impedance-based propagation models
Approach:
Frequency-domain load-flow or transfer-impedance analysis to compute voltage distortion from known current injections.
Evidence:
	MV feeder studies compute driving-point and transfer impedance to evaluate amplification under supraharmonic injections (Letha et al., 2021).
Gap:
Topology and source placement are deterministic; no uncertainty quantification of node occupancy or penetration patterns.

D. Time-varying spectral aggregation models
Approach:
Dynamic phasor and high-resolution parametric methods model non-stationary emission components before aggregation.
Evidence:
	Time-varying supraharmonic components are estimated using dynamic phasor and matrix-pencil approaches, enabling improved spectral decomposition prior to aggregation (Frigo & Braun, 2022); (Li et al., 2023).
Gap:
Temporal variability is handled, but spatial randomness across feeder nodes is not incorporated.

3) Summary table
Approach	Core Method	Spatial Treatment	Key Supporting Study	Limitation
Norton + Monte Carlo	Random phase aggregation	Fixed node placement	Mariscotti et al., 2022	No probabilistic siting
Empirical diversity factors	Field-based scaling	Site-specific	Slangen et al., 2023	Limited transferability
Impedance-based propagation	Transfer impedance analysis	Deterministic topology	Letha et al., 2021	No topology uncertainty
Time-varying spectral models	Dynamic phasor / matrix pencil	No spatial modeling	Frigo & Braun, 2022; Li et al., 2023	Only temporal variability

4) Gaps and future research
	Development of stochastic spatial aggregation models combining PPP/Cox siting with harmonic propagation.
	Closed-form analytical aggregation bounds incorporating random node occupancy.
	Coupling of impedance uncertainty and spatial randomness in hosting studies.
	Validation against real feeder-wide deployment datasets.
	Hybrid analytical–probabilistic frameworks for regulator-level compatibility assessment.

Q2: What point-process/shot-noise models have been applied in power systems (interference, DG hosting, EV spatial modelling), and what insights do they provide that are missing from deterministic studies?
Since 2021, point-process and shot-noise–type models in power systems have been applied to EV charging spatial deployment, distributed generation (DG) hosting assessment, and interference aggregation problems, typically using Poisson point processes (PPP), inhomogeneous PPP, and Cox (doubly stochastic) processes. These models provide analytical expressions for spatial density, interference-like aggregation, and hosting probability that are not available in deterministic feeder simulations (Zhang et al., 2022); (Wang et al., 2023). By treating device locations as random fields, these frameworks yield probabilistic bounds, outage probabilities, and expected hosting capacity under uncertain penetration—insights that deterministic studies cannot generalize beyond a specific topology (Li et al., 2021).
________________________________________
2) Evidence synthesis
A. Poisson Point Process (PPP) for EV spatial modeling
Application:
EV charging infrastructure and EV adoption modeled as spatially random points.
Evidence:
	EV charger deployment modeled using homogeneous and inhomogeneous PPP to derive expected charger density and grid load distribution (Zhang et al., 2022).
	Analytical results provide closed-form intensity functions and penetration–impact relationships.
Insight beyond deterministic models:
Allows derivation of expected load variance and probabilistic overload risk rather than single-scenario outcomes.
________________________________________
B. Cox (doubly stochastic) processes for DG clustering
Application:
PV and distributed generation modeled with spatially varying intensity influenced by socio-economic covariates.
Evidence:
	Rooftop PV installations modeled using Cox process with income and irradiance as random intensity drivers (Wang et al., 2023).
	Validation performed via spatial clustering metrics and likelihood-based tests against real GIS deployment data.
Insight:
Captures spatial clustering and hotspot formation that deterministic uniform-placement models miss.
________________________________________
C. Shot-noise–type interference analogies
Application:
Aggregate load or voltage impact treated analogously to interference fields in stochastic geometry.
Evidence:
	PPP-based EV deployment integrated with grid-impact modeling produces probabilistic hosting estimates under spatial randomness (Zhang et al., 2022).
Insight:
Aggregate impact can be treated as a spatially weighted sum of random contributions (shot-noise process), enabling variance and tail-probability estimation—features absent in deterministic feeder simulations.
________________________________________
D. Spatial econometrics as semi-stochastic alternative
Application:
Spatial autoregressive (SAR) models quantify spillover effects in PV adoption.
Evidence:
	Significant positive spatial lag effects show that DER adoption correlates with neighboring adoption patterns (Li et al., 2021).
Insight:
Reveals endogenous clustering mechanisms not captured in purely deterministic technical simulations.
________________________________________
3) Summary table
Model Type	Study	Application	Key Insight	Deterministic Gap Addressed
PPP	Zhang et al., 2022	EV charger siting	Analytical load density variance	No probabilistic hosting in deterministic feeders
Cox process	Wang et al., 2023	Rooftop PV deployment	Captures clustering/hotspots	Deterministic uniform assumptions
Shot-noise analogy	Zhang et al., 2022	EV grid impact	Tail probability of overload	Single-case feeder analysis
Spatial econometrics	Li et al., 2021	PV adoption	Spatial spillover quantification	Ignores socio-economic drivers
________________________________________
4) Gaps and future research
	Integration of stochastic geometry with harmonic/supraharmonic propagation models.
	Validation of shot-noise–based hosting predictions using feeder-wide measurement datasets.
	Coupling spatial stochastic models with impedance uncertainty.
	Multi-scale modeling linking socio-economic clustering to electrical resonance risk.
	Development of closed-form reliability bounds for high-frequency distortion hosting.
________________________________________

Q3: What common reviewer critiques appear in similar analytical PQ papers (over-claiming, weak validation, missing uncertainty quantification), and how do authors typically address them?

Across analytical power-quality (PQ) studies since 2021, recurring reviewer critiques—reflected in published limitations and validation sections—include (i) over-claiming generalisability from feeder-specific models, (ii) weak or single-site validation, (iii) insufficient uncertainty quantification (measurement and modeling), and (iv) limited treatment of variability (operating state, phase, topology). Authors typically address these critiques by adding Monte Carlo analysis, expanded uncertainty budgets (k=2), cross-validation against measurements, multi-feeder case studies, or sensitivity analysis (Mariscotti et al., 2022); (Rom et al., 2025); (Letha et al., 2021). Field-based aggregation studies explicitly compare analytical predictions with measured diversity factors to strengthen validation (Slangen et al., 2023).
________________________________________
2) Evidence synthesis
A. Critique 1: Over-claiming generalisability from feeder-specific studies
Observed issue:
Analytical or harmonic-domain results are often based on a single feeder or lab setup, yet conclusions may imply broader applicability.
Evidence from published limitations:
	MV resonance study emphasizes that impedance and amplification differ significantly across feeders, limiting extrapolation (Letha et al., 2021).
	EVSE impedance study notes that results depend on specific grid and filter configurations (Grasel et al., 2022).
Typical author response:
	Explicitly limit scope in conclusions.
	Add additional feeder scenarios or parametric sweeps.
________________________________________
B. Critique 2: Weak or limited validation
Observed issue:
Analytical aggregation models validated only via simulation or single-site data.
Evidence:
	Monte Carlo aggregation study acknowledges model-based validation and calls for broader field data (Mariscotti et al., 2022).
	EV fast-charging study strengthens claims by comparing analytical summation with real multi-unit measurements (Slangen et al., 2023).
Typical author response:
	Add field measurements.
	Compare analytical and empirical aggregation factors.
________________________________________
C. Critique 3: Missing uncertainty quantification
Observed issue:
Earlier PQ modeling often omitted measurement or model uncertainty.
Evidence:
	Wideband current transformer calibration study explicitly reports expanded uncertainty (k=2) and frequency-dependent error growth (Rom et al., 2025).
	Statistical characterization of LV supraharmonics uses percentile-based and distribution-based descriptors rather than single deterministic values (de Falco & Varilone, 2021).
Typical author response:
	Provide confidence intervals.
	Include Monte Carlo sensitivity analysis.
	Report measurement uncertainty budgets.
________________________________________
D. Critique 4: Insufficient treatment of variability (phase, operating mode, topology)
Observed issue:
Assuming stationary, coherent emissions or fixed topology.
Evidence:
	Dynamic phasor modeling highlights non-stationary emissions, addressing prior simplifications (Frigo & Braun, 2022).
	UPS feeder study shows operating mode significantly alters propagation patterns (Sutaria & Rönnberg, 2023).
Typical author response:
	Include time-varying modeling.
	Analyze multiple operating states.
	Perform sensitivity analysis over topology variations.
________________________________________

	Standardised reporting checklist for PQ analytical papers (uncertainty, validation scope, topology limits).
	Mandatory cross-feeder validation for aggregation claims.
	Unified stochastic + impedance modeling frameworks.
	Public benchmark datasets for reproducibility.
	Formal statistical tests comparing analytical predictions to field data.

1.6 Paper roadmap
Q1: What IMRaD structures are commonly used in Q1 power-quality / stochastic power systems journals for theory+validation papers?

In Q1 power-quality and stochastic power systems journals (2021–present), theory+validation papers typically follow an extended IMRaD structure: (i) Introduction with explicit contribution claims, (ii) System/model formulation and theoretical development, (iii) Methodology (analytical or stochastic framework), (iv) Case studies or experimental setup, (v) Results (often multi-scenario or Monte Carlo), (vi) Validation against measurements or benchmark models, and (vii) Discussion and limitations. This structure is consistently observed in supraharmonic aggregation, impedance modeling, stochastic siting, and measurement-method papers (Mariscotti et al., 2022); (Letha et al., 2021); (Zhang et al., 2022). Validation sections increasingly include uncertainty quantification, sensitivity analysis, or comparison with field measurements (Rom et al., 2025).
________________________________________
2) Evidence synthesis
A. Introduction: Problem framing + explicit contributions
Common features:
	Clear statement of gap in existing PQ/stochastic literature.
	Bullet-style contribution summary.
	Justification of novelty (e.g., stochastic vs deterministic approach).
Evidence:
	Supraharmonic aggregation paper explicitly defines analytical gap and lists contributions before theory section (Mariscotti et al., 2022).
	EV spatial modeling study highlights stochastic geometry novelty and hosting implications (Zhang et al., 2022).
IMRaD role: Introduction + research gap articulation.
________________________________________
B. Theory / System Modeling Section
Common features:
	Formal mathematical formulation (e.g., Norton equivalents, PPP intensity, impedance matrices).
	Assumption statements (linearity, independence, stationarity).
	Derivation of analytical expressions.
Evidence:
	Monte Carlo aggregation formulated mathematically via Norton equivalents (Mariscotti et al., 2022).
	Cox-process PV deployment model defines spatial intensity function and likelihood formulation (Wang et al., 2023).
IMRaD role: Methods (theoretical framework).
________________________________________
C. Case Studies / Simulation Design
Common features:
	Multi-feeder or multi-scenario analysis.
	Parameter sweeps or penetration scenarios.
	Clearly specified grid data and assumptions.
Evidence:
	Eight-feeder MV resonance study includes comparative case scenarios (Letha et al., 2021).
	EV aggregation study analyzes different charger combinations and simultaneity patterns (Slangen et al., 2023).
IMRaD role: Methods + Results.
________________________________________
D. Validation and Uncertainty Section
Common features:
	Comparison with field measurement.
	Expanded uncertainty (k=2).
	Sensitivity or Monte Carlo analysis.
Evidence:
	Wideband CT calibration includes explicit uncertainty budget and confidence intervals (Rom et al., 2025).
	EV aggregation validated against measured diversity factors (Slangen et al., 2023).
IMRaD role: Results + Validation (often separate subsection).
________________________________________
E. Discussion, Limitations, and Practical Implications
Common features:
	Explicit discussion of topology dependence or data limitations.
	Hosting-capacity or regulatory implications.
	Future research section.
Evidence:
	MV feeder study emphasizes topology-specific findings (Letha et al., 2021).
	Stochastic DER siting papers discuss deployment-data validation limits (Zhang et al., 2022).
IMRaD role: Discussion + Conclusion.
________________________________________

Results	Multi-scenario results with figures	Letha et al., 2021
Validation	Measurement comparison / uncertainty	Rom et al., 2025
Discussion & Limits	Transferability & limitations	Slangen et al., 2023

4) Gaps and future research
	More standardized uncertainty-reporting subsections.
	Formal separation of “Model Validation” vs “Results.”
	Broader multi-region validation in stochastic PQ papers.
	Reproducibility checklists for analytical aggregation studies.
	Open datasets to strengthen validation sections.

Q2: What best-practice conventions exist for presenting mathematical assumptions, theorem statements, and validation pipelines in applied stochastic-geometry power papers?

pplied stochastic-geometry power papers (2021–present) follow consistent best-practice conventions: explicitly stated modeling assumptions (e.g., PPP/Cox independence, stationarity, linear network response), formally numbered propositions/theorems with proof sketches or appendices, and structured validation pipelines combining Monte Carlo simulation with real deployment or feeder data. EV spatial and DER siting studies clearly separate model assumptions, analytical derivation, and empirical validation stages (Zhang et al., 2022); (Wang et al., 2023). Hosting-capacity and aggregation studies further include sensitivity analysis and uncertainty quantification before drawing system-level conclusions (Mariscotti et al., 2022); (Letha et al., 2021).
________________________________________
2) Evidence synthesis
A. Explicit mathematical assumption blocks
Best practice:
	Separate subsection titled System Model or Assumptions.
	Clear statements of independence, stationarity, linearity, or impedance invariance.
	Definition of spatial intensity λ(x) or impedance matrix Z(f).
Evidence:
	EV stochastic-geometry modeling defines homogeneous/inhomogeneous PPP assumptions before derivation (Zhang et al., 2022).
	Cox-process PV deployment paper explicitly defines latent intensity field and conditional Poisson property (Wang et al., 2023).
Convention: Assumptions precede theorem statements and are restated when invoking analytical results.
________________________________________
B. Formal theorem/proposition presentation
Best practice:
	Numbered propositions (Proposition 1, Theorem 1).
	Closed-form expressions followed by proof or reference to appendix.
	Conditions for validity clearly enumerated.
Evidence:
	PPP-based EV deployment work derives analytical expressions for density/impact under specified assumptions (Zhang et al., 2022).
	Cox-process PV modeling includes likelihood formulation and derivation under covariance assumptions (Wang et al., 2023).
Convention: Theoretical statements are followed by numerical verification.
________________________________________
C. Structured validation pipeline
Common validation sequence:
	Analytical derivation
	Monte Carlo simulation under same assumptions
	Sensitivity analysis (penetration, topology, parameter sweep)
	Comparison with field or deployment data
Evidence:
	Supraharmonic aggregation study uses analytical Monte Carlo and simulation comparison (Mariscotti et al., 2022).
	MV feeder resonance study validates impedance predictions with measurement and scenario comparison (Letha et al., 2021).
	Cox-process PV deployment validated using spatial clustering metrics and real GIS data (Wang et al., 2023).
Convention: Analytical and empirical validation are clearly separated.
________________________________________
D. Uncertainty and sensitivity reporting
Best practice:
	Parameter sweeps over intensity λ, penetration, or impedance.
	Reporting of variance or confidence intervals.
	Discussion of assumption robustness.
Evidence:
	Aggregation study evaluates impact of phase randomness via Monte Carlo distributions (Mariscotti et al., 2022).
	MV feeder study examines topology-dependent resonance sensitivity (Letha et al., 2021).
Convention: Validation includes parameter robustness analysis before system-level claims.
________________________________________

Numbered theorems/propositions	Formal statement + proof/appendix	Wang et al., 2023	Rigor
Analytical + Monte Carlo validation	Closed-form vs simulation comparison	Mariscotti et al., 2022	Consistency check
Multi-scenario feeder testing	Topology or penetration sweeps	Letha et al., 2021	Robustness
Empirical cross-validation	GIS or measurement comparison	Wang et al., 2023	External validity

4) Gaps and future research
	Standardized reporting templates for stochastic-geometry power papers.
	Formal reproducibility checklists (code/data availability).
	Stronger statistical goodness-of-fit reporting (KS tests, likelihood ratios).
	Unified validation pipelines combining spatial and impedance uncertainty.
	Benchmark stochastic-grid datasets for cross-paper comparison.

Q3: What reproducibility elements are expected (parameter tables, code availability, benchmark description) in recent high-impact analytical power-quality publications?
Recent high-impact analytical power-quality (PQ) publications (2021–present) consistently include (i) detailed parameter tables (network, device, stochastic variables), (ii) explicit modeling assumptions, (iii) structured case-study or benchmark descriptions, (iv) uncertainty or sensitivity analysis, and increasingly (v) statements on data/code availability or measurement traceability. Supraharmonic aggregation and impedance studies provide full frequency-dependent parameter sets and scenario definitions to enable replication (Mariscotti et al., 2022); (Letha et al., 2021). Measurement-focused PQ papers explicitly report calibration chains and expanded uncertainty budgets to ensure reproducibility (Rom et al., 2025). Stochastic-geometry DER studies additionally document spatial datasets and validation metrics for replicability (Wang et al., 2023).
________________________________________
2) Evidence synthesis
A. Detailed parameter and system-data tables
Expectation:
	Full listing of grid parameters (impedance, cable lengths, transformer ratings).
	Converter or stochastic-model parameters (intensity λ, covariance kernels, phase distributions).
	Frequency resolution and modeling range.
Evidence:
	MV supraharmonic propagation study specifies feeder configurations and frequency-dependent impedance modeling parameters (Letha et al., 2021).
	Supraharmonic aggregation modeling provides Norton-equivalent parameters and Monte Carlo settings (Mariscotti et al., 2022).
Reproducibility function: Enables independent recreation of analytical and simulation results.
________________________________________
B. Clear benchmark and case-study descriptions
Expectation:
	Explicit feeder topology diagrams or descriptions.
	Definition of operating states and penetration scenarios.
	Defined simulation time windows or sampling frequency.
Evidence:
	EV fast-charging aggregation study clearly documents number of chargers, configuration, and measurement setup (Slangen et al., 2023).
	Cox-process PV deployment study specifies spatial dataset and validation metrics (e.g., clustering tests) (Wang et al., 2023).
Reproducibility function: Allows benchmarking across studies.
________________________________________
C. Uncertainty quantification and sensitivity analysis
Expectation:
	Reporting expanded uncertainty (k=2).
	Monte Carlo confidence intervals.
	Parameter sweep robustness checks.
Evidence:
	Wideband CT calibration paper provides traceable calibration chain and frequency-dependent uncertainty budget (Rom et al., 2025).
	Aggregation study uses Monte Carlo phase randomization and distribution analysis (Mariscotti et al., 2022).
Reproducibility function: Makes conclusions robust under parameter variation.
________________________________________
D. Data and code transparency (emerging norm)
Expectation:
	Statements regarding dataset origin (e.g., GIS PV deployment records).
	Disclosure of simulation tools or analytical workflow.
	Increasing inclusion of supplementary material.
Evidence:
	Spatial PV modeling validated against real GIS installation data and clustering statistics (Wang et al., 2023).
	MV feeder resonance work describes measurement–model comparison methodology (Letha et al., 2021).
Reproducibility function: Enables independent verification beyond simulation-only claims.
