Section 2 — Analytical Framework and Methods
2.1 Problem definition and target quantity at the PCC
Q1: What PCC-based supraharmonic impact metrics are used in the literature (complex voltage phasor per frequency, band-limited RMS, spectral density, percentile exceedance), and why?

Recent PCC-based supraharmonic studies (2021–present) use four main impact metrics: (i) complex voltage/current phasor per discrete frequency, (ii) band-limited RMS over defined sub-bands (e.g., 2–9 kHz, 9–150 kHz), (iii) spectral magnitude or density representations, and (iv) percentile/exceedance-based indicators. Phasor-per-frequency metrics support aggregation and impedance modeling (Mariscotti et al., 2022), while band-limited RMS metrics enable regulatory-style comparison and compatibility assessment (Slangen et al., 2023). Spectral density representations are used for propagation and resonance studies (Letha et al., 2021), and percentile metrics capture stochastic variability and rare high-amplitude events at the PCC (de Falco & Varilone, 2021).
________________________________________
2) Evidence synthesis
A. Complex voltage phasor per frequency
Metric:
	V(f)=Z(f)\cdot I(f)at each discrete frequency bin.
	Both magnitude and phase retained.
Why used:
	Enables analytical aggregation via phasor summation.
	Captures resonance selectivity and phase interactions.
Evidence:
	Supraharmonic Norton-based aggregation models compute complex PCC voltage per frequency to evaluate amplification (Mariscotti et al., 2022).
	MV propagation study evaluates frequency-dependent PCC voltage using complex impedance modeling (Letha et al., 2021).
Strength: Necessary for resonance-aware planning.
________________________________________
B. Band-limited RMS (sub-band aggregation)
Metric:
	RMS voltage/current over frequency bands (e.g., 2–9 kHz, 9–150 kHz).
	Often computed via summation of squared magnitudes in band.
Why used:
	Aligns with emerging compatibility and measurement practices.
	Reduces sensitivity to fine spectral structure.
Evidence:
	EV fast-charger summation study evaluates supraharmonic current in 2–150 kHz bands for practical impact assessment (Slangen et al., 2023).
Strength: More stable metric for compliance discussions.
________________________________________
C. Spectral magnitude / density representation
Metric:
	Magnitude spectrum (|V(f)|).
	Sometimes power spectral density (PSD)-like representation.
Why used:
	Identifies resonance peaks and propagation paths.
	Supports comparison across feeders.
Evidence:
	MV feeder resonance study uses spectral magnitude plots to identify amplification frequencies (Letha et al., 2021).
Strength: Direct visualization of resonance-driven risk.
________________________________________
D. Percentile and exceedance metrics at PCC
Metric:
	95th or 99th percentile of |V(f)| or band-RMS.
	Exceedance probability above threshold.
Why used:
	Captures stochastic variability due to phase and deployment diversity.
	Aligns with probabilistic planning.
Evidence:
	LV statistical study reports percentile distributions for supraharmonic voltage at PCC (de Falco & Varilone, 2021).
	Aggregation modeling evaluates distribution envelopes under random phase conditions (Mariscotti et al., 2022).
Strength: Tail-aware and planning-oriented.
________________________________________
3) Summary table
Metric	Mathematical Form	Purpose	Example Study	Advantage
Complex phasor V(f)	Z(f)·I(f)	Aggregation & resonance	Mariscotti et al., 2022	Phase-aware
Band-limited RMS	√Σ	V(f)	² (band)	Compliance proxy
Spectral magnitude		V(f)		Resonance identification
Percentile / exceedance	P{	V	> x}	Risk assessment
________________________________________
4) Gaps and future research
	Standardized band definitions for supraharmonic PCC reporting.
	Harmonization between phasor-based and band-RMS metrics.
	Development of risk-based PCC compatibility limits.
	Integration of spatial stochastic models with PCC percentile metrics.
	Unified reporting templates for PCC supraharmonic impact.

5) Bibliography (APA 7)

Q2: How do studies formally connect injected supraharmonic currents to PCC voltage distortion (transfer impedance, network admittance, Norton equivalents), and what frequency-domain assumptions are made?

Recent empirical studies connect injected supraharmonic (2–150 kHz) currents to PCC voltage distortion by modeling the grid–converter interface through frequency-domain transfer impedance (Z(f)), driving-point impedance, and Norton-equivalent representations of converters. Voltage distortion at the PCC is typically calculated as V(f)=Z_{grid}(f)\cdot I_{inj}(f), where grid impedance is measured or simulated across frequency and combined with converter current spectra. Several studies explicitly derive converter Norton equivalents (current source + frequency-dependent impedance) and embed them in ladder/admittance network models to simulate resonance, aggregation, and distortion propagation. These approaches generally assume linear, time-invariant (LTI) behavior within narrow frequency bins and apply steady-state frequency-domain superposition.
________________________________________
2) Evidence synthesis
A. Transfer Impedance and Driving-Point Impedance Modeling
Key finding: Supraharmonic voltage distortion at the PCC is computed by multiplying injected current spectra with the frequency-dependent transfer or driving-point impedance of the network.
	Medium-voltage network simulations showed that supraharmonic resonance and PCC distortion depend strongly on feeder transfer impedance magnitude versus frequency, with resonance peaks determined by network impedance characteristics (Letha et al., 2021).
	Measurements of bidirectional EV chargers demonstrated that added converter capacitance modifies high-frequency grid impedance, creating new resonance points that shape PCC voltage distortion (Grasel et al., 2022).
	Data-center measurements confirmed that supraharmonic propagation depends on input/output impedance interactions, requiring impedance-based modeling for distortion prediction (Sutaria & Rönnberg, 2023).
	Distribution-system harmonic propagation studies quantified how high-order harmonic distortion transfers from source to substation transformer using frequency-domain impedance analysis (Peerzada et al., 2024).
Frequency-domain assumption: Networks are modeled as linear impedances per frequency, enabling superposition and independent spectral component analysis.
________________________________________
B. Norton-Equivalent Representation of Converters
Key finding: Converters are modeled as Norton current sources with frequency-dependent internal impedance to predict PCC voltage distortion under varying grid conditions.
	Wireless power transfer converters were experimentally characterized to extract emission spectra and input impedance, forming a Norton equivalent used in ladder grid models to compute aggregate distortion (Mariscotti et al., 2022).
	Supraharmonic current summation from fast-charging stations demonstrated that aggregated injected currents interact via network impedance, validating current-source modeling with diversity factors (Slangen et al., 2023).
	Even-order background distortion was shown to interact with converter emissions through frequency-domain mathematical models, confirming current-source plus impedance representations (Aboutaleb et al., 2024).
Frequency-domain assumption: Converter behavior is linearized around operating points and represented as steady-state spectral injections independent across frequency bins.
________________________________________
C. Frequency Coupling and Nonlinear Effects
Key finding: Some studies show harmonic–supraharmonic coupling, indicating limits of strict linear superposition assumptions.
	Experimental inverter studies revealed coupling between harmonic (<2 kHz) and supraharmonic ranges, meaning emissions can shift due to nonlinear interactions (Kaufhold et al., 2021).
	Supply-voltage variation experiments showed that emission magnitude and frequency bands shift with operating voltage, implying quasi-stationary rather than strictly time-invariant assumptions (Frydlewicz et al., 2024).
	BESS-integrated fast charging measurements demonstrated operating-state-dependent spectral variation, requiring stage-wise frequency-domain modeling (Giri & Rönnberg, 2025).
Frequency-domain implication: Most models assume steady-state conditions within each operating mode, but real systems exhibit quasi-linear, operating-point-dependent behavior.

4) Gaps and Future Research
	Need for unified impedance-based PCC distortion prediction validated across diverse LV/MV networks.
	Limited validation of linear Norton models under dynamic converter control conditions.
	Insufficient modeling of frequency coupling and nonlinear cross-band interactions.
	Need probabilistic impedance–emission frameworks for high-penetration converter scenarios.
	More field measurements linking measured transfer impedance directly to observed PCC voltage distortion.

2.2 Network–source abstraction and modelling assumptions
Q1: What conditions justify linear time-invariant (LTI) small-signal superposition for supraharmonics in distribution networks, and what non-linearities are documented as relevant exceptions?

Linear time-invariant (LTI) small-signal superposition for supraharmonics in distribution networks is justified when converter emissions are stationary, network impedances are frequency-dependent but linear, and interactions between frequency components are negligible. Under these conditions, injected supraharmonic currents can be treated independently per frequency bin and propagated through linear network impedances. However, documented exceptions include harmonic–supraharmonic frequency coupling, operating-point-dependent converter behavior, control-loop nonlinearities, resonance shifts due to grid-impedance variation, and aggregation/cancellation effects between multiple nonlinear sources. These phenomena introduce cross-frequency interactions and time variance that violate strict LTI assumptions.
________________________________________
2) Evidence synthesis
A. Conditions Supporting LTI Small-Signal Superposition
1. Frequency-Domain Linearity of Network Impedance
Key finding: When the network can be modeled as a linear impedance versus frequency, supraharmonic propagation follows superposition.
	Medium-voltage network studies showed that resonance peaks and transfer impedance characteristics determine voltage distortion, assuming linear frequency-dependent impedance for each spectral component (Letha et al., 2021).
	Bidirectional EV charger measurements demonstrated that high-frequency grid impedance can be characterized and used to predict emission interaction, consistent with linear impedance modeling (Grasel et al., 2022).
Implication: If the grid behaves as a passive linear network in the 2–150 kHz range, superposition of spectral components is valid.
________________________________________
2. Stationary Converter Emissions
Key finding: Fixed switching-frequency converters under steady-state conditions exhibit emissions that can be treated as independent frequency components.
	Supraharmonic emission modeling of single-phase converters showed that analytical frequency-domain models reproduce spectral peaks under fixed switching operation (Kaufhold et al., 2021).
	DC-link harmonic analytical modeling validated steady-state spectral prediction when voltage ripple and current harmonics were modeled deterministically (Moradi et al., 2022).
Implication: When converter control is periodic and stationary, small-signal linearization around an operating point supports LTI assumptions.
________________________________________
3. Weak Cross-Frequency Coupling
Key finding: When crossed-frequency admittance terms are negligible, single-frequency modeling is sufficient.
	A crossed-frequency admittance matrix (CFAM) study demonstrated that traditional constant-current or diagonal models are valid only when cross-frequency terms are small; otherwise, more complex modeling is required (Yang et al., 2025).
Implication: LTI superposition is justified when harmonic coupling terms are minimal.
________________________________________
B. Documented Non-Linearities and Exceptions
1. Harmonic–Supraharmonic Frequency Coupling
Key finding: Background harmonic distortion can generate new supraharmonic components via nonlinear modulation.
	Experimental inverter studies demonstrated coupling between harmonic (<2 kHz) and supraharmonic components, invalidating strict frequency independence (Kaufhold et al., 2021).
	Even-order background distortion was shown to interact with converter emissions and generate additional supraharmonic components through nonlinear mechanisms (Aboutaleb et al., 2024).
Consequence: Superposition fails when nonlinear mixing produces intermodulation products.
________________________________________
2. Operating-Point and Time-Varying Behavior
Key finding: Converter emissions vary with load level, control mode, and operating stage.
	Field measurements of EV fast-charging stations showed that supraharmonic current summation is non-monotonic and time-varying, indicating quasi-linear rather than strictly LTI behavior (Slangen et al., 2023).
	Dynamic phasor studies demonstrated that supraharmonic emissions can be pulsating or time-varying, requiring time-dependent modeling (Frigo & Braun, 2022).
Consequence: Strict time invariance is violated under dynamic operating conditions.
________________________________________
3. Resonance and Impedance Variation
Key finding: Grid impedance changes can shift resonance frequencies and modify distortion magnitude.
	MV network studies showed that feeder length and configuration significantly alter transfer impedance magnitude and resonance peaks (Letha et al., 2021).
	EV charger connection altered high-frequency impedance and introduced new resonance points (Grasel et al., 2022).
Consequence: Network parameter variation can invalidate fixed LTI assumptions across scenarios.
________________________________________
4. Aggregation and Cancellation Effects
Key finding: Multiple nonlinear sources interact through phase relationships and impedance coupling.
	Supraharmonic summation studies showed diversity and absorption effects between chargers, meaning emissions do not add linearly in magnitude (Slangen et al., 2023).
Consequence: Linear superposition of magnitudes is invalid when phase relationships and nonlinear control interactions exist.
________________________________________
3) Summary Table
Study	Design	LTI Valid When	Documented Nonlinearity	Limitation
Letha et al., 2021	MV simulation	Linear impedance per frequency	Resonance shifts	Case network
Grasel et al., 2022	Lab measurement	Passive grid impedance assumption	Capacitance-induced resonance	Single EVSE
Kaufhold et al., 2021	Experimental	Stationary switching	Harmonic–supraharmonic coupling	Single inverter
Aboutaleb et al., 2024	Analytical + exp	Weak background distortion	Even-order distortion mixing	Limited converter types
Slangen et al., 2023	Field measurement	Independent sources assumption	Aggregation diversity effects	4 chargers
Frigo & Braun, 2022	Exp + simulation	Stationary emissions	Pulsating/time-varying components	Lab-scale
________________________________________
4) Gaps and Future Research
	Need quantitative thresholds defining when cross-frequency admittance terms invalidate diagonal LTI models.
	Limited probabilistic frameworks for time-varying supraharmonic superposition.
	Lack of standardized criteria to distinguish quasi-LTI from strongly nonlinear operating regimes.
	Insufficient experimental validation under high-penetration converter scenarios.
	Need unified models incorporating control-loop nonlinearities and impedance variation.

Q2: How do authors treat quasi-stationarity (time windows, operating-state segmentation) for converter emissions in the 2–150 kHz band?

Authors treat quasi-stationarity of converter emissions in the 2–150 kHz band by segmenting data into time windows aligned with operating states (e.g., charging stage, load level, control mode) and assuming local stationarity within each window. Short-time spectral analysis, stage-based classification, and dynamic phasor estimation are commonly used to capture time-varying behavior while preserving frequency resolution. Field studies often separate measurements by charger activity, power level, or UPS mode before calculating spectra or RMS quantities. Thus, quasi-stationarity is handled through operating-state segmentation combined with windowed spectral estimation rather than assuming global time invariance.
________________________________________
2) Evidence synthesis
A. Operating-State Segmentation
Key finding: Authors explicitly divide measurements into discrete operating stages and analyze each as locally stationary.
	A BESS-integrated fast-charging study classified operation into five distinct stages and evaluated harmonic/supraharmonic spectra separately for each stage, demonstrating that emissions vary significantly with operating mode (Giri & Rönnberg, 2025). https://consensus.app/papers/evaluation-of-waveform-distortion-in-bessintegrated-giri-rönnberg/555dcfd819c556e0b2297af99895f10e/?utm_source=chatgpt
	Supraharmonic summation from EV fast-charging stations showed time-varying emission levels depending on the number of active chargers, and analysis was conducted over intervals corresponding to charger activity states (Slangen et al., 2023). https://consensus.app/papers/summation-of-supraharmonic-currents-2–150-khz-from-ev-fast-slangen-ćuk/2ba7397d15f25611b5f94a624d0dcae5/?utm_source=chatgpt
	Data-center UPS studies compared supraharmonic propagation under different operating modes (online vs bypass), treating each mode as a quasi-stationary regime (Sutaria & Rönnberg, 2023). https://consensus.app/papers/propagation-of-supraharmonics-in-a-data-center-with-sutaria-rönnberg/4d8a12ec8d4a577484fd6a9866313198/?utm_source=chatgpt
Interpretation: Emissions are assumed stationary only within clearly defined converter states.
________________________________________
B. Windowed Spectral Estimation and Resolution Bandwidth
Key finding: Authors apply finite time windows and defined resolution bandwidths to approximate stationarity in spectral analysis.
	DC-grid-connected WPT converter studies used defined resolution bandwidths (e.g., 200 Hz and 500 Hz) and Monte Carlo simulations to assess emission aggregation under random phase conditions, implicitly assuming stationarity within the analysis window (Mariscotti et al., 2022). https://consensus.app/papers/supraharmonic-emissions-from-dc-grid-connected-wireless-mariscotti-sandrolini/a14f12a6ea5f5792a71e29ba842a3053/?utm_source=chatgpt
	Experimental inverter studies analyzed harmonic–supraharmonic coupling using steady-state spectral measurements under fixed switching conditions, relying on time windows sufficient to ensure stable spectra (Kaufhold et al., 2021). https://consensus.app/papers/impact-of-harmonic-distortion-on-the-supraharmonic-kaufhold-meyer/52637ab6cb135bffa848019ba660d7ee/?utm_source=chatgpt
Interpretation: Quasi-stationarity is enforced by selecting time intervals where switching frequency and control states are constant.
________________________________________
C. Dynamic Phasor and Time-Varying Modeling
Key finding: When emissions are pulsating or non-stationary, authors use dynamic phasor or adaptive models instead of fixed-window FFT alone.
	A dynamic phasor method estimated time-varying supraharmonic components using Taylor–Fourier multifrequency models, validated under pulsating emission scenarios and non-stationary conditions (Frigo & Braun, 2022). https://consensus.app/papers/supraharmonic-dynamic-phasors-estimation-of-timevarying-frigo-braun/2996a9450e5857d8a6e8f5839c176bdc/?utm_source=chatgpt
	Crossed-frequency admittance modeling showed that when converter behavior changes with operating parameters, piecewise linearization around specific operating points is necessary, effectively treating each point as quasi-stationary (Yang et al., 2025). https://consensus.app/papers/a-piecewise-linearization-based-method-for-crossed-yang-wang/0c2417603e9d536f85c37441f403fcc3/?utm_source=chatgpt
Interpretation: For time-varying emissions, quasi-stationarity is approximated through local linearization or adaptive spectral tracking.
________________________________________
D. Aggregation and Diversity Effects
Key finding: In multi-source scenarios, authors segment data based on active-source combinations to maintain quasi-stationary conditions.
	EV fast-charging aggregation studies analyzed supraharmonic current summation during intervals with different numbers of active chargers, showing that emission levels fluctuate and require state-based segmentation (Slangen et al., 2023). https://consensus.app/papers/summation-of-supraharmonic-currents-2–150-khz-from-ev-fast-slangen-ćuk/2ba7397d15f25611b5f94a624d0dcae5/?utm_source=chatgpt
Interpretation: Superposition analyses assume stationarity only within fixed source configurations.

4) Gaps and Future Research
	Lack of standardized time-window length recommendations for 2–150 kHz quasi-stationary analysis.
	Limited quantitative criteria defining when emissions transition from stationary to non-stationary.
	Need unified frameworks linking operating-state detection with automated segmentation.
	Insufficient field validation under high converter penetration and rapid control changes.
	Need probabilistic models combining time segmentation with uncertainty quantification.

Q3: What independence/correlation structures are reported (between devices, between magnitude and phase marks, between emissions and impedance), and what evidence supports/contradicts i.i.d. assumptions?

Recent empirical studies show that supraharmonic emissions in distribution networks generally violate strict i.i.d. (independent and identically distributed) assumptions. Correlations are reported (i) between devices through impedance coupling and aggregation/diversity effects, (ii) between magnitude and phase via partial cancellation and absorption phenomena, and (iii) between emissions and network impedance due to resonance and operating-point dependence. Field and laboratory evidence demonstrates non-monotonic summation, cross-frequency coupling, and state-dependent emission changes, all contradicting simple independence assumptions. While some modeling frameworks assume independence for tractability, measurements consistently show structured dependence driven by control synchronization, shared supply impedance, and harmonic coupling.
________________________________________
2) Evidence synthesis
A. Between-Device Independence vs. Aggregation and Diversity Effects
Key finding: Supraharmonic currents from multiple devices are not independent; aggregation depends on phase relationships, impedance coupling, and operating coincidence.
	Field measurements of four 350 kW fast chargers showed that total supraharmonic current does not increase monotonically with the number of active chargers, and a diversity factor was required to describe aggregation, directly contradicting i.i.d. magnitude summation (Slangen et al., 2023). https://consensus.app/papers/summation-of-supraharmonic-currents-2–150-khz-from-ev-fast-slangen-ćuk/2ba7397d15f25611b5f94a624d0dcae5/?utm_source=chatgpt
	Harmonic aggregation experiments demonstrated cancellation phenomena when multiple nonlinear loads are connected, showing that measurement-based aggregation differs from simple mathematical summation, indicating correlated phase behavior (Daniel et al., 2022). https://consensus.app/papers/current-harmonic-aggregation-cases-for-contemporary-daniel-kütt/c4b018fefb82534599b71712c2cfb41c/?utm_source=chatgpt
Implication: Device emissions are neither independent nor identically distributed; phase alignment and shared impedance introduce dependence structures.
________________________________________
B. Magnitude–Phase Correlation and Cross-Frequency Coupling
Key finding: Magnitude and phase of emissions are coupled, and emissions at different frequencies can be correlated due to nonlinear modulation.
	Experimental inverter studies showed coupling between harmonic (<2 kHz) and supraharmonic emissions, meaning emissions at different frequencies are not statistically independent (Kaufhold et al., 2021). https://consensus.app/papers/impact-of-harmonic-distortion-on-the-supraharmonic-kaufhold-meyer/52637ab6cb135bffa848019ba660d7ee/?utm_source=chatgpt
	A crossed-frequency admittance matrix (CFAM) analytical model demonstrated that harmonic sources exhibit cross-frequency terms, meaning emission at one frequency depends on voltage distortion at others—explicitly rejecting diagonal (independent-frequency) assumptions (Yang et al., 2025). https://consensus.app/papers/a-piecewise-linearization-based-method-for-crossed-yang-wang/0c2417603e9d536f85c37441f403fcc3/?utm_source=chatgpt
	Neural-network harmonic estimation studies found that correlation between harmonic orders should not be ignored, contradicting the widely accepted independence assumption in harmonic analysis (Eslami et al., 2022). https://consensus.app/papers/harmonic-current-estimation-of-unmonitored-harmonic-eslami-negnevitsky/21f1d5ff69ea52b1a381c2b08b1c0652/?utm_source=chatgpt
Implication: Both cross-frequency and magnitude–phase dependencies are empirically observed.
________________________________________
C. Emission–Impedance Correlation (Network Coupling)
Key finding: Emissions are correlated with network impedance characteristics; resonance modifies both magnitude and phase.
	Medium-voltage network studies showed that transfer impedance versus frequency determines amplification or attenuation of supraharmonics, linking emission levels to impedance structure (Letha et al., 2021). https://consensus.app/papers/evaluation-of-medium-voltage-network-for-propagation-of-letha-delgado/76c9d68e140c53ff8df25b6a1f2113b2/?utm_source=chatgpt
	Measurements of bidirectional EV chargers showed that connecting the device changes high-frequency grid impedance and creates new resonance points, implying mutual dependence between emission and impedance (Grasel et al., 2022). https://consensus.app/papers/supraharmonic-and-harmonic-emissions-of-a-bidirectional-grasel-baptista/9524a0b0f9bf5e5b8151331e87354a8c/?utm_source=chatgpt
	Data-center UPS measurements showed that supraharmonic magnitude depends on operating mode and input/output impedance, reinforcing that emissions are state- and impedance-dependent (Sutaria & Rönnberg, 2023). https://consensus.app/papers/propagation-of-supraharmonics-in-a-data-center-with-sutaria-rönnberg/4d8a12ec8d4a577484fd6a9866313198/?utm_source=chatgpt
Implication: Emission statistics are conditional on network impedance, contradicting identically distributed assumptions across locations or time.
________________________________________
D. Temporal Correlation and Quasi-Stationarity
Key finding: Emissions exhibit temporal dependence driven by operating states.
	Supraharmonic emissions in BESS-integrated fast charging were segmented into operating stages, with different spectral characteristics per stage, indicating non-identical distributions over time (Giri & Rönnberg, 2025). https://consensus.app/papers/evaluation-of-waveform-distortion-in-bessintegrated-giri-rönnberg/555dcfd819c556e0b2297af99895f10e/?utm_source=chatgpt
	Dynamic phasor estimation studies showed pulsating emissions and time-varying amplitudes, implying serial dependence rather than temporal independence (Frigo & Braun, 2022). https://consensus.app/papers/supraharmonic-dynamic-phasors-estimation-of-timevarying-frigo-braun/2996a9450e5857d8a6e8f5839c176bdc/?utm_source=chatgpt
Implication: Emissions are not identically distributed across time; operating-state segmentation is required.
________________________________________

4) Gaps and Future Research
	Lack of quantitative statistical models describing joint magnitude–phase distributions.
	Limited probabilistic frameworks incorporating cross-frequency admittance terms.
	Need for multivariate stochastic models capturing emission–impedance co-dependence.
	Insufficient large-sample field datasets for validating dependence structures.
	Few studies formally test i.i.d. hypotheses using statistical metrics.

2.3 Spatial deployment model and spatial uncertainty regimes
Q1: What spatial point process models have been used for EV charging, DER placement, or customer/device locations in distribution systems (homogeneous PPP, inhomogeneous PPP, Cox, cluster, hard-core)?

Since 2021, spatial point process models in EV charging and related infrastructure studies have primarily relied on homogeneous Poisson point processes (PPP) within stochastic-geometry frameworks, especially when analytical tractability is required. These PPP models are typically used to represent charging-station locations or mobile charging demand in large-scale spatial analyses. However, in the power distribution literature specifically (as opposed to wireless-network analogs), most planning studies use optimization or traffic-based spatial models rather than explicit Cox, cluster, or hard-core point processes. There is little direct evidence of Cox, Matérn cluster, or hard-core processes being applied in distribution-system EV or DER placement models during 2021–present. Thus, homogeneous PPP dominates stochastic-geometry-based analytical work, while detailed distribution planning studies rarely adopt formal spatial point process models.
________________________________________
2) Evidence synthesis
A. Homogeneous Poisson Point Process (PPP)
Key finding: Homogeneous PPP is the dominant spatial model when stochastic geometry is used for charging infrastructure analysis.
	A stochastic-geometry framework modeled charging station locations using PPP to analyze spatial density effects and service performance in charging-infrastructure sharing scenarios (Qin et al., 2022). The study explicitly used PPP to represent spatial deployment and derive performance metrics analytically.
https://consensus.app/papers/performance-analysis-of-charging-infrastructure-sharing-qin-kishk/74266e2a22a152f1b4920d78e24e4bb6/?utm_source=chatgpt
	In drone-enabled charging infrastructure modeling, charging station locations were represented via stochastic geometry tools, implying PPP-based spatial modeling to capture density and spatial randomness (Qin et al., 2021).
https://consensus.app/papers/on-the-influence-of-charging-stations-spatial-qin-kishk/c236127147fc50978d6bd7b3f12d8eb5/?utm_source=chatgpt
	Dynamic charging deployment frameworks applied spatial statistical models compatible with PPP assumptions to estimate coverage and performance across real city maps (Nguyen et al., 2022).
https://consensus.app/papers/toward-sustainable-transportation-accelerating-vehicle-nguyen-kishk/8abfda6d4cd850d8ba14647c7fb576dd/?utm_source=chatgpt
Interpretation: Homogeneous PPP is used for analytical tractability in large-scale spatial performance evaluation.
________________________________________
B. Inhomogeneous PPP
Key finding: Explicit inhomogeneous PPP formulations are rarely stated in distribution-network EV placement studies; instead, traffic-density weighting or demand-based optimization is used.
	Urban charging load prediction studies incorporate spatial traffic and transportation-network variation but rely on Monte Carlo and demand modeling rather than formal inhomogeneous PPP (Li & Chang, 2024).
https://consensus.app/papers/ev-charging-station-load-prediction-in-coupled-urban-li-chang/c8e30ec3f3db5d8ab03e510949c160b9/?utm_source=chatgpt
	Integrated EV load and charging infrastructure studies use Gaussian copula methods to model correlated spatial demand, not PPP-based spatial randomness (Fan et al., 2021).
https://consensus.app/papers/integration-of-electric-vehicle-load-and-charging-fan-meng/73abf5c8c1b052e78eaf266498b43d48/?utm_source=chatgpt
Interpretation: Spatial heterogeneity is modeled through transportation-network flows or probabilistic demand models rather than inhomogeneous PPP intensity functions.
________________________________________
C. Cox (Doubly Stochastic) and Cluster Processes
Key finding: No empirical distribution-network EV or DER placement studies (2021–present) were found using Cox or log-Gaussian Cox processes.
	Reviews of integrated transportation–distribution charging planning note the lack of detailed stochastic spatial modeling and highlight reliance on optimization-based or simulation-based frameworks instead of point process models (Unterluggauer et al., 2022).
https://consensus.app/papers/electric-vehicle-charging-infrastructure-planning-for-unterluggauer-rich/ee4d61a47eba5cafbf790ad2fe9fcad0/?utm_source=chatgpt
Interpretation: Despite suitability for capturing demand clustering, Cox or cluster processes have not been widely adopted in distribution power-system EV placement studies.
________________________________________
D. Hard-Core / Repulsive Processes
Key finding: Hard-core or repulsive point processes are not commonly applied in distribution-network EV or DER placement models.
	Charging-station planning studies typically use integer programming, two-stage stochastic programming, or heuristic optimization rather than spatial inhibition models (Wu et al., 2024; Deza et al., 2024).
https://consensus.app/papers/a-twolayer-planning-method-for-location-and-capacity-wu-wang/60880a81b91657da881935eef793cae5/?utm_source=chatgpt
https://consensus.app/papers/electric-vehicle-charging-network-design-under-deza-huang/7fdf1227fd74531b95cb1cfe66acfea4/?utm_source=chatgpt
Interpretation: Spatial exclusion constraints are typically enforced deterministically (via optimization constraints) rather than via hard-core stochastic processes.

4) Gaps and Future Research
	No widespread adoption of Cox or log-Gaussian Cox processes for modeling spatial demand clustering in distribution EV studies.
	Hard-core or repulsive spatial processes are not used to model siting exclusion zones.
	Need for stochastic-geometry models tailored to radial distribution topology rather than planar PPP abstractions.
	Limited integration of spatial point processes with power-flow constraints in hosting-capacity analysis.
	Lack of empirical validation comparing PPP assumptions against real spatial EV adoption patterns.

Q2: How is intensity (λ) interpreted/estimated in power applications (customers per area, active-charger density, thinning by activity probability), and what datasets/methods support estimation?

In power-system applications using stochastic geometry, the intensity parameter \lambdais typically interpreted as the spatial density of charging stations, EV users, or active devices per unit area, and is often adjusted through activity probabilities (thinning) to represent time-varying active loads. Empirical studies estimate \lambdafrom real traffic datasets, urban geographic data, smart-meter records, or transportation-network simulations. When charging activity is stochastic, effective intensity is modeled as \lambda_{\mathrm{active}}=p\lambda, where prepresents charging probability or activation rate. In distribution-planning contexts, Monte Carlo simulation and travel-diary data are commonly used to estimate spatial load density rather than direct point-process fitting.
________________________________________
2) Evidence synthesis
A. λ as Spatial Density of Charging Stations (Stations per Unit Area)
Key finding: In stochastic-geometry-based EV infrastructure studies, \lambdarepresents the spatial density of charging stations (e.g., stations/km²).
	Charging infrastructure sharing analysis modeled station locations via a homogeneous Poisson point process with density \lambda, explicitly interpreting \lambdaas the spatial density of charging stations used to derive coverage and waiting-time metrics (Qin et al., 2022).
https://consensus.app/papers/performance-analysis-of-charging-infrastructure-sharing-qin-kishk/74266e2a22a152f1b4920d78e24e4bb6/?utm_source=chatgpt
	Drone-enabled charging network analysis also modeled station spatial distribution using stochastic geometry, where performance scaled directly with station density \lambda(Qin et al., 2021).
https://consensus.app/papers/on-the-influence-of-charging-stations-spatial-qin-kishk/c236127147fc50978d6bd7b3f12d8eb5/?utm_source=chatgpt
	Dynamic charging deployment across New York City and Xi’an used real traffic and road data to estimate spatial infrastructure density and evaluate performance versus coverage percentage, effectively linking \lambdato road-network deployment ratio (Nguyen et al., 2022).
https://consensus.app/papers/toward-sustainable-transportation-accelerating-vehicle-nguyen-kishk/8abfda6d4cd850d8ba14647c7fb576dd/?utm_source=chatgpt
Dataset support: Real city traffic maps, GIS road datasets, and infrastructure maps were used to estimate deployment density.
________________________________________
B. λ as Active-Charger Density (Thinned PPP)
Key finding: Several studies treat active chargers as a thinned version of the base spatial process using activation probability.
	Probabilistic activation modeling in spatial infrastructure contexts uses independent thinning where each node is active with probability p, leading to effective density p\lambda(Qin et al., 2022).
	Monte Carlo–based EV load simulations treat vehicle arrivals and charging events as stochastic, implicitly thinning spatial customer density by charging probability derived from behavioral models (Yang et al., 2023).
https://consensus.app/papers/electric-vehicle-charging-capacity-of-distribution-yang-cao/440228cd938f53938889d84b308862fb/?utm_source=chatgpt
Dataset support:
	Trip-chain theory and Monte Carlo sampling based on real travel behavior data were used to estimate charging activity probabilities (Yang et al., 2023).
	REFIT residential smart-meter data were used to estimate charging demand intensity over time (Mudgal & Tiwari, 2024).
https://consensus.app/papers/investigation-of-the-impact-of-electric-vehicles-in-the-mudgal-tiwari/aacce17c826851aa9160378ca271b344/?utm_source=chatgpt
Interpretation: Effective λ becomes time-dependent when thinned by charging activity probability.
________________________________________
C. λ as Customer/EV Density in Distribution Networks
Key finding: In distribution hosting-capacity and load-prediction studies, λ corresponds to spatial density of EV owners or charging demand nodes.
	EV load prediction in coupled transportation–distribution networks used Monte Carlo simulation to estimate spatial-temporal EV charging load distributions, effectively treating EV spatial density as a derived parameter from traffic and demographic data (Li & Chang, 2024).
https://consensus.app/papers/ev-charging-station-load-prediction-in-coupled-urban-li-chang/c8e30ec3f3db5d8ab03e510949c160b9/?utm_source=chatgpt
	Integrated EV load and charging infrastructure modeling applied Gaussian copula methods to capture correlated spatial charging demand, using probabilistic multivariate distributions rather than fixed λ (Fan et al., 2021).
https://consensus.app/papers/integration-of-electric-vehicle-load-and-charging-fan-meng/73abf5c8c1b052e78eaf266498b43d48/?utm_source=chatgpt
Dataset support:
	Urban traffic flow datasets
	Smart-meter load datasets
	Household consumption databases
	Transportation network simulations
________________________________________
D. Estimation Methods for λ
Across studies, λ is estimated via:
	Direct density calculation: Number of stations divided by geographic area (Qin et al., 2022).
	GIS-based infrastructure mapping: Road coverage percentage converted to spatial density (Nguyen et al., 2022).
	Monte Carlo sampling of EV arrival rates: Spatial charging intensity derived from simulated trip-chain behavior (Yang et al., 2023).
	Smart-meter datasets: Empirical charging event frequency per household cluster (Mudgal & Tiwari, 2024).
Overall finding: λ is rarely estimated through formal spatial point process fitting (e.g., maximum likelihood); instead, it is computed from infrastructure counts, traffic density, or simulated behavioral probabilities.

4) Gaps and Future Research
	Few studies estimate λ using formal spatial point process fitting (e.g., likelihood-based inference).
	Limited validation of PPP assumptions against real EV adoption spatial patterns.
	Lack of dynamic λ models integrating temporal demand variability into power-flow studies.
	Need for joint spatial–temporal Cox process modeling for clustered EV adoption.
	Insufficient integration of λ uncertainty into hosting-capacity risk assessment.

Q3: What modelling strategies represent spatial uncertainty in region geometry and PCC siting (random domain, uncertain boundary, random observation point), and how do they affect predicted aggregates?

Recent power-system studies represent spatial uncertainty in region geometry and PCC siting using (i) stochastic-geometry models with random domains and random observation points, (ii) Monte Carlo sampling over uncertain customer/device placements and feeder topology, and (iii) probabilistic transportation–distribution coupling that treats EV locations as random over road networks. Random-domain and random-observer formulations (e.g., PPP over planar regions) change predicted aggregates by altering expected source counts and distance distributions, thereby modifying aggregate load, interference, or distortion metrics. In distribution-specific studies, uncertainty in feeder layout and EV siting is propagated via scenario-based Monte Carlo simulations, which broaden predicted aggregate distributions (e.g., peak loading, voltage deviation). Overall, spatial uncertainty primarily affects predicted aggregates through changes in effective source density, clustering, and distance-dependent impedance interactions.
________________________________________
2) Evidence synthesis
A. Random Spatial Domain and Random Observation Point (Stochastic Geometry)
Key finding: Homogeneous PPP over random planar regions with performance evaluated at a “typical” observation point is used to represent spatial uncertainty in charging infrastructure.
	Charging infrastructure sharing analysis modeled station locations as a homogeneous PPP over a planar region and evaluated performance at a typical user (random observation point), deriving coverage and delay metrics as functions of spatial density (Qin et al., 2022).
https://consensus.app/papers/performance-analysis-of-charging-infrastructure-sharing-qin-kishk/74266e2a22a152f1b4920d78e24e4bb6/?utm_source=chatgpt
	Spatial distribution of charging stations was modeled using stochastic geometry to study coverage and influence of spatial randomness, explicitly treating charger locations as spatially random within a defined region (Qin et al., 2021).
https://consensus.app/papers/on-the-influence-of-charging-stations-spatial-qin-kishk/c236127147fc50978d6bd7b3f12d8eb5/?utm_source=chatgpt
Effect on aggregates:
Aggregate performance metrics (e.g., service probability, waiting time, effective coverage) scale with λ and region size; random domain assumptions change the expected number of sources within distance r, altering predicted mean and variance of aggregated load.
________________________________________
B. Uncertain Region Geometry via GIS-Based and Road-Network Modeling
Key finding: Road-network–constrained spatial uncertainty is modeled by embedding charging locations along real geographic maps rather than fixed geometric regions.
	Dynamic charging deployment was evaluated over real urban maps (New York City, Xi’an), where infrastructure was randomly deployed along road segments; predicted aggregate coverage depended strongly on spatial geometry and deployment percentage (Nguyen et al., 2022).
https://consensus.app/papers/toward-sustainable-transportation-accelerating-vehicle-nguyen-kishk/8abfda6d4cd850d8ba14647c7fb576dd/?utm_source=chatgpt
	Coupled transportation–distribution EV load modeling used spatially varying traffic flows and node locations, meaning that region geometry (urban density gradients) directly influenced predicted charging aggregates (Li & Chang, 2024).
https://consensus.app/papers/ev-charging-station-load-prediction-in-coupled-urban-li-chang/c8e30ec3f3db5d8ab03e510949c160b9/?utm_source=chatgpt
Effect on aggregates:
Non-uniform geometry produces spatially heterogeneous charging densities, increasing variance of feeder-level aggregates compared to uniform-domain PPP assumptions.
________________________________________
C. Random PCC Siting and Monte Carlo Scenario Sampling
Key finding: Distribution-level studies represent uncertain PCC or EV siting by Monte Carlo random assignment of EVs or chargers to network nodes.
	EV charging capacity analysis of distribution networks used Monte Carlo sampling based on trip-chain behavior to randomly assign EV charging events to feeder nodes, producing probabilistic voltage deviation and hosting-capacity distributions (Yang et al., 2023).
https://consensus.app/papers/electric-vehicle-charging-capacity-of-distribution-yang-cao/440228cd938f53938889d84b308862fb/?utm_source=chatgpt
	Investigation of EV impact using REFIT smart-meter data generated random charging scenarios across residential nodes to evaluate voltage and loading variability, explicitly modeling random observation nodes (Mudgal & Tiwari, 2024).
https://consensus.app/papers/investigation-of-the-impact-of-electric-vehicles-in-the-mudgal-tiwari/aacce17c826851aa9160378ca271b344/?utm_source=chatgpt
Effect on aggregates:
Random PCC siting broadens predicted peak-load and voltage-drop distributions; aggregates become scenario-dependent rather than deterministic.
________________________________________
D. Spatial Correlation and Coupled Networks
Key finding: Spatial uncertainty is also represented through correlated placement tied to transportation flows rather than independent PPP placement.
	Integration of EV load and infrastructure used correlated probabilistic modeling (e.g., Gaussian copula) to reflect spatial dependence between customer demand points, affecting predicted distribution loading (Fan et al., 2021).
https://consensus.app/papers/integration-of-electric-vehicle-load-and-charging-fan-meng/73abf5c8c1b052e78eaf266498b43d48/?utm_source=chatgpt
Effect on aggregates:
Correlated spatial siting increases clustering, which raises local voltage stress and peak feeder loading compared to independent random placement.

	Few studies model uncertain feeder boundaries as random geometric domains.
	Limited use of Cox or cluster processes to model spatial clustering explicitly.
	Lack of analytical results linking random-domain uncertainty to harmonic/distortion aggregates at PCC.
	Need for integrated stochastic-geometry + power-flow models on radial networks.
	Sparse empirical validation comparing deterministic vs. random-domain assumptions.

2.4 Electrical mark model for supraharmonic sources
Q1: What emission “mark” definitions are used for converters at supraharmonic frequencies (complex injection, magnitude/phase, frequency-dependent spectra, time-state marks)?
In supraharmonic (2–150 kHz) studies, converter emission “marks” are most commonly defined as frequency-dependent complex current injections (magnitude and phase per frequency bin), often derived via FFT or dynamic phasor estimation. Some models represent marks as Norton-equivalent current sources with frequency-dependent internal impedance, while others use crossed-frequency admittance matrices (CFAM) to capture inter-frequency coupling. Time-varying or quasi-stationary studies introduce time-state marks, where spectra are indexed by operating stage or dynamic phasor parameters. Overall, emission marks range from simple magnitude-only spectra to fully complex, frequency-coupled, and state-dependent representations.
________________________________________
2) Evidence synthesis
A. Complex Current Injection (Magnitude + Phase per Frequency)
Key finding: Emissions are frequently represented as complex current phasors per frequency bin in the 2–150 kHz band.
	Experimental supraharmonic inverter studies characterized emissions using spectral magnitude and phase components, demonstrating that both parameters influence coupling and distortion propagation (Kaufhold et al., 2021).
https://consensus.app/papers/impact-of-harmonic-distortion-on-the-supraharmonic-kaufhold-meyer/52637ab6cb135bffa848019ba660d7ee/?utm_source=chatgpt
	Data-center UPS propagation studies measured supraharmonic currents and voltages as complex frequency-domain quantities to analyze transfer through impedance networks (Sutaria & Rönnberg, 2023).
https://consensus.app/papers/propagation-of-supraharmonics-in-a-data-center-with-sutaria-rönnberg/4d8a12ec8d4a577484fd6a9866313198/?utm_source=chatgpt
Interpretation: The emission mark is a complex-valued current injection I(f)=\mid I(f)\mid e^{j\phi(f)}.
________________________________________
B. Frequency-Dependent Spectral Marks (Amplitude Spectra)
Key finding: Many studies define emission marks as frequency-dependent magnitude spectra measured within defined resolution bandwidths.
	Supraharmonic emissions from DC grid-connected wireless power transfer converters were reported as spectral magnitudes across frequency with specified resolution bandwidth (e.g., 200 Hz, 500 Hz), forming a frequency-dependent emission signature (Mariscotti et al., 2022).
https://consensus.app/papers/supraharmonic-emissions-from-dc-grid-connected-wireless-mariscotti-sandrolini/a14f12a6ea5f5792a71e29ba842a3053/?utm_source=chatgpt
	MV network propagation studies used measured spectral amplitudes to evaluate resonance and amplification effects, effectively treating emissions as magnitude-versus-frequency marks (Letha et al., 2021).
https://consensus.app/papers/evaluation-of-medium-voltage-network-for-propagation-of-letha-delgado/76c9d68e140c53ff8df25b6a1f2113b2/?utm_source=chatgpt
Interpretation: Marks are spectral amplitude vectors over the 2–150 kHz range.
________________________________________
C. Norton-Equivalent and Admittance-Based Marks
Key finding: Some analytical models define emission marks as Norton-equivalent sources with frequency-dependent impedance.
	Modeling of unintentional emissions of single-phase converters defined the emission through equivalent current source models combined with frequency-dependent impedance, enabling distortion prediction under varying grid conditions (Collin et al., 2024).
https://consensus.app/papers/modeling-the-unintentional-emissions-of-singlephase-collin-meyer/d230da580ac55309a7996ab4c2b83f3d/?utm_source=chatgpt
	Crossed-frequency admittance matrix (CFAM) modeling introduced emission marks as multi-frequency injection vectors where current at one frequency depends on voltage at others, extending beyond independent per-frequency marks (Yang et al., 2025).
https://consensus.app/papers/a-piecewise-linearization-based-method-for-crossed-yang-wang/0c2417603e9d536f85c37441f403fcc3/?utm_source=chatgpt
Interpretation: Marks can be vector-valued across frequencies with cross-coupling terms, not purely diagonal.
________________________________________
D. Time-State or Dynamic Phasor Marks
Key finding: When emissions are time-varying, marks are indexed by operating state or estimated via dynamic phasor models.
	Dynamic phasor estimation of supraharmonics used Taylor–Fourier multi-frequency models to represent emissions as time-varying phasors I(f,t), capturing pulsating and non-stationary behavior (Frigo & Braun, 2022).
https://consensus.app/papers/supraharmonic-dynamic-phasors-estimation-of-timevarying-frigo-braun/2996a9450e5857d8a6e8f5839c176bdc/?utm_source=chatgpt
	BESS-integrated fast-charging measurements segmented emissions by operating stage, effectively defining emission marks conditional on system state (Giri & Rönnberg, 2025).
https://consensus.app/papers/evaluation-of-waveform-distortion-in-bessintegrated-giri-rönnberg/555dcfd819c556e0b2297af99895f10e/?utm_source=chatgpt
Interpretation: Marks may be state-dependent spectral signatures rather than static frequency vectors.
________________________________________

Kaufhold et al., 2021	Complex magnitude + phase per frequency	Limited	Stationary	Demonstrated coupling effects
Mariscotti et al., 2022	Frequency-dependent magnitude spectrum	No	Stationary window	Resolution-bandwidth-based spectra
Collin et al., 2024	Norton-equivalent current source + impedance	Diagonal	Stationary	Grid-condition-aware modeling
Yang et al., 2025	Cross-frequency admittance matrix	Yes (off-diagonal terms)	Piecewise	Multi-frequency interaction model
Frigo & Braun, 2022	Time-varying dynamic phasors	Possible	Yes	Pulsating emission representation
Giri & Rönnberg, 2025	Stage-indexed spectra	No explicit cross-frequency	Yes (state-based)	Operating-state segmentation

4) Gaps and Future Research
	Lack of unified statistical mark definitions combining magnitude, phase, and state dependence.
	Limited probabilistic modeling of emission marks as random vectors.
	Few studies integrate CFAM cross-frequency coupling into stochastic aggregate models.
	Insufficient validation of dynamic phasor marks under real multi-device conditions.
	Need standardized emission-mark datasets for 2–150 kHz modeling.

Q2: What empirical distributions or parametric models have been reported for emission magnitudes and phases (lognormal, mixture models, heavy-tailed, bounded), and how are parameters fitted?

Recent empirical studies (2021–present) report that supraharmonic emission magnitudes are most often modeled using empirical distributions (histograms, percentiles, CCDFs) or parametric distributions such as lognormal and Gaussian, while mixture and copula-based models are used to capture inter-frequency or inter-source dependence. Heavy-tailed behavior is observed in aggregated harmonic magnitudes under stochastic EV charging and nonlinear load scenarios, though explicit Pareto-type fits are rare. Phases are typically treated as either uniformly distributed (for aggregation studies) or empirically measured complex phasors, with some works modeling magnitude–phase correlation. Parameters are commonly fitted via maximum likelihood estimation (MLE), method of moments, Monte Carlo simulation calibration, or data-driven learning (e.g., ANN regression).
________________________________________
2) Evidence synthesis
A. Lognormal and Gaussian Models for Emission Magnitudes
Key finding: Lognormal and Gaussian-type distributions are frequently used for modeling harmonic/supraharmonic magnitudes in distribution studies.
	Harmonic estimation studies using oversampling techniques model harmonic current variability statistically and evaluate estimation uncertainty assuming Gaussian-like error structures, effectively treating magnitudes as normally distributed residuals (Eslami et al., 2022).
https://consensus.app/papers/harmonic-current-estimation-of-unmonitored-harmonic-eslami-negnevitsky/21f1d5ff69ea52b1a381c2b08b1c0652/?utm_source=chatgpt
	In EV load and charging infrastructure integration, probabilistic demand distributions were modeled using Gaussian copulas to capture correlated charging magnitudes across nodes, implying marginal Gaussian or lognormal representations of magnitude variables (Fan et al., 2021).
https://consensus.app/papers/integration-of-electric-vehicle-load-and-charging-fan-meng/73abf5c8c1b052e78eaf266498b43d48/?utm_source=chatgpt
Parameter fitting:
Parameters were fitted using historical load data and statistical correlation matrices (Fan et al., 2021), and ANN-based residual error fitting (Eslami et al., 2022).
________________________________________
B. Empirical and Percentile-Based (Non-Parametric) Models
Key finding: Many supraharmonic studies report empirical magnitude distributions via histograms, percentiles (e.g., 95th), and CCDF-style curves rather than closed-form parametric fits.
	Supraharmonic current summation from EV fast chargers analyzed magnitude distributions over time and reported diversity factors based on measured statistical spread rather than assuming a predefined distribution (Slangen et al., 2023).
https://consensus.app/papers/summation-of-supraharmonic-currents-2–150-khz-from-ev-fast-slangen-ćuk/2ba7397d15f25611b5f94a624d0dcae5/?utm_source=chatgpt
	Inaccuracies and uncertainties in harmonic estimation were quantified via statistical spread and uncertainty ranges derived from measurement datasets, emphasizing empirical distribution characterization (Iqbal et al., 2024).
https://consensus.app/papers/inaccuracies-and-uncertainties-for-harmonic-estimation-iqbal-kütt/efbfa47b263b5bbdb3a115abd139b42e/?utm_source=chatgpt
Parameter fitting:
Distribution shape inferred from measurement histograms; percentiles and RMS statistics computed directly from sample sets.
________________________________________
C. Mixture and Copula-Based Models for Correlated Emissions
Key finding: Mixture-type and copula-based models are used to capture spatial and cross-frequency dependence between emissions.
	Integrated EV load modeling applied Gaussian copula techniques to represent correlated charging demand across nodes, allowing joint distribution modeling of emission magnitudes (Fan et al., 2021).
	Crossed-frequency admittance modeling demonstrates that current injection at one frequency depends on voltage distortion at others, implying that multivariate (possibly mixture or vector) distributions are needed rather than independent marginals (Yang et al., 2025).
https://consensus.app/papers/a-piecewise-linearization-based-method-for-crossed-yang-wang/0c2417603e9d536f85c37441f403fcc3/?utm_source=chatgpt
Parameter fitting:
Correlation matrices estimated from measured or simulated harmonic datasets; parameters tuned via least-squares or linearization fitting (Yang et al., 2025).
________________________________________
D. Heavy-Tailed and Aggregation Effects
Key finding: Aggregated harmonic/supraharmonic magnitudes exhibit broader-than-Gaussian spreads, indicating heavy-tailed or skewed behavior.
	Field aggregation measurements of harmonic currents show non-monotonic and skewed distributions due to cancellation and diversity effects, contradicting simple Gaussian assumptions (Daniel et al., 2022).
https://consensus.app/papers/current-harmonic-aggregation-cases-for-contemporary-daniel-kütt/c4b018fefb82534599b71712c2cfb41c/?utm_source=chatgpt
	EV fast-charger supraharmonic measurements showed variability dependent on activity state and phase alignment, leading to wide distribution tails in instantaneous magnitude statistics (Slangen et al., 2023).
Interpretation: Although explicit Pareto or power-law fits are rare, empirical evidence suggests skewed and occasionally heavy-tailed magnitude distributions.
________________________________________
E. Phase Distribution Assumptions
Key finding: Phases are often treated as uniformly distributed for aggregation modeling, but measurements reveal structured phase relationships.
	Harmonic aggregation studies demonstrate phase-dependent cancellation, implying that uniform phase assumptions may not hold in practice (Daniel et al., 2022).
	Cross-frequency admittance modeling shows phase coupling between frequency components, implying correlated phase structures (Yang et al., 2025).
Parameter fitting:
Phase statistics derived directly from complex phasor measurements; correlation assessed via empirical covariance matrices.
________________________________________

Slangen et al., 2023	Empirical histogram	Direct sample statistics	Magnitude	Skewed aggregation distribution

4) Gaps and Future Research
	Few explicit lognormal or heavy-tailed parametric fits reported for supraharmonic magnitudes.
	Limited joint magnitude–phase probabilistic modeling.
	Lack of standardized CCDF-based distribution reporting.
	Sparse large-scale datasets for validating tail behavior.
	Need multivariate stochastic emission-mark models incorporating frequency coupling.

Q3: How do studies incorporate device input admittance/absorption (frequency-dependent, stochastic, state-dependent), and what impact does it have on aggregation predictions?

Recent empirical studies incorporate device input admittance (or absorption) in the 2–150 kHz range using frequency-dependent impedance/admittance measurements, Norton-equivalent models, and crossed-frequency admittance matrices (CFAM) that capture inter-frequency coupling. Some works further treat admittance as state-dependent, varying with operating mode, load level, or background distortion. Incorporating admittance/absorption significantly alters aggregation predictions: measured supraharmonic currents do not sum linearly because devices can absorb or amplify emissions depending on impedance interaction. Overall, accounting for frequency-dependent and state-dependent admittance reduces overestimation of aggregate distortion compared to independent current-source models.
________________________________________
2) Evidence synthesis
A. Frequency-Dependent Admittance and Norton-Equivalent Modeling
Key finding: Devices are modeled as Norton equivalents (current source + frequency-dependent impedance), explicitly incorporating input admittance into propagation and aggregation models.
	Modeling of unintentional emissions from single-phase converters defined frequency-dependent input impedance and equivalent current sources, showing that predicted distortion at the PCC depends on both source emission and converter admittance (Collin et al., 2024).
https://consensus.app/papers/modeling-the-unintentional-emissions-of-singlephase-collin-meyer/d230da580ac55309a7996ab4c2b83f3d/?utm_source=chatgpt
	MV network supraharmonic propagation analysis used frequency-dependent network impedance to demonstrate resonance amplification and attenuation effects, effectively showing that absorption depends on the combined admittance of connected devices (Letha et al., 2021).
https://consensus.app/papers/evaluation-of-medium-voltage-network-for-propagation-of-letha-delgado/76c9d68e140c53ff8df25b6a1f2113b2/?utm_source=chatgpt
Impact on aggregation:
Frequency-dependent admittance modifies voltage distortion prediction via V(f)=Z_{eq}(f)I_{inj}(f); ignoring admittance can misestimate resonance peaks.
________________________________________
B. Cross-Frequency Admittance and Coupled Interaction
Key finding: Crossed-frequency admittance matrix (CFAM) modeling captures how device input admittance couples frequencies and affects aggregation.
	A piecewise linearization method for CFAM modeling showed that current injection at one frequency depends on voltage components at other frequencies, meaning admittance is not diagonal and influences multi-frequency aggregation (Yang et al., 2025).
https://consensus.app/papers/a-piecewise-linearization-based-method-for-crossed-yang-wang/0c2417603e9d536f85c37441f403fcc3/?utm_source=chatgpt
	Experimental inverter studies demonstrated that harmonic distortion influences supraharmonic emissions, implying admittance-dependent intermodulation effects (Kaufhold et al., 2021).
https://consensus.app/papers/impact-of-harmonic-distortion-on-the-supraharmonic-kaufhold-meyer/52637ab6cb135bffa848019ba660d7ee/?utm_source=chatgpt
Impact on aggregation:
Aggregation is no longer simple superposition; cross-frequency admittance can introduce additional spectral components or alter magnitudes.
________________________________________
C. Absorption and Non-Monotonic Aggregation Effects
Key finding: Field measurements confirm that connected devices may absorb supraharmonic currents from other devices.
	Supraharmonic summation measurements of EV fast-charging stations showed that total current does not increase proportionally with the number of chargers; some chargers absorb or damp certain frequency components due to impedance interaction (Slangen et al., 2023).
https://consensus.app/papers/summation-of-supraharmonic-currents-2–150-khz-from-ev-fast-slangen-ćuk/2ba7397d15f25611b5f94a624d0dcae5/?utm_source=chatgpt
	Harmonic aggregation experiments on contemporary nonlinear loads demonstrated cancellation and absorption phenomena driven by impedance and phase relationships (Daniel et al., 2022).
https://consensus.app/papers/current-harmonic-aggregation-cases-for-contemporary-daniel-kütt/c4b018fefb82534599b71712c2cfb41c/?utm_source=chatgpt
Impact on aggregation:
Ignoring absorption leads to conservative (overestimated) aggregate emission predictions.
________________________________________
D. State-Dependent and Background-Dependent Admittance
Key finding: Device admittance changes with operating state and background distortion.
	BESS-integrated fast-charging measurements showed that spectral characteristics differ across operating stages, implying state-dependent admittance behavior (Giri & Rönnberg, 2025).
https://consensus.app/papers/evaluation-of-waveform-distortion-in-bessintegrated-giri-rönnberg/555dcfd819c556e0b2297af99895f10e/?utm_source=chatgpt
	Studies comparing impacts of background distortion showed that even- and odd-order background harmonics modify converter supraharmonic emissions, indicating that effective admittance depends on supply distortion (Aboutaleb et al., 2024).
https://consensus.app/papers/comparison-of-the-impacts-of-background-distortion-with-aboutaleb-osheba/281370088dcf59169d191423c64403e5/?utm_source=chatgpt
Impact on aggregation:
State-dependent admittance increases variability of aggregate predictions and challenges stationary aggregation assumptions.

2.5 Propagation kernel (transfer impedance) and integrability conditions
Q1: What methods exist to compute or estimate transfer impedance/transfer functions from distributed injections to PCC in the 2–150 kHz band (analytical cable models, network reduction, EMTP, measurement-based)?

In the 2–150 kHz supraharmonic band, transfer impedance or transfer functions from distributed injections to the PCC are computed using (i) analytical frequency-dependent cable and line models combined with nodal/admittance matrix reduction, (ii) EMTP-type time-domain simulations converted to frequency responses, (iii) Norton-equivalent and impedance-based network reduction methods, and (iv) measurement-based impedance identification techniques. Analytical and admittance-matrix approaches are commonly used for steady-state frequency sweeps, while EMTP models capture detailed cable parasitics and resonance. Measurement-based approaches estimate transfer impedance by injecting or observing spectral components and computing frequency-domain ratios. Across studies, the predicted PCC distortion strongly depends on resonance peaks and frequency-dependent damping captured by these methods.
________________________________________
2) Evidence synthesis
A. Analytical Cable and Line Models (Frequency-Dependent)
Key finding: Frequency-dependent cable/line models are used to derive transfer impedance functions analytically across the 2–150 kHz band.
	Medium-voltage network propagation analysis computed supraharmonic transfer characteristics using frequency-dependent line and transformer impedances in nodal models, demonstrating resonance amplification at specific frequencies (Letha et al., 2021).
https://consensus.app/papers/evaluation-of-medium-voltage-network-for-propagation-of-letha-delgado/76c9d68e140c53ff8df25b6a1f2113b2/?utm_source=chatgpt
	Modeling of unintentional emissions of single-phase converters employed analytical network models including cable impedance and converter input impedance to compute transfer functions from device injection to PCC voltage (Collin et al., 2024).
https://consensus.app/papers/modeling-the-unintentional-emissions-of-singlephase-collin-meyer/d230da580ac55309a7996ab4c2b83f3d/?utm_source=chatgpt
Method principle:
Transfer impedance Z_{tr}(f)is obtained from the nodal admittance matrix Y(f)via network inversion or reduction.
________________________________________
B. Network Reduction and Admittance-Matrix Methods
Key finding: Nodal admittance formulations and reduced-order models are used to compute multi-node transfer functions.
	Crossed-frequency admittance matrix (CFAM) modeling computes multi-frequency current–voltage relationships by forming extended admittance matrices and applying piecewise linearization, allowing transfer estimation including cross-frequency effects (Yang et al., 2025).
https://consensus.app/papers/a-piecewise-linearization-based-method-for-crossed-yang-wang/0c2417603e9d536f85c37441f403fcc3/?utm_source=chatgpt
	Supraharmonic propagation in data centers used impedance-network representations of UPS systems and feeders, effectively reducing network elements to equivalent transfer impedances between injection points and PCC (Sutaria & Rönnberg, 2023).
https://consensus.app/papers/propagation-of-supraharmonics-in-a-data-center-with-sutaria-rönnberg/4d8a12ec8d4a577484fd6a9866313198/?utm_source=chatgpt
Method principle:
Transfer functions derived from reduced Y-matrix inversion or Norton-equivalent substitution.
________________________________________
C. EMTP and Time-Domain Simulation Approaches
Key finding: Electromagnetic transient programs (EMTP-type tools) are used to model frequency-dependent cable parameters and derive transfer responses.
	MV supraharmonic studies validated frequency-domain resonance predictions using detailed time-domain simulation models of distribution feeders including cable capacitance and inductance, then extracting frequency spectra (Letha et al., 2021).
	Converter emission modeling combined detailed switching models with frequency-domain analysis to estimate transfer from internal switching ripple to grid-side voltage (Collin et al., 2024).
Method principle:
Inject time-domain switching waveform → FFT → compute ratio V_{PCC}(f)/I_{inj}(f).
________________________________________
D. Measurement-Based Impedance Identification
Key finding: Experimental measurements are used to estimate transfer impedance directly by measuring voltage and current spectra.
	Bidirectional EV charger studies measured grid impedance in the supraharmonic range using spectral analysis of voltage/current interactions, deriving frequency-dependent transfer impedance (Grasel et al., 2022).
https://consensus.app/papers/supraharmonic-and-harmonic-emissions-of-a-bidirectional-grasel-baptista/9524a0b0f9bf5e5b8151331e87354a8c/?utm_source=chatgpt
	Supraharmonic aggregation measurements from EV fast charging stations compared injected current spectra and measured PCC voltage to infer effective transfer impedance behavior (Slangen et al., 2023).
https://consensus.app/papers/summation-of-supraharmonic-currents-2–150-khz-from-ev-fast-slangen-ćuk/2ba7397d15f25611b5f94a624d0dcae5/?utm_source=chatgpt
Method principle:
Estimate Z_{tr}(f)=V_{PCC}(f)/I_{inj}(f)using synchronized spectral measurements.

Q2: What simplified kernel forms (distance-based attenuation, electrical-distance kernels, resonance envelopes) have been proposed, and under what feeder archetypes are they valid?
Recent studies (2021–present) propose simplified kernels for supraharmonic propagation that approximate full network transfer functions using (i) distance-based attenuation laws, (ii) electrical-distance/impedance-based kernels derived from nodal admittance reductions, and (iii) resonance-envelope models capturing frequency-dependent amplification bands. Distance-based attenuation is typically valid in short radial LV feeders with weak resonance, while electrical-distance kernels apply to radial MV/LV feeders where impedance can be approximated by cumulative series elements. Resonance-envelope models are needed in feeders with significant shunt capacitance (e.g., underground cables, data centers) where frequency-selective amplification dominates. Across studies, simplified kernels are valid only when cross-frequency coupling is weak and network topology is approximately radial.
2) Evidence synthesis
A. Distance-Based Attenuation Kernels
Key finding: In radial feeders with moderate damping, supraharmonic transfer can be approximated by attenuation increasing with physical or electrical distance from the PCC.
	MV network propagation analysis showed that supraharmonic voltage distortion depends strongly on feeder length and topology, with attenuation/amplification governed by line impedance accumulation along radial paths (Letha et al., 2021).
https://consensus.app/papers/evaluation-of-medium-voltage-network-for-propagation-of-letha-delgado/76c9d68e140c53ff8df25b6a1f2113b2/?utm_source=chatgpt
	Data-center UPS propagation measurements demonstrated that transfer magnitude decreases with distance when resonance is absent, supporting approximate distance-based attenuation kernels in locally radial segments (Sutaria & Rönnberg, 2023).
https://consensus.app/papers/propagation-of-supraharmonics-in-a-data-center-with-sutaria-rönnberg/4d8a12ec8d4a577484fd6a9866313198/?utm_source=chatgpt
Feeder archetype validity:
	Radial LV/MV feeders
	Overhead lines with relatively low shunt capacitance
	Weak resonance conditions
B. Electrical-Distance / Impedance-Based Kernels
Key finding: Transfer kernels can be expressed using electrical distance (cumulative series impedance) derived from nodal admittance matrices.
	Analytical modeling of unintentional converter emissions used frequency-dependent cable and transformer impedance in nodal models to compute transfer functions; these can be reduced to equivalent impedance between injection node and PCC (Collin et al., 2024).
https://consensus.app/papers/modeling-the-unintentional-emissions-of-singlephase-collin-meyer/d230da580ac55309a7996ab4c2b83f3d/?utm_source=chatgpt
	Crossed-frequency admittance matrix (CFAM) modeling provides reduced-form transfer functions derived from network admittance inversion, effectively yielding electrical-distance-based kernels (Yang et al., 2025).
https://consensus.app/papers/a-piecewise-linearization-based-method-for-crossed-yang-wang/0c2417603e9d536f85c37441f403fcc3/?utm_source=chatgpt
Feeder archetype validity:
	Radial or weakly meshed MV networks
	Feeders where frequency-dependent impedance dominates over mutual coupling
	Systems without strong nonlinear cross-frequency interaction
C. Resonance Envelope Kernels
Key finding: In cable-rich or capacitor-dominated networks, simplified models approximate transfer using resonance envelopes rather than monotonic attenuation.
	MV supraharmonic resonance studies identified discrete resonance peaks caused by cable capacitance and transformer inductance; simplified envelope models approximate transfer magnitude near resonance frequencies (Letha et al., 2021).
	Bidirectional EV charger measurements showed that adding converter capacitance shifts resonance frequencies and modifies the supraharmonic transfer envelope (Grasel et al., 2022).
https://consensus.app/papers/supraharmonic-and-harmonic-emissions-of-a-bidirectional-grasel-baptista/9524a0b0f9bf5e5b8151331e87354a8c/?utm_source=chatgpt
	Data-center studies showed strong amplification bands in cable-heavy infrastructures, requiring resonance-aware transfer characterization rather than distance-only attenuation (Sutaria & Rönnberg, 2023).
Feeder archetype validity:
	Underground cable-dominated MV feeders
	Data centers with high shunt capacitance
	Networks with distributed power-electronic filters
D. Aggregation-Aware Simplified Kernels
Key finding: In multi-device scenarios, simplified kernels must account for absorption and interaction effects.
	Supraharmonic summation measurements demonstrated that aggregation cannot be modeled by simple additive kernels because devices interact via shared impedance, implying that simplified kernels must incorporate damping or absorption factors (Slangen et al., 2023).
https://consensus.app/papers/summation-of-supraharmonic-currents-2–150-khz-from-ev-fast-slangen-ćuk/2ba7397d15f25611b5f94a624d0dcae5/?utm_source=chatgpt
Feeder archetype validity:
	High-penetration EV fast-charging clusters
	Shared PCC with multiple parallel converters
3) Summary Table
Kernel Type	Simplified Form	Valid Feeder Archetype	Supporting Study	Limitation
Distance-based attenuation		Z(f,d)	∝ e^{-αd} or impedance growth with length	Radial LV/MV, weak resonance
Electrical-distance kernel	Reduced nodal impedance	Radial MV networks	Collin et al., 2024	Ignores cross-frequency coupling
Resonance-envelope	Peak amplification around f_res	Cable-heavy MV, data centers	Grasel et al., 2022; Sutaria & Rönnberg, 2023	Site-specific
Aggregation-damped kernel	Additive + damping factor	Multi-charger PCC	Slangen et al., 2023	Empirical
4) Gaps and Future Research
	Lack of closed-form parametric kernel expressions validated across diverse feeders.
	Few studies quantify accuracy loss of distance-only kernels near resonance.
	Limited probabilistic kernel formulations incorporating impedance uncertainty.
	Need hybrid kernels combining electrical distance and resonance envelopes.
	Sparse validation for meshed urban MV networks.
