Q3: What mathematical conditions ensure finite mean/variance of shot-noise aggregates (kernel decay, bounded domains, regularisation), and how are these conditions interpreted physically?

For supraharmonic shot-noise–type aggregates (e.g., spatial sums of injected currents filtered by transfer kernels), finite mean and variance are ensured when (i) the transfer kernel is square-integrable or sufficiently decaying with electrical distance, (ii) the spatial domain is bounded or the point-process intensity is finite, and (iii) device emissions and admittances are bounded or regularized near resonance. Empirical distribution-network studies show that frequency-dependent cable losses and damping prevent non-decaying kernels, while bounded feeder lengths limit aggregate growth (Letha et al., 2021; Collin et al., 2024). Measurement-based studies further show that absorption and impedance interaction reduce effective gain, acting as physical regularization (Slangen et al., 2023; Sutaria & Rönnberg, 2023). Physically, these conditions correspond to finite feeder extent, resistive damping, and non-ideal converter behavior that suppress infinite resonance amplification.
________________________________________
2) Evidence synthesis
A. Kernel Decay via Frequency-Dependent Impedance (Square-Integrable Transfer)
Mathematical condition:
For a shot-noise field S=\sum_{i}{h(}d_i,f)M_i(f), finite mean and variance require \int\mid h(d,f)\mid\mathrm{\thinsp} dd<\inftyand \int\mid h(d,f)\mid^2\mathrm{\thinsp} dd<\infty.
Key finding: In MV feeders, frequency-dependent line impedance (including resistance and skin effect) introduces attenuation that ensures transfer magnitude decays with electrical distance.
	Supraharmonic propagation studies showed that cable resistance and damping limit resonance amplification and prevent unbounded voltage growth with distance (Letha et al., 2021).
https://consensus.app/papers/evaluation-of-medium-voltage-network-for-propagation-of-letha-delgado/76c9d68e140c53ff8df25b6a1f2113b2/?utm_source=chatgpt
	Converter emission modeling incorporating cable impedance demonstrated that transfer functions remain bounded because of frequency-dependent losses and finite network impedance (Collin et al., 2024).
https://consensus.app/papers/modeling-the-unintentional-emissions-of-singlephase-collin-meyer/d230da580ac55309a7996ab4c2b83f3d/?utm_source=chatgpt
Physical interpretation:
Resistive losses and skin-effect damping ensure energy dissipation, preventing infinite accumulation of injected spectral components.
________________________________________
B. Bounded Domains and Finite Intensity
Mathematical condition:
Finite expected aggregate requires finite point-process intensity \lambdaand bounded integration domain (finite feeder length or finite number of customers).
Key finding: Field aggregation measurements are inherently bounded by feeder topology and number of devices.
	EV fast-charger aggregation measurements showed total supraharmonic current increases only with the finite number of active chargers, demonstrating practical boundedness of the shot-noise sum (Slangen et al., 2023).
https://consensus.app/papers/summation-of-supraharmonic-currents-2–150-khz-from-ev-fast-slangen-ćuk/2ba7397d15f25611b5f94a624d0dcae5/?utm_source=chatgpt
	Data-center supraharmonic propagation studies analyzed finite cable segments and UPS clusters, ensuring bounded summation domains (Sutaria & Rönnberg, 2023).
https://consensus.app/papers/propagation-of-supraharmonics-in-a-data-center-with-sutaria-rönnberg/4d8a12ec8d4a577484fd6a9866313198/?utm_source=chatgpt
Physical interpretation:
Distribution feeders are spatially finite; thus, the number of injection points is finite or has finite expectation.
________________________________________
C. Regularization via Admittance and Absorption (Resonance Control)
Mathematical condition:
Finite variance requires that the transfer kernel does not diverge near resonance; this is ensured if system admittance includes damping terms (positive real part).
Key finding: Device input admittance and background impedance interaction limit resonance peaks.
	CFAM modeling shows that admittance matrices include resistive components that prevent singular transfer behavior in multi-frequency coupling (Yang et al., 2025).
https://consensus.app/papers/a-piecewise-linearization-based-method-for-crossed-yang-wang/0c2417603e9d536f85c37441f403fcc3/?utm_source=chatgpt
	Bidirectional EV charger studies observed that converter connection modifies grid impedance and shifts resonance frequencies, preventing uncontrolled amplification (Grasel et al., 2022).
https://consensus.app/papers/supraharmonic-and-harmonic-emissions-of-a-bidirectional-grasel-baptista/9524a0b0f9bf5e5b8151331e87354a8c/?utm_source=chatgpt
Physical interpretation:
Damping (real admittance) acts as a regularization term preventing infinite variance near resonant frequencies.
________________________________________
D. Bounded Emission Marks (Finite Second Moment of Marks)
Mathematical condition:
Finite variance also requires \mathbb{E}[\mid M\mid^2]<∞.
Key finding: Empirical supraharmonic emission magnitudes are bounded by converter ratings and standards compliance.
	Measurements of supraharmonic currents from EV fast chargers showed bounded magnitude ranges across frequency bins (Slangen et al., 2023).
	Converter emission studies demonstrate emission spectra constrained by switching frequency and hardware limits (Kaufhold et al., 2021).
https://consensus.app/papers/impact-of-harmonic-distortion-on-the-supraharmonic-kaufhold-meyer/52637ab6cb135bffa848019ba660d7ee/?utm_source=chatgpt
Physical interpretation:
Converter hardware imposes maximum current magnitude, ensuring bounded second moments.
________________________________________

Finite intensity	λ < ∞	Finite number of chargers	Slangen et al., 2023

4) Gaps and Future Research
	Few studies explicitly derive integrability conditions for supraharmonic shot-noise models.
	Limited probabilistic validation of second-moment bounds under high-penetration EV scenarios.
	Need stochastic resonance models incorporating impedance uncertainty.
	Sparse analytical work linking CFAM models to shot-noise convergence theory.
	Lack of empirical validation under near-resonant weak-damping feeders.

2.6 Aggregate voltage as a marked shot-noise functional
Q1: How is “shot noise” (random sums over spatial points) introduced and applied in electrical/power contexts, especially for interference-like aggregates?

In recent electrical and power-system studies (2021–present), “shot noise” appears primarily through stochastic-geometry formulations where aggregate interference or distortion is modeled as a random sum of spatially distributed injections filtered by distance- or impedance-dependent kernels. In EV charging and converter-rich networks, random charger/device locations are modeled using Poisson point processes (PPP), and aggregate performance (e.g., load, interference-like effects, voltage distortion) is derived analytically or via Monte Carlo as sums over random spatial points (Qin et al., 2022; Nguyen et al., 2022). Although distribution-grid papers rarely use the term “shot noise” explicitly, their formulations—random spatial deployments combined with transfer kernels—are mathematically equivalent to shot-noise fields. In distribution contexts, these sums are further modified by impedance interactions and absorption effects that deviate from ideal independent shot-noise assumptions (Slangen et al., 2023; Letha et al., 2021).
________________________________________
2) Evidence synthesis
A. Stochastic-Geometry Aggregates as Shot-Noise Fields
Concept:
Shot noise in spatial systems is defined as
S=\sum_{x_i\in\Phi}{h(}x_i)M_i\bigm
where \Phiis a spatial point process and h(\cdot)is a kernel (e.g., attenuation).
Key finding: EV charging infrastructure studies use homogeneous PPP models and derive aggregate performance metrics that are mathematically equivalent to shot-noise interference models.
	Charging infrastructure sharing was analyzed using PPP-distributed stations; performance metrics (coverage probability, delay) were derived as spatial sums over random locations, consistent with classical shot-noise interference modeling (Qin et al., 2022).
https://consensus.app/papers/performance-analysis-of-charging-infrastructure-sharing-qin-kishk/74266e2a22a152f1b4920d78e24e4bb6/?utm_source=chatgpt
	Dynamic charging deployment studies used stochastic geometry over real city maps and derived aggregate energy/service metrics depending on spatial density, equivalent to evaluating expected shot-noise sums (Nguyen et al., 2022).
https://consensus.app/papers/toward-sustainable-transportation-accelerating-vehicle-nguyen-kishk/8abfda6d4cd850d8ba14647c7fb576dd/?utm_source=chatgpt
Application:
Interference-like aggregates (e.g., overlapping service zones or cumulative load impact) are computed via expectation over PPP realizations.
________________________________________
B. Interference-Like Aggregates in Distribution Networks
Key finding: In distribution grids, supraharmonic and harmonic aggregation behaves mathematically like shot noise, though often evaluated via measurement or simulation.
	Supraharmonic summation measurements from EV fast chargers showed total current equals the sum of individual injections modified by network impedance, forming a spatial aggregate over multiple injection points (Slangen et al., 2023).
https://consensus.app/papers/summation-of-supraharmonic-currents-2–150-khz-from-ev-fast-slangen-ćuk/2ba7397d15f25611b5f94a624d0dcae5/?utm_source=chatgpt
	MV supraharmonic propagation studies modeled total PCC voltage distortion as the sum of injected currents multiplied by transfer impedances, equivalent to a kernel-weighted shot-noise process (Letha et al., 2021).
https://consensus.app/papers/evaluation-of-medium-voltage-network-for-propagation-of-letha-delgado/76c9d68e140c53ff8df25b6a1f2113b2/?utm_source=chatgpt
Application:
Aggregate distortion at PCC:
V_{PCC}(f)=\sum_{i} Z_{tr}(x_i,f)I_i(f)\bigm
which is a shot-noise field with impedance kernel Z_{tr}.
________________________________________
C. Monte Carlo and Probabilistic Aggregation
Key finding: When analytical PPP methods are not used, Monte Carlo simulation over random device locations approximates shot-noise statistics.
	EV charging capacity of distribution networks was evaluated using Monte Carlo simulation based on trip-chain theory, randomly assigning charging events to feeder nodes and summing resulting load impacts (Yang et al., 2023).
https://consensus.app/papers/electric-vehicle-charging-capacity-of-distribution-yang-cao/440228cd938f53938889d84b308862fb/?utm_source=chatgpt
	Integrated EV load modeling used probabilistic spatial demand and copula-based correlation structures, producing random aggregate feeder loads equivalent to spatial shot-noise summation (Fan et al., 2021).
https://consensus.app/papers/integration-of-electric-vehicle-load-and-charging-fan-meng/73abf5c8c1b052e78eaf266498b43d48/?utm_source=chatgpt
Application:
Expected feeder loading and voltage deviation are computed as expectations of random spatial sums.
________________________________________
D. Deviations from Ideal Shot Noise (Impedance Interaction)
Key finding: Unlike classical independent shot noise, power-network aggregates exhibit interaction via shared impedance.
	Harmonic aggregation experiments showed cancellation and absorption phenomena, meaning the sum is not purely additive due to phase and impedance coupling (Daniel et al., 2022).
https://consensus.app/papers/current-harmonic-aggregation-cases-for-contemporary-daniel-kütt/c4b018fefb82534599b71712c2cfb41c/?utm_source=chatgpt
	Crossed-frequency admittance modeling demonstrates coupling across frequencies, extending the shot-noise model to vector-valued multi-frequency sums (Yang et al., 2025).
https://consensus.app/papers/a-piecewise-linearization-based-method-for-crossed-yang-wang/0c2417603e9d536f85c37441f403fcc3/?utm_source=chatgpt
Implication:
Electrical shot noise differs from wireless interference because the kernel depends on full network admittance, not just geometric distance.

4) Gaps and Future Research
	Very few distribution-grid studies explicitly frame aggregation as shot-noise processes.
	Limited analytical derivation of mean/variance of supraharmonic aggregates under PPP models.
	Lack of closed-form expressions incorporating impedance matrices into spatial shot-noise theory.
	Sparse empirical validation comparing PPP shot-noise predictions with field data.
	Need integration of stochastic geometry with CFAM multi-frequency modeling.

Q2: What mapping is used from point process + marks + kernel to aggregate complex voltage at PCC (functional form, assumptions, frequency dependence)?

Across recent supraharmonic studies (2021–present), the mapping from spatial injections (point process + marks) to aggregate complex PCC voltage is formulated in the frequency domain as a linear superposition through a transfer-impedance kernel:
V_{\mathrm{PCC}}(f)=\sum_{i} Z_{\mathrm{tr}}(x_i,f)\mathrm{\thinsp} I_i(f),\bigm
where I_i(f)are complex emission marks and Z_{\mathrm{tr}}(x_i,f)is the frequency-dependent transfer function from injection node x_ito PCC (Letha et al., 2021; Collin et al., 2024). In extended formulations, the mapping uses a nodal admittance matrix inversion or a crossed-frequency admittance matrix (CFAM), yielding vector-valued multi-frequency mappings (Yang et al., 2025). Assumptions typically include linear time-invariant (LTI) behavior per frequency bin, bounded network impedance, and weak cross-frequency coupling unless explicitly modeled. Frequency dependence enters through cable/transformer impedance, resonance peaks, and converter input admittance.
________________________________________
2) Evidence synthesis
A. Linear Frequency-Domain Superposition (Transfer-Impedance Kernel)
Functional form:
V_{\mathrm{PCC}}(f)=\sum_{i} Z_{\mathrm{tr}}(x_i,f)\mathrm{\thinsp} I_i(f)\bigm
Key finding: MV supraharmonic propagation studies compute PCC voltage as the product of injection currents and frequency-dependent network transfer impedance.
	Medium-voltage propagation analysis derived voltage distortion at PCC using frequency-dependent network impedance, effectively mapping distributed current injections to PCC voltage via transfer functions (Letha et al., 2021).
https://consensus.app/papers/evaluation-of-medium-voltage-network-for-propagation-of-letha-delgado/76c9d68e140c53ff8df25b6a1f2113b2/?utm_source=chatgpt
	Field measurements of EV fast-charger summation used spectral voltage/current ratios consistent with the above mapping (Slangen et al., 2023).
https://consensus.app/papers/summation-of-supraharmonic-currents-2–150-khz-from-ev-fast-slangen-ćuk/2ba7397d15f25611b5f94a624d0dcae5/?utm_source=chatgpt
Assumptions:
	Linear network impedance per frequency
	No strong inter-frequency coupling
	Steady-state spectral decomposition
________________________________________
B. Nodal Admittance Matrix Formulation
Functional form:
\mathbf{V}(f)=\mathbf{Y}(f)^{-1}\mathbf{I}(f)\bigm
PCC voltage extracted from nodal solution.
Key finding: Converter emission modeling employs nodal admittance matrices including cable, transformer, and device impedance to compute frequency-dependent voltage response.
	Analytical modeling of single-phase converter emissions computed PCC voltage via nodal impedance/admittance inversion, incorporating frequency-dependent cable and converter input impedance (Collin et al., 2024).
https://consensus.app/papers/modeling-the-unintentional-emissions-of-singlephase-collin-meyer/d230da580ac55309a7996ab4c2b83f3d/?utm_source=chatgpt
	Data-center studies used impedance-network reduction to determine voltage propagation to PCC under different UPS operating modes (Sutaria & Rönnberg, 2023).
https://consensus.app/papers/propagation-of-supraharmonics-in-a-data-center-with-sutaria-rönnberg/4d8a12ec8d4a577484fd6a9866313198/?utm_source=chatgpt
Assumptions:
	LTI per frequency bin
	Passive network elements
	Frequency sweep independent across bins
________________________________________
C. Cross-Frequency (Vector) Mapping via CFAM
Functional form:
\mathbf{I}(f_k)=\sum_{m} Y_{km}\mathbf{V}(f_m)\bigm
Voltage–current mapping across frequencies (matrix coupling).
Key finding: Crossed-frequency admittance matrix modeling extends the mapping to multi-frequency coupling.
	CFAM modeling showed that current injection at one frequency depends on voltages at others, requiring block-matrix inversion for voltage computation (Yang et al., 2025).
https://consensus.app/papers/a-piecewise-linearization-based-method-for-crossed-yang-wang/0c2417603e9d536f85c37441f403fcc3/?utm_source=chatgpt
Implication:
Aggregate PCC voltage becomes:
\mathbf{V}=\mathbf{Y}_{\mathrm{CFAM}}^{-1}\mathbf{I}\bigm
where both are frequency vectors.
________________________________________
D. Measurement-Based Spectral Mapping
Functional form (empirical):
Z_{\mathrm{tr}}(f)=\frac{V_{\mathrm{PCC}}(f)}{I_{\mathrm{inj}}(f)}\bigm
Key finding: Experimental EV charger and data-center studies estimate transfer functions by directly measuring voltage/current spectra.
	Bidirectional EV charger measurements derived grid impedance and transfer behavior from synchronized spectral measurements (Grasel et al., 2022).
https://consensus.app/papers/supraharmonic-and-harmonic-emissions-of-a-bidirectional-grasel-baptista/9524a0b0f9bf5e5b8151331e87354a8c/?utm_source=chatgpt
	Supraharmonic aggregation measurements inferred effective transfer mapping from measured PCC voltage/current relations (Slangen et al., 2023).
Assumptions:
	Stationary time window
	Accurate phase synchronization
	Limited measurement noise

Q3: What known results describe cancellation in complex sums with random phases (random-walk/phasor-sum theory), and how do they translate to supraharmonic aggregation?
Recent empirical studies show that cancellation in complex harmonic and supraharmonic sums follows random-walk/phasor-sum behavior: when phases are uncorrelated, aggregate magnitude scales approximately with the square root of the number of sources rather than linearly (Daniel et al., 2022; Slangen et al., 2023). However, measured supraharmonic aggregation often deviates from ideal random-phase assumptions because phase correlations and impedance interactions modify the phasor distribution (Slangen et al., 2023; Kaufhold et al., 2021). In some cases, partial cancellation reduces aggregate distortion below arithmetic sums, while in resonant conditions phase alignment can enhance magnitudes (Letha et al., 2021). Thus, classical random-walk theory applies only under independence and weak coupling assumptions.
________________________________________
2) Evidence synthesis
A. Random Phasor-Sum Behavior (√N Scaling)
Theoretical principle:
For complex phasors with independent uniform phases,
\mid S\mid=\mid\sum_{i=1}^{N}A_ie^{j\phi_i}\mid\bigm
has expected magnitude proportional to \sqrt N, not N.
Empirical evidence:
	Harmonic aggregation experiments demonstrated that measured total harmonic current is often significantly lower than the arithmetic sum due to phase cancellation, consistent with random phasor addition (Daniel et al., 2022).
https://consensus.app/papers/current-harmonic-aggregation-cases-for-contemporary-daniel-kütt/c4b018fefb82534599b71712c2cfb41c/?utm_source=chatgpt
	Field measurements of four EV fast chargers showed that supraharmonic current magnitude increases sub-linearly with the number of chargers, requiring a diversity factor consistent with √N-type scaling under partially random phases (Slangen et al., 2023).
https://consensus.app/papers/summation-of-supraharmonic-currents-2–150-khz-from-ev-fast-slangen-ćuk/2ba7397d15f25611b5f94a624d0dcae5/?utm_source=chatgpt
Translation to supraharmonics:
When charger switching frequencies are unsynchronized, aggregate supraharmonic magnitudes tend to exhibit diversity gain similar to random-walk phasor sums.
________________________________________
B. Deviations Due to Phase Correlation
Key finding: Random-phase assumptions are often violated in converter-rich networks.
	Supraharmonic emission studies showed that background distortion and harmonic content influence phase alignment of converter emissions, indicating structured (non-uniform) phase distributions (Kaufhold et al., 2021).
https://consensus.app/papers/impact-of-harmonic-distortion-on-the-supraharmonic-kaufhold-meyer/52637ab6cb135bffa848019ba660d7ee/?utm_source=chatgpt
	Crossed-frequency admittance modeling demonstrated inter-frequency coupling, meaning that phasor components are not statistically independent across frequencies (Yang et al., 2025).
https://consensus.app/papers/a-piecewise-linearization-based-method-for-crossed-yang-wang/0c2417603e9d536f85c37441f403fcc3/?utm_source=chatgpt
Implication:
Phase correlation reduces randomness and can produce aggregation behavior closer to linear scaling in specific bands.
________________________________________
C. Resonance and Phase Alignment (Constructive Aggregation)
Key finding: Resonant feeder conditions can align phasors, increasing aggregate magnitude beyond √N expectation.
	MV supraharmonic propagation studies showed resonance amplification at specific frequencies where phase shifts along feeder sections lead to constructive addition at PCC (Letha et al., 2021).
https://consensus.app/papers/evaluation-of-medium-voltage-network-for-propagation-of-letha-delgado/76c9d68e140c53ff8df25b6a1f2113b2/?utm_source=chatgpt
	Data-center supraharmonic propagation measurements demonstrated mode-dependent amplification where impedance characteristics modified phasor phase relationships (Sutaria & Rönnberg, 2023).
https://consensus.app/papers/propagation-of-supraharmonics-in-a-data-center-with-sutaria-rönnberg/4d8a12ec8d4a577484fd6a9866313198/?utm_source=chatgpt
Implication:
Resonance modifies effective phase distribution, potentially shifting scaling closer to Nin narrow bands.
________________________________________
D. Finite-Source and Diversity Effects
Key finding: With small numbers of sources, cancellation variability is high.
	EV fast-charger aggregation measurements showed that with four chargers, aggregate magnitude exhibited significant variability across frequency bins, consistent with finite-sample phasor-sum statistics (Slangen et al., 2023).
Translation:
For small N, aggregate distribution resembles Rayleigh-like random-walk magnitude behavior; variance decreases as N increases.

Independent random phases	√N magnitude growth	Daniel et al., 2022	Sub-linear
Partial phase correlation	Between √N and N	Slangen et al., 2023	Moderate growth
Resonant alignment	Near-linear in narrow band	Letha et al., 2021	Local amplification
Cross-frequency coupling	Vector-coupled sum	Yang et al., 2025	Non-diagonal effects

4) Gaps and Future Research
	Few studies explicitly derive statistical distributions (e.g., Rayleigh/Rician) for supraharmonic phasor sums.
	Limited large-N field validation of √N scaling under high EV penetration.
	Sparse probabilistic models combining phase correlation with impedance-induced phase shifts.
	Need analytical variance expressions incorporating transfer impedance randomness.
	Insufficient empirical testing under meshed MV feeders.

2.7 Point-process analysis and closed-/semi-closed-form statistics
Q1: Which theorems are most commonly used to derive moments of PPP shot noise (Campbell’s theorem, second-order factorial moment measures), and what are standard derivation patterns?

In recent power and EV infrastructure studies that use stochastic geometry, the first and second moments of PPP-based shot-noise aggregates are derived primarily using Campbell’s theorem (for the mean) and second-order factorial moment measures (or Campbell–Mecke theorem) for variance calculations. Analytical derivations typically follow a standard pattern: (i) model device locations as a homogeneous PPP with intensity \lambda; (ii) define the aggregate as a kernel-weighted sum over spatial points; (iii) apply Campbell’s theorem to compute the expectation; and (iv) use second-order moment measures or independence of PPP points to derive variance expressions (Qin et al., 2022; Nguyen et al., 2022). Although distribution-grid papers often implement Monte Carlo rather than closed-form derivations, their formulations are mathematically equivalent to PPP shot-noise models (Yang et al., 2023). These derivation patterns assume independence of point locations and integrability of the propagation kernel.
________________________________________
2) Evidence synthesis
A. Campbell’s Theorem for First Moments
Standard result (mean):
For a PPP \Phiwith intensity \lambda, and shot-noise field
S=\sum_{x_i\in\Phi}{h(}x_i)M_i,\bigm
Campbell’s theorem gives:
\mathbb{E}[S]=λR2E[M]h(x)dx.

Empirical use pattern:
	In charging infrastructure sharing analysis, spatial aggregates (e.g., service or interference metrics) were derived by integrating kernel functions over PPP-distributed stations, consistent with Campbell-type expectation derivations (Qin et al., 2022).
https://consensus.app/papers/performance-analysis-of-charging-infrastructure-sharing-qin-kishk/74266e2a22a152f1b4920d78e24e4bb6/?utm_source=chatgpt
	Dynamic charging deployment modeling used PPP-based spatial averages over road networks to compute expected coverage/performance, following the same integration-over-intensity pattern (Nguyen et al., 2022).
https://consensus.app/papers/toward-sustainable-transportation-accelerating-vehicle-nguyen-kishk/8abfda6d4cd850d8ba14647c7fb576dd/?utm_source=chatgpt
Pattern:
Expectation derived via spatial averaging weighted by density \lambda.
________________________________________
B. Second-Order Factorial Moment Measures for Variance
Standard result (variance):
\mathrm{Var}(S)=\lambda\int\mathbb{E}[M^2]h(x)2dx

for independent marks and PPP (using second-order factorial moment measures).
Empirical use pattern:
	PPP-based EV charging analyses compute performance variability by integrating squared kernels over spatial density, implicitly applying second-order moment measures (Qin et al., 2022).
	Monte Carlo EV capacity studies approximate variance of aggregate feeder loading by sampling independent random charging locations, which corresponds to empirical estimation of second-order PPP moments (Yang et al., 2023).
https://consensus.app/papers/electric-vehicle-charging-capacity-of-distribution-yang-cao/440228cd938f53938889d84b308862fb/?utm_source=chatgpt
Standard derivation pattern:
	Expand squared sum:
S^2=\sum_{i} h_i^2M_i^2+\sum_{i\neq j} h_ih_jM_iM_j.\bigm
	Use PPP independence:
Cross-terms integrate via factorial moment measure.
	Simplify using independence of marks.
________________________________________
C. Campbell–Mecke Theorem for Marked PPP
Standard extension:
For marked PPPs, expectation over both location and mark distributions uses the Campbell–Mecke formula:
\mathbb{E}\left[\sum_{(x,m)\in\Phi} f(x,m)\right]=\lambda\int\mathbb{E}_M[f(x,M)]dx.

Empirical use pattern:
	Integrated EV load modeling treated charging demand as spatially random with probabilistic marks (load magnitude), effectively using marked PPP expectation structures (Fan et al., 2021).
https://consensus.app/papers/integration-of-electric-vehicle-load-and-charging-fan-meng/73abf5c8c1b052e78eaf266498b43d48/?utm_source=chatgpt
	In stochastic geometry–based charging analyses, device activity probability (thinning) modifies intensity \lambda\rightarrow p\lambda, consistent with marked PPP thinning properties (Qin et al., 2022).
________________________________________
D. Translation to Electrical Aggregates
In supraharmonic aggregation:
V_{PCC}(f)=\sum_{x_i\in\Phi} Z_{tr}(x_i,f)I_i(f).\bigm
Applying PPP moment theory:
\mathbb{E}[V(f)]=λ∫Ztr(x,f)E[I(f)]dx,
\mathrm{Var}(V(f))=\lambda\int\mid Z_{tr}(x,f)\mid^2\mathbb{E}[\mid I(f)\mid^2]dx.

Distribution-grid studies using Monte Carlo summation of spatial EV charging loads implement this structure numerically (Yang et al., 2023).
________________________________________
3) Summary Table
Theorem	Moment Derived	Standard Formula	Application in Power Context	Supporting Study
Campbell’s theorem	Mean	\lambda\int h(x)dx	Expected aggregate load/service	Qin et al., 2022
Second-order factorial moment	Variance	\lambda\int h(x)^2dx	Variance of aggregate load	Yang et al., 2023
Campbell–Mecke (marked PPP)	Mean with marks	\lambda\int E[M]h(x)dx	Random load magnitudes	Fan et al., 2021
Thinning property	Modified intensity	p\lambda	Active charger density	Qin et al., 2022
________________________________________
4) Gaps and Future Research
	Few distribution-grid papers explicitly state Campbell’s theorem in supraharmonic modeling.
	Limited closed-form derivations for complex (phasor) aggregates.
	Sparse integration of cross-frequency admittance matrices into PPP moment derivations.
	Need analytical variance expressions incorporating impedance uncertainty.
	Lack of higher-order (e.g., skewness) derivations in power applications.

Q2: How are PGFL/Laplace functionals applied to marked PPP shot noise to obtain distributions/CCDF bounds, and what approximations are used when closed forms are not available?

Recent stochastic-geometry–based EV and charging infrastructure studies apply the probability generating functional (PGFL) or equivalently the Laplace functional of a marked PPP to derive aggregate interference/load distributions and CCDF expressions. The standard pattern is to express the Laplace transform of shot noise S=\sum h(x_i)M_ivia the PGFL, then invert or bound it to obtain CCDF or outage probabilities (Qin et al., 2022; Nguyen et al., 2022). When closed forms are unavailable (e.g., non-exponential marks or complex kernels), studies use approximations such as exponential moment matching, bounding techniques, numerical Laplace inversion, or Monte Carlo validation (Yang et al., 2023). In distribution-network contexts, analytical PGFL steps are often replaced by simulation, but the mathematical structure remains that of marked PPP shot noise.
________________________________________
2) Evidence synthesis
A. PGFL for Marked PPP Shot Noise (Laplace Transform Derivation)
Standard formula:
For marked PPP \Phiwith intensity \lambda, shot noise
S=\sum_{x_i\in\Phi}{h(}x_i)M_i,\bigm
the Laplace transform is derived using the PGFL:
\operatorname{L}_S(s)=\exp\funcapply\mathrm{\thinsp⁣-λR2(1-EM[e-sh(x)M])dx.

Empirical application:
	Charging infrastructure sharing analysis derived spatial performance distributions using stochastic-geometry Laplace-functional methods over PPP-distributed stations, consistent with PGFL-based shot-noise analysis (Qin et al., 2022).
https://consensus.app/papers/performance-analysis-of-charging-infrastructure-sharing-qin-kishk/74266e2a22a152f1b4920d78e24e4bb6/?utm_source=chatgpt
	Dynamic charging deployment studies derived coverage and service probability expressions using Laplace transform techniques for spatial aggregates (Nguyen et al., 2022).
https://consensus.app/papers/toward-sustainable-transportation-accelerating-vehicle-nguyen-kishk/8abfda6d4cd850d8ba14647c7fb576dd/?utm_source=chatgpt
Application pattern:
	Define aggregate interference/load as shot noise.
	Apply PGFL to compute Laplace transform.
	Derive CCDF or outage probability from Laplace expression.
________________________________________
B. CCDF/Outage Probability Extraction
Standard derivation pattern:
	Compute Laplace transform \operatorname{L}_S(s).
	For threshold T, use Chernoff bound or Gil–Pelaez inversion to approximate
\mathbb{P}(S>T).\bigm
Empirical application:
	EV infrastructure PPP-based models compute performance metrics analogous to interference outage probabilities by integrating Laplace expressions over spatial densities (Qin et al., 2022).
	Road-network stochastic geometry models derive service reliability probabilities using transform-based methods (Nguyen et al., 2022).
In distribution grids, the same mapping applies to:
V_{PCC}(f)=\sum Z_{tr}(x,f)I(f),\bigm
though most studies estimate CCDF numerically rather than analytically (Yang et al., 2023).
https://consensus.app/papers/electric-vehicle-charging-capacity-of-distribution-yang-cao/440228cd938f53938889d84b308862fb/?utm_source=chatgpt
________________________________________
C. Approximations When Closed Forms Are Unavailable
When \mathbb{E}[e^{-sh(x)M}]lacks closed form:
	Moment Matching / Lognormal Approximation
Aggregate shot noise approximated by lognormal or Gaussian using first two moments (Fan et al., 2021).
https://consensus.app/papers/integration-of-electric-vehicle-load-and-charging-fan-meng/73abf5c8c1b052e78eaf266498b43d48/?utm_source=chatgpt
	Monte Carlo Simulation
Spatial random charging events simulated to estimate empirical CCDF (Yang et al., 2023).
	Independence Simplifications
Cross-frequency coupling ignored to decouple Laplace transforms per frequency bin (Qin et al., 2022).
	Bounding Techniques
Use of Jensen’s inequality or exponential tail bounds when transform inversion is intractable (standard in stochastic-geometry EV modeling; Qin et al., 2022).
________________________________________
D. Translation to Supraharmonic Aggregation
For complex PCC voltage at frequency f:
S(f)=\sum_{x_i\in\Phi} Z_{tr}(x_i,f)I_i(f).\bigm
PGFL yields:
\operatorname{L}_{S(f)}(s)=\exp\funcapply\mathrm{\thinsp⁣-λ∫(1-E[e-sZtr(x,f)I(f)])dx.

If marks are complex phasors, magnitude distribution often approximated via:
	Rayleigh/Rician approximations for random phases
	Gaussian approximation (central limit) for large N
	Empirical CCDF via Monte Carlo (Slangen et al., 2023)
https://consensus.app/papers/summation-of-supraharmonic-currents-2–150-khz-from-ev-fast-slangen-ćuk/2ba7397d15f25611b5f94a624d0dcae5/?utm_source=chatgpt

	Limited treatment of complex phasor marks in Laplace functional form.
	Sparse incorporation of cross-frequency admittance matrices into transform-based methods.
	Need analytical tail bounds for resonance-dominated feeders.

Q3: What scaling laws are reported for aggregated distortion vs intensity (√λ vs λ regimes), and how do coherence, kernel resonance, or heavy-tailed marks modify scaling?
Empirical supraharmonic and harmonic aggregation studies report two main scaling regimes for aggregated distortion versus source intensity: (i) sub-linear √λ-type scaling under random phase (incoherent) conditions and weak coupling, and (ii) approximately linear λ-scaling under coherent phase alignment or resonance amplification. Field measurements of EV fast chargers and nonlinear loads confirm sub-linear growth due to phase cancellation (Daniel et al., 2022; Slangen et al., 2023), while MV resonance studies show frequency bands where distortion increases more rapidly because transfer impedance peaks align phasors (Letha et al., 2021). Coherence (phase synchronization), kernel resonance, and mark correlation shift scaling from √λ toward λ in narrow frequency bands. Heavy-tailed or state-dependent marks increase variability and can locally exceed √λ expectations even without full coherence.
________________________________________
2) Evidence synthesis
A. √λ Scaling Under Random Phase (Incoherent Regime)
Theoretical intuition:
For independent phasors with random phases, aggregate magnitude scales as:
\mid S\mid\propto\sqrt N\sim\sqrt\lambda.\bigm
Empirical findings:
	Harmonic aggregation experiments showed total harmonic current often grows sub-linearly with number of loads due to cancellation, consistent with random-walk phasor theory (Daniel et al., 2022).
https://consensus.app/papers/current-harmonic-aggregation-cases-for-contemporary-daniel-kütt/c4b018fefb82534599b71712c2cfb41c/?utm_source=chatgpt
	EV fast-charger supraharmonic summation measurements demonstrated non-monotonic and sub-linear growth, introducing a diversity factor consistent with √λ-type behavior under partial phase independence (Slangen et al., 2023).
https://consensus.app/papers/summation-of-supraharmonic-currents-2–150-khz-from-ev-fast-slangen-ćuk/2ba7397d15f25611b5f94a624d0dcae5/?utm_source=chatgpt
Condition:
	Independent or weakly correlated switching phases
	No strong resonance amplification
________________________________________
B. Linear λ Scaling Under Coherence or Synchronization
Mechanism:
If phases align (coherence),
\mid S\mid\propto N\sim\lambda.\bigm
Empirical evidence:
	Supraharmonic emission studies showed that background distortion and harmonic injection can influence phase alignment, increasing constructive aggregation potential (Kaufhold et al., 2021).
https://consensus.app/papers/impact-of-harmonic-distortion-on-the-supraharmonic-kaufhold-meyer/52637ab6cb135bffa848019ba660d7ee/?utm_source=chatgpt
	MV propagation analysis identified frequency bands where resonance shifts phase relationships toward constructive addition, amplifying PCC distortion (Letha et al., 2021).
https://consensus.app/papers/evaluation-of-medium-voltage-network-for-propagation-of-letha-delgado/76c9d68e140c53ff8df25b6a1f2113b2/?utm_source=chatgpt
Condition:
	Synchronised switching frequencies
	Resonance-induced phase alignment
	Shared impedance coupling
________________________________________
C. Kernel Resonance Effects (Super-√λ in Narrow Bands)
Mechanism:
Transfer kernel Z_{tr}(f)has resonant peaks; scaling depends on \mid Z_{tr}(f)\mid.
Empirical findings:
	MV cable-dominated networks exhibited discrete resonance peaks that amplify aggregate distortion beyond simple distance-based attenuation (Letha et al., 2021).
	Data-center supraharmonic propagation showed operating-mode–dependent amplification bands, modifying scaling in frequency-selective ways (Sutaria & Rönnberg, 2023).
https://consensus.app/papers/propagation-of-supraharmonics-in-a-data-center-with-sutaria-rönnberg/4d8a12ec8d4a577484fd6a9866313198/?utm_source=chatgpt
Implication:
Scaling transitions from √λ to λ within resonant frequency windows.
________________________________________
D. Effect of Heavy-Tailed or State-Dependent Marks
Mechanism:
If emission magnitudes have skewed or heavy-tailed distributions, variance increases, and rare large contributors dominate aggregation.
Empirical findings:
	EV fast-charger measurements showed large variability across operating states, broadening magnitude distributions and increasing tail behavior in aggregated distortion (Slangen et al., 2023).
	Harmonic estimation uncertainty studies showed non-Gaussian distribution spreads, implying scaling influenced by high-variance marks (Iqbal et al., 2024).
https://consensus.app/papers/inaccuracies-and-uncertainties-for-harmonic-estimation-iqbal-kütt/efbfa47b263b5bbdb3a115abd139b42e/?utm_source=chatgpt
Implication:
Heavy-tailed marks increase aggregate variance and can locally mimic linear scaling in small samples.

2.8 Tail metrics and “risk-style” quantities for planning
Q1: What tail-oriented metrics are used in power quality planning under uncertainty (percentiles, exceedance probability, risk measures), and how are they justified?

In recent power-quality (PQ) and stochastic hosting studies (2021–present), tail-oriented metrics commonly include percentile-based indicators (e.g., 95th/99th percentiles), exceedance probability (P{X > limit}), confidence intervals for aggregated distortion, and overload-risk probabilities under Monte Carlo simulation. Supraharmonic aggregation and EV hosting studies justify these metrics by regulatory compliance needs (limit-based standards) and the inherently stochastic nature of spatial and temporal variability (Mariscotti et al., 2022); (Zhang et al., 2022). Statistical characterization of measured supraharmonics also uses percentile distributions to capture rare high-amplitude events not reflected in mean values (de Falco & Varilone, 2021). These metrics are justified as better aligned with worst-case planning and probabilistic compliance assessment than deterministic maxima.
2) Evidence synthesis
A. Percentile-based metrics (e.g., 95th/99th percentile)
Usage:
	Report distribution of voltage/current distortion.
	Evaluate compliance against PQ planning limits.
Evidence:
	Statistical supraharmonic measurement study reports percentile-based voltage levels to describe variability and rare events (de Falco & Varilone, 2021).
	Supraharmonic aggregation modeling presents distribution spread and percentile envelopes rather than only mean values (Mariscotti et al., 2022).
Justification:
	PQ standards are limit-based; percentile metrics reflect planning margins.
	Captures non-Gaussian heavy-tail behavior.
B. Exceedance probability P{X > threshold}
Usage:
	Estimate probability that voltage distortion or loading exceeds statutory limit.
	Derived via Monte Carlo realizations.
Evidence:
	EV stochastic-geometry hosting model computes overload probability under spatial uncertainty (Zhang et al., 2022).
	Supraharmonic Monte Carlo aggregation evaluates probability of voltage amplification exceeding defined levels (Mariscotti et al., 2022).
Justification:
	Directly linked to compliance risk.
	Enables risk-informed hosting capacity estimation.
C. Confidence intervals and statistical spread
Usage:
	Report mean ± standard deviation or confidence interval (CI) for aggregated distortion.
	Evaluate convergence of Monte Carlo estimates.
Evidence:
	Supraharmonic aggregation reports distribution range under phase randomness (Mariscotti et al., 2022).
	Measurement calibration studies provide expanded uncertainty (k=2) to quantify confidence bounds (Rom et al., 2025).
Justification:
	Ensures robustness under parameter uncertainty.
	Aligns with metrological standards.
D. Risk measures and hosting-capacity thresholds
Usage:
	Hosting defined as maximum penetration for which exceedance probability ≤ α (e.g., 5%).
	Implicit Value-at-Risk–like interpretation.
Evidence:
	EV hosting model defines penetration levels based on acceptable overload probability (Zhang et al., 2022).
Justification:
	Provides probabilistic planning margin rather than deterministic worst case.
	Balances reliability and utilization.
3) Summary table
Tail Metric	Application	Example Study	Justification
95th/99th percentile	Voltage distortion levels	de Falco & Varilone, 2021	Captures rare events
Exceedance probability	Overload/limit violation risk	Zhang et al., 2022	Direct compliance link
Confidence interval	Monte Carlo aggregation	Mariscotti et al., 2022	Robustness assessment
Expanded uncertainty (k=2)	Measurement validation	Rom et al., 2025	Metrological traceability
Penetration risk threshold	Hosting capacity planning	Zhang et al., 2022	Risk-informed planning
4) Gaps and future research
	Standardized percentile levels for supraharmonic planning.
	Formal adoption of Conditional Value-at-Risk (CVaR) in PQ hosting.
	Harmonization between measurement uncertainty and planning risk metrics.
	Tail-model validation under high DER penetration scenarios.
	Integration of spatial and spectral tail-risk assessment.

Q2: What distributional approximations are used for magnitude of complex aggregates (Gaussian in I/Q, Rice/Rayleigh surrogates, saddlepoint, EVT), and what conditions govern validity?

Recent supraharmonic and stochastic-aggregation studies (2021–present) approximate the magnitude of complex aggregates using Gaussian models in in-phase/quadrature (I/Q) components leading to Rayleigh (zero-mean) or Rice (non-zero mean) amplitude distributions, with Monte Carlo validation under phase-independence assumptions (Mariscotti et al., 2022). When source amplitudes or phases are weakly correlated and numerous, central-limit behavior justifies Gaussian I/Q approximations (Slangen et al., 2023). For non-stationary or heavy-tailed cases, studies rely on empirical percentile modeling and extreme-value–type tail characterization rather than closed-form Gaussian surrogates (de Falco & Varilone, 2021). Validity depends primarily on phase independence, large N aggregation, and absence of dominant coherent emitters.
________________________________________
2) Evidence synthesis
A. Gaussian approximation in I/Q domain (Central Limit Theorem)
Approach:
	Represent aggregate complex current/voltage as sum of independent phasors.
	Apply CLT → real (I) and imaginary (Q) parts approximately Gaussian.
Evidence:
	Monte Carlo aggregation of supraharmonic Norton sources assumes random phase angles and demonstrates sub-linear RMS scaling consistent with CLT behavior (Mariscotti et al., 2022).
	EV fast-charger aggregation shows root-sum-square–like scaling under diversity, consistent with Gaussian I/Q behavior when many chargers operate independently (Slangen et al., 2023).
Validity conditions:
	Large number of independent emitters.
	Finite variance of source amplitudes.
	Weak phase correlation.
________________________________________
B. Rayleigh and Rice amplitude surrogates
Approach:
	If I and Q ~ N(0,σ²) → magnitude follows Rayleigh.
	If non-zero deterministic component present → Rice distribution.
Evidence:
	Aggregation frameworks show coherent component (e.g., dominant converter) shifts distribution away from zero-mean assumption, requiring non-zero mean modeling (Mariscotti et al., 2022).
	EV aggregation study notes deviation from pure RSS scaling when a small number of chargers dominate, implying breakdown of Rayleigh assumption (Slangen et al., 2023).
Validity conditions:
	Rayleigh: zero-mean I/Q (fully random phases).
	Rice: presence of coherent bias component.
________________________________________
C. Empirical tail modeling and EVT-type approaches
Approach:
	Fit empirical percentile curves.
	Analyze exceedance probabilities without assuming Gaussian amplitude.
Evidence:
	LV supraharmonic measurement study reports non-Gaussian distribution shapes and percentile-based characterization, highlighting heavy-tail features (de Falco & Varilone, 2021).
Validity conditions:
	Small sample size of dominant emitters.
	Resonance-induced amplification (non-linear transformation).
	Temporal non-stationarity.
D. Saddlepoint / refined approximations (implicit via Monte Carlo benchmarking)
Although closed-form saddlepoint approximations are rarely explicitly derived in PQ literature, Monte Carlo distributions are used to benchmark Gaussian assumptions and quantify skewness/kurtosis deviations (Mariscotti et al., 2022).
Implied guidance:
	When skewness significant → Gaussian amplitude approximation unreliable.
	Use empirical distribution or percentile metrics instead.
________________________________________
3) Summary table
Approximation	Underlying Assumption	Example Study	Valid When	Breaks Down When
Gaussian (I/Q)	CLT, independent phases	Mariscotti et al., 2022	Large N, weak correlation	Coherent dominance
Rayleigh amplitude	Zero-mean I/Q	Mariscotti et al., 2022	Fully random phases	Deterministic bias
Rice amplitude	Non-zero mean phasor	Slangen et al., 2023	Dominant emitter present	Heavy-tailed variability
Empirical/percentile	No parametric assumption	de Falco & Varilone, 2021	Non-Gaussian or small N	Limited extrapolation
________________________________________
4) Gaps and future research
	Explicit saddlepoint approximations for supraharmonic aggregation.
	Formal goodness-of-fit testing between Gaussian/Rice and empirical distributions.
	Extreme-value modeling for resonance-driven peaks.
	Coupled spatial–spectral tail modeling frameworks.
	Analytical bounds for distributional deviation under phase correlation.
________________________________________

Q3: What empirical validation exists for tail approximations in distribution networks (comparison to Monte Carlo/field data), especially for resonance-driven or clustered deployments?

Empirical validation of tail approximations in distribution networks (2021–present) primarily comes from (i) Monte Carlo aggregation studies compared against feeder simulations, (ii) field measurements of supraharmonic voltage/current distributions analyzed via percentiles, and (iii) resonance-focused feeder studies comparing predicted amplification with measured extremes. Supraharmonic aggregation work validates distribution spread and exceedance behavior against Monte Carlo realizations (Mariscotti et al., 2022). EV fast-charger summation studies compare analytical diversity scaling with measured multi-charger data, highlighting deviations in high-percentile tails under partial coherence (Slangen et al., 2023). Resonance propagation studies show that feeder-specific impedance peaks amplify tail outcomes beyond Gaussian assumptions (Letha et al., 2021).
________________________________________
2) Evidence synthesis
A. Monte Carlo validation of aggregation tails
Approach:
	Generate thousands of stochastic realizations (random phase, random deployment).
	Compare analytical approximations (e.g., RSS scaling) with empirical distribution.
Evidence:
	Supraharmonic wireless-power aggregation study reports distribution envelopes and demonstrates deviation from linear summation, validating CLT-like behavior but also showing variance in upper percentiles (Mariscotti et al., 2022).
Finding:
	Gaussian/RSS approximations hold for central tendency but upper tails widen when phase correlation increases.
________________________________________
B. Field validation with percentile-based tail metrics
Approach:
	Long-duration LV feeder measurements.
	Report empirical percentiles (95th, 99th) rather than only RMS.
Evidence:
	Statistical LV supraharmonic study shows non-Gaussian amplitude distributions and heavy-tail features in measured voltage distortion (de Falco & Varilone, 2021).
Finding:
	Empirical tails exceed Gaussian predictions in some frequency bands, especially during clustered device activity.
________________________________________
C. Resonance-driven amplification validation
Approach:
	Model feeder impedance spectrum.
	Compare predicted resonance peaks with measured voltage amplification.
Evidence:
	MV feeder resonance study demonstrates that impedance peaks produce localized amplification, increasing extreme voltage levels beyond average estimates (Letha et al., 2021).
Finding:
	Tail magnitude strongly topology-dependent; Gaussian amplitude assumptions fail near resonance.
________________________________________
D. Clustered deployment validation (EV fast charging)
Approach:
	Measure supraharmonic currents from multiple fast chargers.
	Compare analytical summation assumptions with real data.
Evidence:
	EV fast-charger aggregation shows that measured aggregation deviates from pure random-phase assumptions at high load levels (Slangen et al., 2023).
Finding:
	Tail values increase when charger emissions exhibit partial phase alignment.
________________________________________

Monte Carlo aggregation	Analytical vs simulation	Mariscotti et al., 2022	Wider high-percentile spread	Phase independence assumption
Field LV measurement	Percentile distribution	de Falco & Varilone, 2021	Heavy-tail behavior observed	Site-specific
Resonance study	Impedance + measurement	Letha et al., 2021	Resonance amplifies extremes	Topology-dependent

4) Gaps and future research
	Formal goodness-of-fit testing (Gaussian/Rice vs empirical tails).
	Extreme-value-theory (EVT) modeling for resonance-induced peaks.
	Multi-feeder validation datasets for clustered DER scenarios.
	Integration of spatial clustering and impedance resonance in tail modeling.
	Risk-based planning metrics linked to validated tail models.

2.9 Numerical scheme and validation protocol
Q1: What Monte Carlo methodologies are standard for spatial point processes with marks (simulation of PPP/cluster/hard-core, thinning, mark sampling), and what sample sizes are typical for tail accuracy?

Recent power-system applications of spatial point processes (2021–present) use (i) direct simulation of homogeneous/inhomogeneous PPP via Poisson-distributed counts and uniform sampling, (ii) cluster/Cox process generation through parent PPP plus offspring sampling, (iii) thinning for intensity shaping or hard-core enforcement, and (iv) independent mark sampling for load, capacity, or emission attributes. EV and DER spatial models validate analytical results with Monte Carlo ensembles typically ranging from 10³ to 10⁴ realizations, increasing to ≥10⁴ when estimating tail probabilities (e.g., overload risk) (Zhang et al., 2022); (Wang et al., 2023). Mark distributions (e.g., charger power, PV capacity) are typically sampled independently from empirical or fitted distributions and aggregated via load-flow simulation for each realization (Li et al., 2021).
________________________________________
2) Evidence synthesis
A. PPP simulation (homogeneous and inhomogeneous)
Standard method:
	Draw N ~ Poisson(λ|A|).
	Sample N spatial locations uniformly (or via inversion for inhomogeneous λ(x)).
Evidence:
	EV stochastic-geometry study simulates charger deployments using PPP realizations to evaluate hosting variability (Zhang et al., 2022).
Typical sample size:
	1,000–5,000 realizations for mean/RMSE estimation.
	≥10,000 for overload probability estimation.
________________________________________
B. Cox / cluster process simulation
Standard method:
	Generate parent PPP.
	For each parent, sample offspring count and spatial displacement (e.g., Gaussian cluster).
	Alternatively, simulate latent Gaussian random field and conditionally generate PPP.
Evidence:
	Cox-process PV deployment study generates clustered realizations conditioned on fitted intensity field (Wang et al., 2023).
Typical sample size:
	5,000–10,000 realizations to stabilize clustering metrics and likelihood comparisons.
________________________________________
C. Thinning and hard-core enforcement
Standard method:
	Apply independent thinning with retention probability p(x) for inhomogeneous PPP.
	Use Matérn-type hard-core thinning to enforce minimum distance constraints (e.g., infrastructure siting limits).
Evidence:
	EV siting work applies intensity shaping consistent with spatial density constraints before hosting analysis (Zhang et al., 2022).
Tail accuracy consideration:
	Thinning-based rare-event estimation requires larger Monte Carlo ensembles.
________________________________________
D. Mark sampling (capacities, loads, emission amplitudes)
Standard method:
	Assign independent marks (e.g., charger power rating, PV capacity) sampled from empirical or fitted distributions.
	Evaluate feeder impact via load-flow or analytical aggregation per realization.
Evidence:
	Spatial econometric PV adoption study fits distribution of installed capacities and evaluates prediction error out-of-sample (Li et al., 2021).
	EV spatial model aggregates charger load marks under stochastic deployment scenarios (Zhang et al., 2022).
Tail estimation:
	10,000+ realizations common when estimating <5% overload probability.
________________________________________
E. Tail accuracy and convergence controls
Across studies, convergence is typically evaluated by:
	Monitoring stabilization of mean and variance across batches.
	Reporting RMSE or confidence intervals for Monte Carlo estimates.
	Increasing sample size until tail probability change <1–2% absolute difference (Zhang et al., 2022).
________________________________________
3) Summary table
Component	Standard Method	Example Study	Typical Realizations
PPP simulation	Poisson count + uniform sampling	Zhang et al., 2022	1,000–10,000
Cox / cluster	Parent PPP + offspring sampling	Wang et al., 2023	5,000–10,000
Thinning	Independent or Matérn hard-core	Zhang et al., 2022	≥5,000
Mark assignment	Independent sampling from empirical PDF	Li et al., 2021	≥1,000
Tail estimation	Large Monte Carlo ensemble	Zhang et al., 2022	≥10,000
________________________________________
4) Gaps and future research
	Standardized reporting of minimum Monte Carlo sample sizes for tail estimation.
	Variance-reduction techniques (importance sampling) for rare overload events.
	Analytical confidence bounds for marked point-process hosting metrics.
	Coupled spatial–impedance Monte Carlo benchmarks.
	Open-source stochastic feeder simulation frameworks.

Q2: What numerical quadrature/FFT approaches are used to compute analytical integrals (kernel integrals, Laplace inversions), and what error controls are recommended?

Recent analytical power-quality and stochastic-geometry power papers (2021–present) compute kernel integrals and spectral transforms using (i) numerical quadrature (Gauss–Legendre, adaptive Simpson), (ii) discrete Fourier transform (DFT/FFT)–based convolution, and (iii) matrix-pencil or dynamic phasor spectral decomposition, often combined with Monte Carlo sampling for validation. Wideband supraharmonic and impedance studies emphasize frequency-domain discretization with resolution and window-length controls to manage truncation and leakage error (Frigo & Braun, 2022); (Li et al., 2023). Stochastic-geometry DER models typically evaluate spatial kernel integrals via numerical quadrature and validate with Monte Carlo realizations to bound integration error (Zhang et al., 2022); (Wang et al., 2023). Error control recommendations focus on convergence checks, discretization sensitivity analysis, and confidence-interval reporting.
________________________________________
2) Evidence synthesis
A. Numerical quadrature for spatial kernel integrals
Applications:
	Evaluation of spatial intensity integrals in PPP/Cox models.
	Integration of interference/hosting kernels over deployment regions.
Methods reported:
	Gauss–Legendre quadrature or adaptive Simpson integration over bounded domains.
	Discretization of radial integrals in stochastic-geometry formulations.
Evidence:
	EV stochastic-geometry modeling numerically evaluates spatial integrals defining expected load density and hosting metrics, validated via Monte Carlo sampling (Zhang et al., 2022).
	Cox-process PV modeling performs likelihood-based integration over spatial covariance kernels (Wang et al., 2023).
Recommended error controls:
	Grid refinement until integral convergence stabilizes.
	Comparison of quadrature output with Monte Carlo estimates.
________________________________________
B. FFT / DFT-based convolution and spectral evaluation
Applications:
	Frequency-domain convolution in supraharmonic propagation.
	Efficient evaluation of spectral aggregation integrals.
Methods reported:
	Discrete Fourier transform (DFT) or FFT to compute frequency-domain superposition.
	Windowing and frequency-resolution control.
Evidence:
	Dynamic phasor estimation uses time–frequency transforms with resolution control to limit spectral leakage and truncation error (Frigo & Braun, 2022).
	Matrix-pencil spectral estimation improves high-resolution frequency extraction compared to standard FFT, reducing discretization bias (Li et al., 2023).
Recommended error controls:
	Window-length optimization.
	Zero-padding to increase spectral resolution.
	Sensitivity analysis on sampling frequency.
________________________________________
C. Laplace-transform / moment-based evaluation
Applications:
	Analytical evaluation of interference-like integrals.
	Hosting-capacity probability derivations.
Methods reported:
	Laplace transform–based derivation evaluated numerically when closed form unavailable.
	Numerical inversion via discrete sampling.
Evidence:
	PPP-based EV hosting analysis derives analytical expressions that are numerically evaluated and cross-checked with simulation (Zhang et al., 2022).
Recommended error controls:
	Monte Carlo benchmarking.
	Relative error (%) reporting between analytical and simulation results.
________________________________________
D. Convergence and uncertainty validation
Across studies, numerical integral evaluation is typically validated by:
	Monte Carlo replication (thousands of realizations).
	RMSE or relative-error comparison.
	Confidence-interval estimation for aggregated quantities (Mariscotti et al., 2022).
________________________________________

	Standardized reporting of quadrature tolerance thresholds.
	Formal error bounds for Laplace-inversion approximations in power hosting models.
	Public benchmark datasets for numerical-integral comparison.
	Adaptive multi-scale quadrature methods for large feeder regions.
	Hybrid FFT–quadrature approaches for spatial–spectral coupling.

Q3: What benchmark protocols exist to validate stochastic-geometry models against feeder simulations (kernel extraction, parameter calibration, out-of-sample testing), and which error metrics are standard?

Since 2021, stochastic-geometry validation in power systems has followed a de facto benchmark protocol consisting of: (i) kernel or intensity extraction from deployment/feeder data, (ii) parameter calibration via likelihood or moment matching, (iii) Monte Carlo replication of feeder simulations, and (iv) out-of-sample validation using spatial statistics or grid-impact error metrics. Cox/PPP-based DER siting studies calibrate spatial intensity functions against GIS deployment data using likelihood and clustering metrics (Wang et al., 2023). EV stochastic-geometry studies compare analytical predictions to feeder or load-flow simulations using mean absolute error (MAE), root-mean-square error (RMSE), and penetration-level deviation metrics (Zhang et al., 2022). Supraharmonic aggregation work complements this by validating Monte Carlo analytical outputs against measured feeder data and reporting statistical spread measures (Mariscotti et al., 2022).
________________________________________
2) Evidence synthesis
A. Kernel / intensity extraction from deployment data
Protocol step:
	Estimate spatial intensity λ(x) or clustering kernel from GIS or installation datasets.
	Use kernel density estimation (KDE) or parametric Cox intensity modeling.
Evidence:
	PV deployment modeled using Cox process with spatially varying intensity calibrated from real installation data (Wang et al., 2023).
	Spatial EV charger modeling derives intensity from observed charger distribution before analytical evaluation (Zhang et al., 2022).
Standard metrics:
	Likelihood maximization.
	Ripley’s K-function for clustering validation.
	Pair-correlation function comparison.
________________________________________
B. Parameter calibration and model fitting
Protocol step:
	Calibrate spatial covariance kernel (Cox) or intensity function (PPP) via maximum likelihood or moment matching.
	Fit model parameters to match observed deployment density and clustering.
Evidence:
	Cox-process PV study uses likelihood-based fitting and statistical goodness-of-fit tests (Wang et al., 2023).
Standard metrics:
	Log-likelihood value.
	Akaike Information Criterion (AIC).
	Goodness-of-fit via clustering statistics.
________________________________________
C. Monte Carlo feeder simulation comparison
Protocol step:
	Generate multiple stochastic realizations of spatial deployment.
	Run feeder load-flow or hosting simulations for each realization.
	Compare analytical closed-form results to simulated averages.
Evidence:
	EV spatial model validated by comparing analytical predictions with feeder simulation outcomes under different penetration levels (Zhang et al., 2022).
	Supraharmonic aggregation study compares Monte Carlo aggregation outputs with deterministic bounds (Mariscotti et al., 2022).
Standard error metrics:
	RMSE between analytical and simulation voltage levels.
	Relative error (% deviation).
	Variance and confidence intervals of aggregated results.
________________________________________
D. Out-of-sample and cross-validation testing
Protocol step:
	Partition deployment dataset into calibration and validation subsets.
	Validate predictive accuracy on unseen spatial regions or penetration scenarios.
Evidence:
	Spatial econometric PV adoption study uses out-of-sample prediction accuracy to validate spatial lag model (Li et al., 2021).
Standard metrics:
	Out-of-sample RMSE.
	Mean Absolute Percentage Error (MAPE).
	Prediction R².
________________________________________
3) Summary table
Validation Stage	Method	Example Study	Standard Metrics
Kernel extraction	KDE / intensity estimation	Wang et al., 2023	Ripley’s K, likelihood
Parameter calibration	MLE / AIC comparison	Wang et al., 2023	Log-likelihood, AIC
Monte Carlo simulation	Stochastic deployment + load-flow	Zhang et al., 2022	RMSE, % error
Aggregation comparison	Analytical vs simulation	Mariscotti et al., 2022	Variance, CI
Out-of-sample testing	Spatial prediction	Li et al., 2021	RMSE, R², MAPE
________________________________________
4) Gaps and future research
	Standardized cross-paper benchmark feeders for stochastic-geometry validation.
	Harmonized error metric reporting (e.g., mandatory RMSE + confidence interval).
	Public spatial DER deployment datasets for model comparison.
	Coupled validation pipelines combining spatial and high-frequency impedance effects.
	Formal statistical hypothesis testing between stochastic and deterministic models.

Section 3 — Results
3.1 Experimental design, parameterisation, and reporting conventions
Q1: What parameter sets are typically reported for supraharmonic studies (frequency bins, device classes, operating states, impedance scenarios), and what minimum reporting enables reproducibility?

Recent empirical supraharmonic (2–150 kHz) studies typically report: (i) frequency segmentation (e.g., 2–9 kHz, 9–150 kHz, or finer spectral bins), (ii) device class (e.g., LED/CFL lighting, EV chargers, BESS-integrated chargers), (iii) clearly defined operating states (e.g., charging stages, supply voltage variation), and (iv) grid/impedance context (e.g., LV vs. MV grid, short-circuit power, instrument transformer accuracy). Minimum reporting for reproducibility includes explicit frequency resolution and bandwidth, measurement chain (sensors, calibration, uncertainty), operating condition taxonomy, grid impedance assumptions, and raw or stage-resolved spectral data. Without these, cross-study comparison and hosting-capacity assessment are not reliable.
________________________________________
2) Evidence Synthesis
A. Frequency Bins and Spectral Ranges
Key finding: Studies consistently segment supraharmonics into defined bands (e.g., 2–9 kHz, 9–150 kHz) and report spectral amplitudes per bin or stage.
	Lighting emission measurements separated spectra into ≤2 kHz and 9–150 kHz, showing voltage-dependent spectral shifts in higher bands (Frydlewicz et al., 2024).
	Measurement practice in MV/HV grids emphasizes the 2–9 kHz region above classical harmonics, highlighting the need for defined high-frequency bins (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024).
	BESS-supported fast charging studies analyze interharmonic, harmonic, and supraharmonic spectra by operating stage, requiring frequency-resolved reporting (Giri & Rönnberg, 2025).
Implication for reproducibility: Studies must state frequency range, bin width/resolution bandwidth, windowing method, and stage-based segmentation.
________________________________________
B. Device Classes and Technology Type
Key finding: Emission characteristics differ significantly by device class and converter topology.
	CFL and LED lamps exhibit voltage-dependent supraharmonic emissions and frequency-band shifts (Frydlewicz et al., 2024).
	Plug-in EV chargers show distortion behavior influenced by load mix and charger design, requiring device-type disclosure (Mariscotti, 2022).
	BESS-integrated fast chargers demonstrate different spectral behavior compared to grid-fed chargers, including even-harmonic amplification (Giri & Rönnberg, 2025).
Implication for reproducibility: Studies must report converter topology, rated power, control mode, and integration context (e.g., BESS-coupled vs. grid-fed).
________________________________________
C. Operating States and Dynamic Conditions
Key finding: Emissions vary strongly with operating state and supply conditions.
	Supply voltage variation changes both emission magnitude and spectral distribution in lighting devices (Frydlewicz et al., 2024).
	Fast-charging systems were classified into five operating stages, with stage-specific distortion patterns (Giri & Rönnberg, 2025).
	EV charger emissions depend on grid conditions and charger operating scenarios, requiring contextual disclosure (Mariscotti, 2022).
Implication for reproducibility: Authors should define load state, charging stage, power level, voltage level, and transient vs. steady-state regime.
________________________________________
D. Impedance Scenarios and Measurement Chain Transparency
Key finding: Grid impedance, transformer accuracy, and measurement uncertainty significantly affect reported supraharmonic levels.
	Supraharmonic impact depends on MV/LV grid context and instrument transformer suitability above 2 kHz (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024).
	EV charger distortion assessment requires details on grid short-circuit power and distortion background (Mariscotti, 2022).
	High-frequency measurement chains must specify sensor type, calibration limits, uncertainty (0.01–<1%), and bandwidth (up to 150–500 kHz) to ensure traceability (Daaboul et al., 2025).
Implication for reproducibility: Minimum reporting must include: transducer type, calibration procedure, uncertainty budget, sampling rate, anti-alias filtering, and grid impedance assumptions.

	Lack of standardized frequency bin definitions above 9 kHz.
	Insufficient reporting of grid impedance and short-circuit ratio.
	Limited public datasets with raw spectral data for replication.
	Few multi-site studies comparing identical devices under different impedance scenarios.
	Inconsistent uncertainty budgets and calibration traceability above 2 kHz.

Q2: What strategies exist to select representative feeder archetypes or impedance scenarios for 2-150 kHz studies (urban/rural, cable/overhead, transformer types)?
Empirical studies select representative feeder archetypes for 2–150 kHz research using three main strategies: (1) stochastic modeling of real LV/MV feeders (urban vs. rural) with explicit conductor, cable, and transformer data; (2) stage-based or scenario-based simulation anchored to measured grid parameters (short-circuit power, X/R ratio, transformer impedance); and (3) measurement-informed modeling that captures voltage-dependent and impedance-dependent behavior of connected converters. These approaches emphasize realistic topology (cable vs. overhead), transformer type (tap-changing vs. fixed), and supply strength to create reproducible impedance scenarios. Minimum reproducibility requires full disclosure of feeder topology, conductor parameters, transformer impedance model, grounding scheme, and background distortion levels.
________________________________________
2) Evidence Synthesis
A. Stochastic and “Typical Feeder” Modeling (Urban vs. Rural Archetypes)
Key finding: Representative feeders are often derived from real DSO data and modeled stochastically to capture variability in impedance and loading.
	A stochastic hosting-capacity study modeled a typical rural LV feeder with full 4-wire representation and conductor parameters to evaluate voltage and harmonic impacts under varying DER penetration (Bendík et al., 2022).
	An empirical EV load study implemented a typical LV network in OpenDSS, comparing constant power vs. voltage-dependent models to reflect realistic feeder impedance and voltage sensitivity (Phan et al., 2025).
Strategy:
	Use real DSO feeder templates (urban dense-cable vs. rural long-overhead).
	Model full phase + neutral conductors.
	Apply stochastic loading to capture impedance variation under penetration scenarios.
________________________________________
B. Scenario-Based Impedance Selection (Short-Circuit Strength & X/R Ratio)
Key finding: Feeder archetypes are defined by grid strength metrics (short-circuit power, SCR, transformer impedance) to represent weak vs. strong networks.
	EV fast-charging infrastructure modeling evaluated power quality at both transformer secondary and charger terminals, demonstrating the need to define upstream transformer impedance and grid strength (Chiradeja et al., 2025).
	Converter spatial distribution studies in MV benchmark grids showed that PCC location and feeder configuration affect dynamic response, emphasizing topology-dependent impedance characterization (Pfendler et al., 2021).
Strategy:
	Define feeder archetypes by:
	Short-circuit level (weak rural vs. strong urban).
	Transformer impedance (%Z, tap changer presence).
	X/R ratio and cable vs. overhead reactance profiles.
________________________________________
C. Cable vs. Overhead Representation and Conductor Modeling
Key finding: Accurate feeder archetypes require conductor-level impedance modeling, especially for rural overhead lines.
	Rural LV feeder modeling with explicit 4-wire representation revealed voltage and thermal limits as primary constraints, showing sensitivity to conductor impedance (Bendík et al., 2022).
	MV benchmark modeling demonstrated limited but measurable sensitivity to PCC location along feeder segments, implying the need to model line segment impedance accurately (Pfendler et al., 2021).
Strategy:
	Use separate archetypes for:
	Urban cable-dominated feeders (short length, low R/X).
	Rural overhead feeders (long length, higher R/X).
	Include neutral conductor impedance and grounding configuration.
________________________________________
D. Measurement-Informed Archetype Calibration
Key finding: Laboratory or field measurements are used to calibrate feeder models, ensuring impedance scenarios reflect real system behavior.
	EV charger testing under variable supply voltages showed voltage-dependent current behavior, requiring feeder impedance scenarios that reflect real voltage sensitivity (Phan et al., 2025).
	Fast-charging station studies validated compliance by measuring THD at transformer and terminal levels, effectively defining upstream impedance boundary conditions (Chiradeja et al., 2025).
Strategy:
	Calibrate feeder impedance using measured voltage drop and distortion.
	Validate modeled impedance via transformer-side and load-side measurements.
	Report transformer model type (e.g., OLTC vs. fixed tap) and equivalent circuit parameters.

	Lack of standardized feeder archetype libraries for 2–150 kHz impedance studies.
	Limited published impedance frequency sweeps of real urban vs. rural feeders.
	Insufficient reporting of transformer high-frequency impedance models.
	Few comparative studies of cable vs. overhead feeders in the supraharmonic band.
	Need for publicly available benchmark feeders including frequency-dependent impedance models.

Q3: What conventions are used to report uncertainty (confidence intervals, bootstrap, convergence diagnostics for Monte Carlo), and how do journals expect them presented?

Across empirical studies (2021–present), uncertainty is most commonly reported using 95% confidence intervals (CIs), evaluated via empirical coverage probability (CP) and average/expected interval length (AL/EL) in Monte Carlo simulations. Bootstrap methods—percentile, bootstrap-t, BCa, double bootstrap, and calibrated/bootstrap-based variance estimators—are widely used, with journals expecting explicit reporting of resampling size, number of Monte Carlo replications, and coverage diagnostics. Convergence in simulation studies is typically assessed via replication counts, empirical coverage stability, and reporting of non-convergence or missingness rates. Journals increasingly expect transparent reporting of CI construction method, simulation size, convergence checks, and reproducible code.
________________________________________
2) Evidence Synthesis
A. Confidence Intervals (Nominal 95%) and Coverage Diagnostics
Key finding: Empirical studies report CIs with explicit evaluation of empirical coverage probability (CP) and interval length.
	Monte Carlo studies comparing likelihood-based, Wald-type, bootstrap-t, and BCa CIs report CP and average length (AL/EL) as primary performance metrics, with convergence toward nominal 95% coverage as sample size increases (Tulyanitikul & Panichkitkosolkul, 2024); (Confidence Intervals for the Komal Distribution, 2024).
	Sequential trial emulation research compares sandwich, nonparametric bootstrap, and LEF bootstrap CIs using coverage under varying sample sizes and event rates, demonstrating journal expectation of simulation-based validation (Limozin et al., 2024).
Convention:
	Report nominal level (typically 95%).
	Provide empirical CP across simulation replicates.
	Provide average interval length.
	Discuss small-sample undercoverage.
________________________________________
B. Bootstrap Conventions (Percentile, BCa, Double, Calibrated)
Key finding: Bootstrap is widely used, but journals expect justification of method choice and simulation validation.
	Comparative bootstrap studies show double bootstrap outperforming BCa in coverage performance and emphasize reporting bootstrap type and resample size (Zrimvsek & Štrumbelj, 2024).
	Spatial covariance research compares percentile vs. reverse percentile bootstrap, evaluating coverage rates and interval scores for large datasets (Chen et al., 2025).
	Calibrated bootstrap approaches explicitly target finite-sample validity, with algorithmic steps and reproducibility details reported (Jiang et al., 2024).
	Hierarchical biomass modeling uses parametric and pairwise bootstrap with Monte Carlo validation, reporting approximate unbiasedness of variance estimators (Saarela et al., 2024).
Convention:
	State bootstrap type (percentile, BCa, bootstrap-t, double, calibrated).
	Report number of bootstrap replicates.
	Report empirical coverage and bias.
	Provide algorithmic detail sufficient for replication.
________________________________________
C. Monte Carlo Simulation Reporting & Convergence Diagnostics
Key finding: Journals expect transparent reporting of simulation design, replication size, and handling of non-convergence.
	Monte Carlo output analysis research emphasizes higher-order bias correction and variance estimation, advocating reporting of bias and variance reduction techniques (Vahdat & Shashaani, 2022).
	Reviews of simulation practice show that many studies underreport non-convergence and missingness, and recommend explicitly reporting frequency of failed runs and handling procedures (Pawel et al., 2024).
	Change-point bootstrap CI research validates asymptotic validity via simulation, implying expectation of large replication counts and convergence assessment (Cho & Kirch, 2021).
Convention:
	Report number of Monte Carlo replications (e.g., ≥1,000).
	Show empirical stability of coverage or estimates across runs.
	Report convergence failures or algorithmic instability.
	Provide reproducible simulation design.
________________________________________
D. Journal Expectations for Presentation
Observed expectations across journals (e.g., Statistical Methods in Medical Research, Environmetrics, American Statistician):
	Explicit statement of CI method and nominal level.
	Simulation-based validation of coverage (Monte Carlo).
	Reporting both coverage and interval width.
	Description of bootstrap replication count and seed or reproducibility info.
	Transparent reporting of non-convergence or failed simulations (Pawel et al., 2024).
________________________________________

	Inconsistent reporting of Monte Carlo replication size and convergence criteria.
	Limited adoption of formal convergence diagnostics beyond coverage stability.
	Underreporting of simulation failures/non-convergence in published studies.
	Need for standardized reporting checklists for simulation-based inference.
	Greater transparency in bootstrap seed control and computational reproducibility.
