3.2 Baseline PPP aggregation statistics across frequency
Q1: What baseline results have been reported for aggregated supraharmonic voltage/current spectra under random deployments, and how do they vary with frequency?

Recent field and laboratory studies report that under quasi-random or heterogeneous deployments of converters (e.g., EVSE, LED/CFL drivers), aggregated supraharmonic current spectra exhibit narrowband clusters around switching frequencies (typically 2–20 kHz and their sidebands), while voltage spectra are strongly shaped by frequency-dependent feeder impedance and resonance peaks. Field EVSE measurements show that voltage distortion above 2 kHz increases with weaker grids and short-circuit power reduction, indicating aggregation interacts with impedance amplification (Mariscotti, 2022). MV/LV grid analysis confirms that resonance phenomena cause frequency-selective amplification, producing peaks in specific bands rather than broadband growth (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024). Controlled experiments further show that emission magnitude and spectral location vary with operating state and supply voltage, contributing to frequency-dependent aggregation variability (Frydlewicz et al., 2024).
________________________________________
2) Evidence Synthesis
A. Aggregated Supraharmonic Current Spectra
Baseline observation:
Aggregated converter currents are not broadband but exhibit discrete spectral clusters near switching frequencies and harmonically related components.
	Field measurements of plug-in EV chargers show distinct supraharmonic current clusters typically concentrated in lower supraharmonic bands (<20–30 kHz), with magnitudes varying by charger type (Mariscotti, 2022).
	Laboratory lighting-device experiments show conducted emission peaks within the 2–150 kHz band that shift with supply voltage (Frydlewicz et al., 2024).
Frequency dependence:
	Higher magnitude clusters below ~20–30 kHz.
	Reduced magnitude toward higher frequencies, except at resonance-aligned bands.
________________________________________
B. Aggregated Supraharmonic Voltage Spectra
Baseline observation:
Voltage spectra reflect impedance shaping rather than simple linear summation of currents.
	EVSE field data show that voltage distortion magnitude increases in weak grids (low short-circuit power), demonstrating impedance-controlled amplification (Mariscotti, 2022).
	MV/LV analysis confirms that transformer frequency response and feeder resonance create frequency-selective voltage peaks above 2 kHz (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024).
Frequency dependence:
	Voltage distortion peaks coincide with impedance resonance frequencies.
	Non-monotonic variation across 2–150 kHz.
________________________________________
C. Aggregation Under Heterogeneous Random Deployments
Baseline observation:
Random spatial deployment produces statistical smoothing in current spectra but does not eliminate narrowband peaks; voltage extremes remain resonance-driven.
	EVSE measurements across public LV grids show variability across sites, indicating deployment randomness does not remove feeder-dependent spectral features (Mariscotti, 2022).
	Voltage-variation experiments demonstrate that emission shifts contribute to statistical spread in aggregated spectra (Frydlewicz et al., 2024).
Frequency-dependent variability:
	Lower-frequency supraharmonic bands (<20 kHz) show larger variance.
	Higher bands often dominated by network resonance rather than source magnitude.
________________________________________

4) Gaps and Future Research
	Limited large-scale statistical studies of random spatial deployments across multiple feeders.
	Sparse joint current–voltage percentile analyses under stochastic aggregation.
	Few long-term datasets capturing temporal variability of aggregated spectra.
	Limited modeling of tail behavior at higher supraharmonic frequencies (>50 kHz).
	Need for probabilistic baseline curves for standardized planning.

Q2: How do frequency-dependent kernels (resonance/attenuation) shape mean/RMS/percentile curves, and what empirical or simulated evidence supports these trends?

Recent empirical and analytical studies show that frequency-dependent network kernels—particularly resonance peaks and attenuation at higher frequencies—strongly shape the mean, RMS, and high-percentile supraharmonic voltage curves. Field EVSE measurements demonstrate that voltage distortion increases in weak grids and at frequencies aligned with impedance peaks, indicating resonance-driven amplification of RMS and percentile levels (Mariscotti, 2022). MV/LV analyses further confirm that transformer and feeder frequency response above 2 kHz create band-specific amplification or attenuation patterns (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024). Controlled laboratory studies show that source spectra interact with supply conditions, but voltage trends are dominated by impedance shaping rather than uniform spectral growth (Frydlewicz et al., 2024).
________________________________________
2) Evidence Synthesis
A. Resonance Peaks and Amplification of Mean/RMS
Mechanism:
At frequencies where network impedance |Z(f)| is high (parallel resonance), injected current components produce amplified voltage responses.
	Field EV charger measurements show higher voltage distortion at frequencies associated with feeder impedance characteristics, especially in weak grids with low short-circuit power (Mariscotti, 2022).
	MV/LV studies confirm that resonance phenomena above 2 kHz significantly influence distortion propagation, creating frequency-localized peaks in voltage spectra (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024).
Observed trend:
	Mean and RMS voltage curves exhibit sharp peaks at resonance frequencies.
	Percentile curves (e.g., 95th percentile) rise disproportionately at those bands due to selective amplification.
________________________________________
B. Attenuation at Higher Frequencies
Mechanism:
Line inductance, transformer leakage, and damping effects increase attenuation at higher supraharmonic frequencies.
	MV/LV analysis shows transformer frequency response reduces propagation at certain higher-frequency bands, shaping non-monotonic spectral curves (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024).
Observed trend:
	After resonance peaks, mean/RMS curves often decrease with increasing frequency.
	Percentile spread narrows in attenuated bands due to reduced amplification.
________________________________________
C. Interaction Between Source Spectra and Impedance Kernel
Mechanism:
Converter emission spectra contain narrowband clusters that are selectively amplified or suppressed by |Z(f)|.
	EVSE measurements show discrete current clusters whose voltage impact depends on grid impedance (Mariscotti, 2022).
	Laboratory experiments demonstrate that supply voltage variation shifts emission magnitude and spectral distribution, but overall voltage impact is governed by network transfer characteristics (Frydlewicz et al., 2024).
Observed trend:
	Where emission clusters align with resonance, percentile curves steepen.
	Where emission clusters fall in attenuated regions, RMS values remain moderate despite source magnitude.
________________________________________
D. Effect on Percentile and Extreme Values
Mechanism:
Because voltage distortion scales with |Z(f)|·|I(f)|, frequency bands with high |Z(f)| inflate distribution tails.
	Field data show distortion variability across feeders, with extreme values linked to weaker grids (higher effective impedance) (Mariscotti, 2022).
Observed trend:
	95th/99th percentile curves peak more sharply than mean curves at resonance frequencies.
	Tail amplification is frequency-selective rather than broadband.
________________________________________

Study	Kernel Feature	Evidence Type	Impact on Mean/RMS	Impact on Percentiles
Mariscotti, 2022	Weak-grid impedance	Field EVSE	Higher RMS at impedance peaks	Elevated 95th percentile in weak grids
Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024	Resonance & transformer response	MV/LV analysis	Frequency-selective amplification/attenuation	Tail amplification at resonance bands
Frydlewicz et al., 2024	Source spectral shifts	Lab experiment	Emission shift interacts with	Z(f)

4) Gaps and Future Research
	Few feeder-scale statistical comparisons of mean vs. percentile amplification ratios.
	Limited modeling of extreme-value scaling under resonance conditions.
	Sparse long-term measurements to quantify frequency-dependent percentile stability.
	Need for impedance-aware probabilistic modeling linking |Z(f)| to distribution tails.
	Lack of standardized percentile reporting across frequency bins.

Q3: What comparisons exist between analytical PPP-based predictions and measured feeder/site data for supraharmonics, and what mismatch sources are identified?

Direct PPP-based analytical predictions of aggregated supraharmonics are rarely validated end-to-end against feeder data, but recent field and MV/LV studies provide partial comparisons between stochastic-aggregation assumptions and measured spectra. EVSE field measurements show that distortion levels depend strongly on feeder impedance and short-circuit power, deviating from simple random independent source aggregation assumptions (Mariscotti, 2022). MV/LV analyses confirm that resonance and transformer frequency response introduce frequency-selective amplification not captured by spatially homogeneous kernels (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024). Laboratory studies further show operating-state dependence and emission shifts that violate stationary mark assumptions (Frydlewicz et al., 2024). Identified mismatch sources include impedance uncertainty, device heterogeneity, nonlinear coupling, and measurement-chain limitations.
________________________________________
2) Evidence Synthesis
A. Comparison of Random Aggregation Assumptions vs. Field EVSE Data
Analytical baseline (PPP-style assumption):
	Independent random deployment of converters with identical mark distributions.
Measured findings:
	Field EVSE measurements show distortion magnitude varies significantly with feeder short-circuit power and local impedance conditions (Mariscotti, 2022).
Mismatch source identified:
	Spatial heterogeneity of impedance invalidates homogeneous PPP kernel assumption.
	Device-to-device variability violates identical mark assumption.
________________________________________
B. Frequency-Selective Amplification vs. Homogeneous Kernel Models
Analytical baseline:
	Aggregation models often assume frequency-independent or smooth attenuation kernels.
Measured findings:
	MV/LV analysis demonstrates strong resonance peaks above 2 kHz that produce band-specific voltage amplification (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024).
Mismatch source identified:
	Real feeders exhibit frequency-dependent impedance with sharp resonances.
	Transformer response introduces non-monotonic transfer functions.
Impact on predictions:
	PPP-based models with smooth kernels underestimate peak distortion percentiles at resonance frequencies.
________________________________________
C. Operating-State and Supply-Dependent Emission Variability
Analytical baseline:
	Stationary mark distribution (fixed emission spectrum).
Measured findings:
	Laboratory results show conducted emissions shift with supply voltage variation (Frydlewicz et al., 2024).
Mismatch source identified:
	Non-stationary emission behavior.
	Dependence between source magnitude and grid state.
Impact:
	Analytical predictions based on independent marks underestimate variance and tail behavior.
________________________________________
D. Measurement-Chain and Instrumentation Effects
Measured findings:
	MV/LV distortion levels depend partly on instrument transformer frequency response (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024).
Mismatch source identified:
	Measurement bandwidth limitations.
	Calibration uncertainty above classical harmonic range.
Impact:
	Analytical–measurement discrepancies may reflect instrumentation artifacts rather than modeling error alone.
________________________________________

	Converter–grid control coupling.
	Measurement-chain bandwidth and calibration uncertainty.

3.3 Scaling with charger density and service-area size
Q1: What theoretical results describe scaling of aggregated interference/distortion with point intensity and observation window size in shot-noise models?

Recent stochastic-geometry results show that in Poisson shot-noise models, the mean aggregate interference scales linearly with point intensity λ, while variance and higher moments depend on both λ and the kernel’s integrability, often scaling as \mathcal{O}(\lambda)for finite second moments (Ganti & Haenggi, 2022). For clustered (Neyman–Scott) processes, aggregation scaling incorporates both parent intensity and offspring number, increasing correlation and tail probability relative to homogeneous PPPs (Afshang & Dhillon, 2021). Multi-point interference results further show that correlation across observation points increases with λ and decreases with spatial separation, affecting percentile scaling (Ganti & Haenggi, 2022). Observation-window enlargement leads to convergence toward spatial averages under ergodicity, with variance decreasing as window size grows when kernels are integrable.
________________________________________
2) Evidence Synthesis
A. Scaling with Point Intensity (PPP Shot Noise)
Model:
Shot noise I=\sum_{x_i\in\Phi}{h(}x_i), where \Phiis a PPP of intensity λ.
Theoretical results:
	The mean interference scales proportionally to λ when the kernel h(\cdot)is integrable (Ganti & Haenggi, 2022).
	Variance also scales linearly with λ under finite second-moment conditions.
Implication:
	Doubling converter density approximately doubles expected aggregate distortion (in linear regimes).
	Percentile levels increase with λ but not necessarily proportionally if tails are heavy.
________________________________________
B. Tail and Percentile Scaling
Result:
	Higher λ increases both mean and variance, shifting percentile curves upward.
	Tail probability depends on both λ and kernel decay rate (Ganti & Haenggi, 2022).
Key mechanism:
	For kernels with slow decay, extreme values are more sensitive to intensity increases.
	For rapidly decaying kernels, percentile growth is closer to linear scaling.
________________________________________
C. Effect of Clustering (Non-PPP Scaling)
Model:
Neyman–Scott or Poisson cluster processes.
	Cluster models show that aggregate interference scaling depends on both parent intensity and mean cluster size (Afshang & Dhillon, 2021).
	Clustering increases spatial correlation and tail probability relative to PPP with same λ.
Scaling insight:
	Effective intensity becomes \lambda_{\mathrm{eff}}=\lambda_p\timesn.
	Tail growth can exceed linear scaling due to local concentration effects.
________________________________________
D. Scaling with Observation Window Size
Result:
	As observation window size increases, sample mean converges to ensemble mean under ergodicity (Ganti & Haenggi, 2022).
	Variance of spatial average decreases with window size when kernel is integrable.
Interpretation:
	Larger feeder monitoring zones produce smoother average distortion estimates.
	Extreme percentiles may still be dominated by local clustering.
________________________________________
E. Multi-Point Scaling and Correlation
Result:
	Interference correlation between two points increases with λ and decreases with distance (Ganti & Haenggi, 2022).
Implication:
	Aggregated distortion percentiles across multiple PCCs scale jointly rather than independently.
	Window expansion across spatial domain reduces relative variability but preserves correlation structure.
________________________________________
3) Summary Table
Study	Process Type	Scaling with λ	Window Scaling	Tail Behavior
Ganti & Haenggi, 2022	PPP shot noise	Mean ∝ λ, Var ∝ λ	Variance ↓ with window size	Tail depends on kernel decay
Afshang & Dhillon, 2021	Cluster process	∝ parent intensity × cluster size	Increased local correlation	Heavier tails vs PPP
Ganti & Haenggi, 2022	Multi-point PPP	Correlation ↑ with λ	Correlation ↓ with distance	Joint exceedance scaling
________________________________________
4) Key Theoretical Scaling Laws (Conceptual)
	Mean scaling:
\mathbb{E}[I]∝λ
	Variance scaling:
Var(I)\propto\lambda(finite second moment)
	Cluster scaling:
\mathbb{E}[I]∝λpn
	Window scaling:
Spatial average variance ↓ as observation window area ↑ (ergodic averaging)
________________________________________
5) Gaps and Future Research
	Limited direct application of scaling laws to supraharmonic impedance kernels.
	Sparse validation of percentile scaling in electrical feeders.
	Few extreme-value analyses for clustered converter deployments.
	Need for impedance-aware analytical kernel scaling results.
	Limited feeder-scale empirical verification of window-size convergence.
________________________________________

IEEE Transactions on Wireless Communications. https://consensus.app/papers/poisson-cluster-process-based-analysis-of-downlink-afshang-dhillon/5e46a9e25e6f58a7bdb3c03c2eaa1b8a/?utm_source=chatgpt

Q2: What power-systems studies demonstrate density-driven transitions (low-density linear growth vs high-density √growth under cancellation), and what modelling assumptions drive the transition?

Recent supraharmonic and converter-emission studies show evidence consistent with density-driven transitions: near-linear growth of aggregate distortion at low deployment density, and sublinear (approximately √N-type) growth when phase diversity and partial cancellation dominate, though full analytical √N scaling is rarely formalized explicitly. Field EVSE measurements indicate that distortion increases with the number of chargers and is amplified in weak grids, consistent with linear superposition under low correlation (Mariscotti, 2022). MV/LV analyses demonstrate that resonance and shared impedance coupling modify aggregation behavior at higher densities, producing deviations from simple linear scaling (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024). Laboratory evidence of supply-dependent spectral shifts implies that modelling assumptions regarding phase independence and identical marks critically determine whether growth appears linear or sublinear (Frydlewicz et al., 2024).
________________________________________
2) Evidence Synthesis
A. Low-Density Regime: Linear Growth (Superposition Dominant)
Observed behavior:
	At low penetration, aggregate distortion tends to scale approximately with the number of active converters.
Evidence:
	Field EVSE measurements show that voltage distortion increases with charger presence and is more pronounced in feeders with lower short-circuit power (Mariscotti, 2022).
Model assumptions driving linearity:
	Independent current injections.
	Identical mark distributions.
	Weak correlation among phase angles.
	Linear impedance kernel.
Interpretation:
	When sources are few and weakly coupled, mean voltage ∝ N.
________________________________________
B. Intermediate/High-Density Regime: Sublinear (√N-Type) Growth Under Cancellation
Mechanism:
	If converter emissions are partially uncorrelated in phase, RMS aggregation may grow proportionally to √N (variance summation) rather than N.
Indirect evidence:
	Variability across feeders and operating states suggests partial cancellation or diversity effects, especially when multiple device types coexist (Mariscotti, 2022).
	Supply-voltage-dependent emission shifts indicate non-identical spectral contributions that may reduce coherent summation (Frydlewicz et al., 2024).
Model assumptions enabling √N scaling:
	Random phase assumption.
	Finite second moment of emission marks.
	No dominant coherent frequency component.
Interpretation:
	RMS distortion ∝ √N when cancellation dominates.
________________________________________
C. High-Density Regime with Resonance and Coupling: Superlinear or Non-Monotonic Effects
Observed behavior:
	Frequency-selective amplification at resonance may counteract cancellation and produce local superlinear growth.
Evidence:
	MV/LV studies show that resonance and transformer frequency response strongly shape voltage distortion above 2 kHz (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024).
Model assumptions driving deviation:
	Frequency-dependent impedance kernel.
	Shared network transfer function across devices.
	Correlated amplification near resonance frequencies.
Interpretation:
	At resonance-aligned frequencies, growth may exceed √N and approach linear or stronger scaling locally.
________________________________________
D. Transition Drivers (Summary of Modelling Assumptions)
Assumption	Low-Density Effect	High-Density Effect
Independent phases	Linear mean growth	√N RMS growth
Identical marks	Smooth scaling	Reduced diversity → more coherent growth
Impedance homogeneous	Predictable scaling	Underestimates resonance amplification
Resonance present	Minor effect	Dominant frequency-selective amplification
Operating-state independence	Stable scaling	Variability increases tail spread
________________________________________
3) Summary Table
Study	Context	Observed Scaling Trend	Key Assumptions Influencing Transition
Mariscotti, 2022	LV EVSE field	Approx. linear growth at low density	Independent injections, weak coupling
Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024	MV/LV resonance study	Frequency-selective amplification	Resonant impedance kernel
Frydlewicz et al., 2024	Lab emission study	Spectral variability affects aggregation	Non-identical, state-dependent marks
________________________________________
4) Gaps and Future Research
	Few formal statistical tests distinguishing N vs. √N scaling in feeder data.
	Limited analytical derivations incorporating impedance resonance into scaling laws.
	Sparse extreme-value analysis at high converter densities.
	Need for controlled density-escalation experiments in real feeders.
	Lack of unified theoretical framework combining phase randomness and resonance effects.
________________________________________

Q3: What boundary effects (finite region, edge corrections, non-uniform density near PCC) are documented, and how significant are they for realistic service areas?


Recent stochastic-geometry and supraharmonic field studies indicate that finite-region boundary effects, spatial edge bias, and non-uniform deployment density near PCCs can materially affect aggregate distortion predictions, particularly for small feeders or clustered installations. Interference-correlation analyses show that finite observation windows alter mean and variance estimates and require edge correction to avoid bias (Ganti & Haenggi, 2022). Cluster-process models demonstrate that spatial clustering increases local interference intensity near observation points compared with homogeneous PPP assumptions (Afshang & Dhillon, 2021). Empirical EVSE and MV/LV studies confirm that feeder topology and resonance produce location-dependent distortion patterns inconsistent with spatial homogeneity assumptions (Mariscotti, 2022); (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024). For realistic service areas (finite feeders), boundary effects are most significant when feeder length is comparable to kernel decay length or when clustering is strong.
________________________________________
2) Evidence Synthesis
A. Finite Observation Region Effects
Theoretical insight:
	In PPP shot-noise models, aggregate interference is often derived under infinite-plane assumptions; truncation to finite windows introduces bias in mean and variance estimation (Ganti & Haenggi, 2022).
Documented effects:
	Under finite windows, interference contributions from outside the region are omitted, reducing expected aggregate levels.
	Spatial averaging variance depends on window size; smaller regions exhibit higher variability.
Significance for service areas:
	Short LV feeders behave like finite windows; omission of upstream/downstream contributions biases predictions.
________________________________________
B. Edge Corrections and Spatial Correlation
Theoretical results:
	Multi-point interference correlation decays with spatial separation, but correlation is artificially reduced near boundaries if edge effects are not corrected (Ganti & Haenggi, 2022).
Implications:
	Edge points in a finite feeder may appear to experience lower aggregate distortion due to missing neighbors outside the modeled region.
	Corrected estimators or guard regions are recommended in spatial simulations.
________________________________________
C. Non-Uniform Density and Clustering Near PCC
Cluster-process findings:
	Poisson cluster processes produce locally elevated point intensity near cluster centers, increasing interference beyond homogeneous PPP predictions (Afshang & Dhillon, 2021).
Translation to distribution networks:
	EVSE often cluster in parking facilities or residential blocks.
	Non-uniform density near PCC increases local distortion variance.
Empirical support:
	Field EVSE measurements show feeder-dependent distortion variability, consistent with heterogeneous spatial deployment (Mariscotti, 2022).
________________________________________
D. Location-Dependent Impedance and Boundary-Like Effects
Observation:
	MV/LV analyses show that transformer response and resonance produce location-dependent distortion propagation (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024).
Interpretation:
	PCCs near transformer boundaries behave differently from mid-feeder nodes.
	Effective “kernel truncation” occurs at feeder ends, analogous to spatial edge effects.
Significance:
	Boundary effects are substantial when feeder length is short or resonance nodes are near edges.
________________________________________
3) Summary Table
Study	Boundary Phenomenon	Evidence Type	Effect Magnitude	Practical Significance
Ganti & Haenggi, 2022	Finite window truncation	Theoretical	Bias in mean/variance	Important for short feeders
Afshang & Dhillon, 2021	Spatial clustering	Analytical	Increased local variance	Significant in clustered EVSE areas
Mariscotti, 2022	Feeder heterogeneity	Field	Location-dependent distortion	Realistic LV feeders affected
Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024	Transformer/resonance boundary	MV/LV study	Frequency-selective location effects	Strong near resonance nodes
________________________________________
4) Significance for Realistic Service Areas
Boundary effects are most significant when:
	Feeder length ≈ kernel decay length.
	Deployment density is strongly clustered.
	PCC is near transformer or network termination.
	Observation region excludes upstream/downstream network contributions.
In large, well-interconnected networks, ergodic averaging reduces bias; in small LV feeders, effects can materially alter percentile estimates.
________________________________________
5) Gaps and Future Research
	Few empirical studies quantify boundary-bias magnitude in real feeders.
	Limited application of spatial edge-correction techniques in power-system simulations.
	Sparse modeling of non-uniform density gradients near PCC.
	Need for feeder-scale validation of cluster-process vs. PPP predictions.
	Lack of standardized boundary-treatment guidelines for stochastic power modeling.

3.4 Spatial uncertainty and inhomogeneity effects
Q1: What empirical evidence supports inhomogeneous deployment of EV chargers/converters (hotspots, urban centres, hubs), and how is this spatial non-uniformity quantified?

Recent empirical studies show that EV chargers and power-electronic converters are deployed in spatially inhomogeneous patterns, with clustering in urban centres, commercial hubs, and parking facilities rather than uniform spatial distributions. Field EVSE measurements across multiple LV sites reveal feeder-dependent distortion variability consistent with localized charger concentrations (Mariscotti, 2022). Spatial cluster-process modeling demonstrates that clustered point processes better match real deployment scenarios than homogeneous PPP assumptions (Afshang & Dhillon, 2021). Spatial non-uniformity is typically quantified using cluster intensity parameters, parent–offspring models, pair-correlation functions, and spatial correlation coefficients (Ganti & Haenggi, 2022).
________________________________________
2) Evidence Synthesis
A. Field Evidence of Localized EVSE Deployment
Empirical observation:
	LV feeder measurements show distortion levels vary substantially between sites, implying non-uniform charger presence and clustering effects (Mariscotti, 2022).
Interpretation:
	If deployment were spatially homogeneous, feeder-to-feeder variability would be smaller.
	Observed variability supports hotspot-style deployment.
Quantification approach in practice:
	Comparison across multiple feeder sites.
	Distortion variance linked to charger penetration level.
________________________________________
B. Cluster-Process Models for Hotspot Deployment
Theoretical/empirical framework:
	Poisson cluster processes (e.g., Neyman–Scott models) represent spatial clustering around hub locations (Afshang & Dhillon, 2021).
Quantification parameters:
	Parent intensity (hub density).
	Mean number of offspring per cluster (charger density per hub).
	Cluster radius.
Relevance to EVSE:
	Urban charging stations resemble clustered offspring around parking facilities or transport hubs.
________________________________________
C. Spatial Correlation and Pair-Correlation Functions
Analytical tools:
	Interference-correlation studies use pair-correlation functions and spatial covariance to quantify non-uniform deployment (Ganti & Haenggi, 2022).
Metrics:
	Pair-correlation function g(r) > 1 indicates clustering.
	Spatial correlation coefficient vs. distance.
	Intensity gradients across regions.
Implication:
	Non-uniformity can be statistically detected through deviation from PPP baseline (g(r)=1).
________________________________________
D. Impedance and Location-Dependent Distortion as Proxy for Density
Observation:
	MV/LV distortion studies show location-dependent amplification influenced by feeder topology and local device presence (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024).
Interpretation:
	Spatially varying distortion levels act as indirect evidence of non-uniform converter density combined with impedance heterogeneity.
________________________________________
3) Summary Table
Study	Evidence Type	Deployment Pattern Indicated	Quantification Method
Mariscotti, 2022	Field EVSE measurements	Site-to-site variability	Feeder comparison, distortion variance
Afshang & Dhillon, 2021	Cluster-process modeling	Hub-based clustering	Parent intensity, cluster radius
Ganti & Haenggi, 2022	Spatial interference theory	Correlated point fields	Pair-correlation, correlation coefficient
Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024	MV/LV grid analysis	Location-dependent distortion	Frequency-selective amplification mapping
________________________________________
4) Common Quantification Metrics
	Intensity function λ(x) (spatially varying density).
	Cluster parameters (λ_parent, mean offspring, cluster radius).
	Pair-correlation function g(r).
	Ripley’s K-function (in spatial statistics practice).
	Spatial correlation coefficient vs. distance.
________________________________________
5) Gaps and Future Research
	Limited publication of detailed EVSE geospatial datasets in peer-reviewed power journals.
	Sparse direct estimation of cluster parameters from feeder-level measurements.
	Few integrated models combining spatial deployment data with impedance-aware distortion modeling.
	Limited long-term monitoring of urban hotspot evolution.
	Need for standardized spatial-nonuniformity metrics in power-quality planning.

Q2: How do inhomogeneous PPP or Cox-process models change aggregate moments and tails relative to homogeneous PPP, and what analytical tools are used?

Recent stochastic-geometry results show that replacing a homogeneous PPP with an inhomogeneous PPP (space-varying intensity λ(x)) or a Cox process (random intensity field) alters both aggregate moments and tail behavior of shot-noise fields. In inhomogeneous PPPs, the mean and variance become spatially dependent and scale with the local intensity function rather than a constant λ (Ganti & Haenggi, 2022). Cox and cluster-process models introduce additional variability from intensity randomness, increasing variance and thickening tails relative to homogeneous PPP with equal mean density (Afshang & Dhillon, 2021). Analytical tools used include Laplace functionals, probability generating functionals (PGFL), moment measures, pair-correlation functions, and joint exceedance probability derivations.
________________________________________
2) Evidence Synthesis
A. Homogeneous PPP Baseline
Model:
Shot noise I=\sum_{x_i\in\Phi}{h(}x_i), with constant intensity λ.
Known results:
	\mathbb{E}[I]∝λ(linear scaling).
	Var(I)\propto\lambdaunder finite second-moment conditions (Ganti & Haenggi, 2022).
Tail behavior:
	Determined primarily by kernel decay and mark distribution.
This provides the reference against which inhomogeneous and Cox models are compared.
________________________________________
B. Inhomogeneous PPP (Deterministic λ(x))
Model:
Intensity varies spatially: λ → λ(x).
Effect on moments:
	Mean becomes spatial integral weighted by λ(x).
	Variance reflects spatial intensity gradients (Ganti & Haenggi, 2022).
Key changes relative to homogeneous PPP:
	Spatial non-stationarity of moments.
	Higher local peaks in regions where λ(x) is large.
	Increased variance near hotspots.
Analytical tools:
	Campbell’s theorem generalized to λ(x).
	Laplace functional with spatially varying intensity.
	Pair-correlation functions for detecting deviation from homogeneity.
________________________________________
C. Cox Processes (Random Intensity Field)
Model:
Λ(x) is random; conditionally PPP given Λ(x).
Effect on moments:
	Total variance decomposes into:
	Conditional PPP variance.
	Additional variance due to randomness of Λ(x).
Thus:
Var(I)=\mathbb{E}[Var(I\mid\Lambda)]+Var(E[I∣Λ])

Result:
	Variance strictly larger than homogeneous PPP with same mean intensity (Afshang & Dhillon, 2021).
	Tails become heavier due to clustering or intensity randomness.
Tail impact:
	Higher joint exceedance probabilities.
	Elevated percentile levels compared to homogeneous PPP.
________________________________________
D. Cluster Processes (Neyman–Scott as Cox Special Case)
Model:
Parent PPP with offspring clusters.
Moment effects:
	Mean determined by parent intensity × mean offspring number.
	Variance inflated by intra-cluster correlation (Afshang & Dhillon, 2021).
Tail behavior:
	Higher probability of local extremes.
	Increased spatial correlation between observation points.
________________________________________
E. Analytical Tools Used
Tool	Purpose
Laplace functional	Derive distribution and moments of shot noise
PGFL (Probability Generating Functional)	Compute expectations over point processes
Campbell’s theorem	Compute mean and second moments
Pair-correlation function g(r)	Quantify clustering vs. PPP
Joint Laplace transform	Evaluate multi-point tail probabilities
Conditional variance decomposition	Separate PPP vs. intensity randomness
All of these tools are explicitly used in multi-point interference and cluster-process analyses (Ganti & Haenggi, 2022); (Afshang & Dhillon, 2021).
________________________________________
3) Summary Table
Process Type	Mean Scaling	Variance	Tail Behavior	Additional Correlation
Homogeneous PPP	∝ λ	∝ λ	Kernel-dependent	Minimal (independent)
Inhomogeneous PPP	∝ ∫λ(x)dx	Spatially varying	Elevated near hotspots	Localized
Cox process	∝ E[Λ]	Larger due to Var(Λ)	Heavier tails	Stronger
Cluster process	∝ λ_parent × n̄	Inflated by clustering	Higher extreme probability	High local correlation
________________________________________
4) Key Differences from Homogeneous PPP
	Spatial non-stationarity of moments.
	Variance inflation beyond λ-scaling.
	Elevated tail probability and joint exceedance.
	Increased multi-point correlation.
________________________________________
5) Gaps and Future Research
	Few direct applications to impedance-shaped supraharmonic kernels.
	Limited empirical validation of Cox-based tail predictions in electrical feeders.
	Sparse integration of spatially varying λ(x) derived from EVSE GIS datasets.
	Need for analytical percentile approximations under Cox models.
	Lack of standardized procedures to select homogeneous vs. inhomogeneous models in grid studies.
________________________________________

Q3: What sensitivity analyses quantify which spatial-uncertainty components dominate output uncertainty (intensity uncertainty, geometry uncertainty, PCC uncertainty), and what are common findings?
Recent stochastic-geometry and simulation-audit studies show that sensitivity analyses typically decompose output uncertainty into contributions from intensity (λ) uncertainty, geometry/clustering structure, and observation-point (PCC) location uncertainty, often using variance decomposition, correlation metrics, and convergence diagnostics. Interference-correlation analyses demonstrate that spatial intensity and clustering substantially inflate variance and tail probability compared to homogeneous assumptions (Ganti & Haenggi, 2022). Cluster-process modeling shows geometry uncertainty (cluster radius and offspring number) can dominate tail behavior even when mean intensity is fixed (Afshang & Dhillon, 2021). Convergence and global-sensitivity analyses indicate that parameter uncertainty in spatial intensity often contributes more to output variance than numerical discretization uncertainty (Sun et al., 2024).
________________________________________
2) Evidence Synthesis
A. Intensity (λ) Uncertainty
Sensitivity approach:
	Vary λ within plausible bounds and compute output variance or percentile shift.
Findings:
	In PPP shot-noise models, both mean and variance scale with λ, making intensity uncertainty a first-order driver of output uncertainty (Ganti & Haenggi, 2022).
	Sensitivity analyses show that small relative errors in λ can produce proportional shifts in expected aggregate interference.
Common finding:
	Intensity uncertainty often dominates mean-output uncertainty.
________________________________________
B. Geometry/Clustering Uncertainty
Sensitivity approach:
	Compare homogeneous PPP vs. cluster-process models while holding average intensity constant.
	Perturb cluster radius or offspring number.
Findings:
	Cluster processes increase variance and tail probabilities relative to homogeneous PPP with same mean density (Afshang & Dhillon, 2021).
	Geometry uncertainty primarily impacts extreme percentiles and joint exceedance probabilities.
Common finding:
	Geometry/clustering uncertainty dominates tail behavior more than mean behavior.
________________________________________
C. PCC (Observation-Point) Uncertainty
Sensitivity approach:
	Evaluate interference or shot noise at multiple spatial observation points.
	Quantify spatial correlation and joint variability.
Findings:
	Interference correlation decreases with distance but remains significant at short separations (Ganti & Haenggi, 2022).
	Output variance at a specific PCC depends strongly on its spatial relationship to clusters.
Common finding:
	PCC-location uncertainty affects percentile estimates and joint violation probability more than average levels.
________________________________________
D. Global Sensitivity and Variance Decomposition
Methodological tools:
	Variance-based sensitivity indices (e.g., Sobol-type methods).
	Convergence-based parameter importance assessment (Sun et al., 2024).
Findings:
	Dominant contributors often identifiable via first-order sensitivity indices.
	Interaction effects between λ and geometry parameters become significant at higher densities.
Common pattern:
	Mean uncertainty → dominated by λ.
	Tail/percentile uncertainty → dominated by clustering and PCC geometry.
	Numerical/discretization uncertainty → typically secondary.
________________________________________
3) Summary Table
Uncertainty Component	Sensitivity Method	Dominant Output Effect	Supporting Evidence
Intensity (λ)	Parameter sweep / variance scaling	Mean & RMS shift	Ganti & Haenggi, 2022
Geometry (clustering)	PPP vs cluster comparison	Tail inflation	Afshang & Dhillon, 2021
PCC location	Multi-point correlation analysis	Percentile variability	Ganti & Haenggi, 2022
Numerical parameters	Convergence diagnostics	Minor vs structural uncertainty	Sun et al., 2024
________________________________________
4) Common Findings Across Studies
	λ uncertainty → linear effect on mean.
	Cluster geometry → disproportionate effect on tails.
	PCC location → affects local extreme values.
	Interaction effects grow with density.
	Structural spatial assumptions dominate over numerical tolerance settings.
________________________________________
5) Gaps and Future Research
	Few feeder-scale empirical decompositions of spatial uncertainty components.
	Limited application of Sobol indices in supraharmonic modeling.
	Sparse joint calibration of λ and geometry parameters from measurement data.
	Need for standardized uncertainty-decomposition reporting in power journals.
	Limited integration of impedance uncertainty with spatial uncertainty.

3.5 Robustness to phase coherence and electrical mark enrichments
Q1: What measurements or studies quantify phase coherence/partial synchronization among converter emissions (common control, grid-locking artefacts), and at what frequencies is it relevant?

Recent empirical studies show that partial phase coherence among converter emissions can arise from grid-synchronous control, common switching-frequency bands, and shared impedance environments, particularly in the lower supraharmonic range (≈2–20 kHz). Field EVSE measurements reveal frequency-aligned current clusters across devices, suggesting structured spectral components rather than fully random-phase aggregation (Mariscotti, 2022). MV/LV analyses show that resonance and transformer response can amplify common-frequency components, effectively reinforcing coherence at specific bands (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024). Laboratory studies further demonstrate supply-voltage-dependent emission shifts, indicating control-coupled behavior that can influence phase alignment at switching-related frequencies (Frydlewicz et al., 2024).
________________________________________
2) Evidence Synthesis
A. Field Evidence of Frequency-Aligned Emission Clusters (EVSE)
Measurement context:
Supraharmonic current measurements at LV public charging points.
Findings:
	Distinct emission clusters appear in specific frequency bands across multiple EV chargers (Mariscotti, 2022).
	Spectral peaks are not uniformly distributed, indicating switching-frequency alignment.
Relevance to coherence:
	Common control architectures (e.g., grid-synchronous converters) may lock to similar switching or modulation schemes.
	Coherence most relevant below ~20–30 kHz where switching-frequency clusters dominate.
________________________________________
B. Resonance-Enhanced Reinforcement of Common Components
Measurement context:
MV/LV feeder distortion studies above 2 kHz.
Findings:
	Transformer frequency response and feeder resonance amplify narrowband components (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024).
	Amplification occurs at specific frequencies rather than broadband.
Interpretation:
	Even partial phase alignment at resonance-aligned frequencies can result in reinforced voltage distortion.
	Effective coherence is frequency-selective.
Relevant frequencies:
	Bands above 2 kHz, particularly resonance peaks.
________________________________________
C. Control-Coupled and Supply-Dependent Emission Behavior
Measurement context:
Laboratory experiments on CFL and LED devices (2–150 kHz).
Findings:
	Conducted emission magnitude and spectral position vary with supply voltage (Frydlewicz et al., 2024).
Implication for coherence:
	Grid voltage variations influence switching dynamics.
	Common grid conditions may partially synchronize emission patterns.
Frequency relevance:
	Lower supraharmonic range (2–20 kHz).
	Device-specific switching frequencies.
________________________________________
D. Indicators Used to Quantify Partial Coherence
While not always explicitly labeled “coherence,” studies implicitly quantify it using:
	Spectral peak alignment across devices.
	Narrowband clustering persistence across sites (Mariscotti, 2022).
	Frequency-selective amplification patterns (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024).
Common metrics:
	RMS magnitude of narrow frequency bins.
	Site-to-site spectral similarity.
	Distortion variability vs. short-circuit power.
________________________________________
3) Summary Table

Mariscotti, 2022	Field EVSE	2–150 kHz	Recurrent spectral clusters	Common switching frequency bands
Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024	MV/LV feeder	>2 kHz	Frequency-selective amplification	Resonance-enhanced reinforcement
Frydlewicz et al., 2024	Lab lighting devices	2–150 kHz	Supply-dependent spectral alignment	Control coupling to grid voltage
________________________________________
4) Frequencies Where Coherence Is Most Relevant
	2–20 kHz: switching-frequency bands and sidebands.
	Resonance frequencies of feeder/transformer systems.
	Narrow frequency bins where device families share modulation patterns.
Higher frequencies (>50–100 kHz) tend to show greater attenuation and reduced coherent reinforcement due to impedance filtering.
________________________________________
5) Gaps and Future Research
	Few direct phase-angle measurements across multiple converters.
	Limited use of cross-spectral coherence metrics in feeder studies.
	Sparse controlled experiments on synchronization under common grid conditions.
	Lack of quantitative coherence coefficients in supraharmonic planning studies.
	Need for joint time-domain phase analysis across multi-device deployments.

Q2: How do analytical models incorporate coherence (coherence factor, correlated phases, common-mode components), and how does this alter mean and variance scaling?

Analytical shot-noise and interference models incorporate coherence by introducing correlated marks (phases or amplitudes), common-mode components, or explicit interference-correlation terms, rather than assuming independent identically distributed (i.i.d.) phases. When phases are independent, the mean scales linearly with intensity λ while variance scales proportionally to λ; introducing phase correlation or common components adds covariance terms that inflate variance and can shift scaling toward linear-in-N growth instead of √N growth (Ganti & Haenggi, 2022). Cluster and Cox-process models implicitly incorporate coherence through spatially correlated intensity fields, which increase tail probabilities and joint exceedance rates relative to homogeneous PPP assumptions (Afshang & Dhillon, 2021). Empirical supraharmonic studies show that resonance and grid-coupled emissions act as frequency-selective common-mode mechanisms that reinforce correlated components (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024).
________________________________________
2) Evidence Synthesis
A. Independent-Phase Baseline (No Coherence)
Model assumption:
Marks M_i=A_ie^{j\phi_i}with independent phases.
Scaling properties:
	Mean aggregate field ∝ λ.
	Variance ∝ λ (finite second moment).
	RMS magnitude ∝ √N under cancellation.
These results are derived via Laplace functionals and second-moment measures for PPP shot noise (Ganti & Haenggi, 2022).
________________________________________
B. Correlated Phases and Covariance Terms
Model extension:
	Introduce non-zero covariance between marks.
	Replace independence assumption with correlated phase model.
Effect on variance:
Var⬚Mi=∑Var(Mi)+i≠jCov(Mi,Mj)

Result:
	Positive covariance terms inflate variance.
	Aggregate magnitude scaling shifts from √N toward N when covariance dominates.
Multi-point interference correlation analysis formalizes how correlation increases joint exceedance probabilities (Ganti & Haenggi, 2022).
________________________________________
C. Coherence via Cluster and Cox Processes
Mechanism:
	Spatial clustering introduces correlated contributions from nearby sources.
Analytical representation:
	Conditional Laplace functional given cluster center.
	Variance decomposition including intensity randomness.
Effect:
	Increased variance relative to homogeneous PPP.
	Elevated tail probability and stronger spatial correlation (Afshang & Dhillon, 2021).
Cluster processes act as an implicit coherence factor through local source concentration.
________________________________________
D. Common-Mode and Frequency-Selective Reinforcement
Empirical evidence:
	Resonance and transformer frequency response amplify narrowband components (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024).
Model interpretation:
	Introduce a deterministic common-mode term C(f)added to random shot noise.
Aggregate model:
I(f)=C(f)+\sum M_ih(x_i,f)\bigm
Effect on scaling:
	Mean increases by |C(f)|.
	Variance unaffected by deterministic term but relative dispersion decreases.
	If C(f) scales with λ (shared control), effective scaling becomes closer to linear.
________________________________________
E. Coherence Factor Approaches
In analytical modeling practice, coherence is often introduced through:
	Coherence coefficient ρ between marks.
	Phase-lock probability models.
	Mixed deterministic + random decomposition.
	Correlated fading/mark distributions in Laplace transforms.
Multi-point Laplace transforms explicitly incorporate correlation structure in deriving joint moments (Ganti & Haenggi, 2022).
________________________________________
3) Effect on Mean and Variance Scaling
Model Assumption	Mean Scaling	Variance Scaling	Tail Behavior
Independent phases	∝ λ	∝ λ	Moderate
Correlated phases	∝ λ	> λ (inflated)	Heavier tails
Cluster/Cox	∝ E[Λ]	Inflated via Var(Λ)	Elevated exceedance
Common deterministic component	∝ λ + C	Similar absolute variance but reduced relative dispersion	Increased extreme magnitude if coherent
________________________________________
4) Key Analytical Tools
	Laplace functional of marked point processes.
	PGFL with correlated marks.
	Variance decomposition (conditional expectation).
	Joint Laplace transform for multi-point interference.
	Pair-correlation function and covariance density.
These tools are explicitly used in interference-correlation and cluster-process analyses (Ganti & Haenggi, 2022); (Afshang & Dhillon, 2021).
________________________________________
5) Common Findings
	Coherence primarily inflates variance and tail probability.
	Mean scaling remains linear in λ unless common-mode components scale with density.
	Even weak positive phase correlation materially alters extreme percentiles.
	Cluster-based coherence is more impactful for tails than for means.
________________________________________
6) Gaps and Future Research
	Few supraharmonic-specific analytical coherence models in power journals.
	Limited empirical estimation of phase-correlation coefficients.
	Sparse feeder-scale validation of correlated-mark models.
	Need for percentile-closed-form approximations under correlated phases.
	Limited integration of impedance resonance with correlated phase models.
________________________________________

Q3: What enriched mark models (multi-class mixtures, state-dependent marks, admittance variability) are supported by data, and how do they change tail risk metrics?
Recent supraharmonic field and laboratory studies support enriched mark models that go beyond i.i.d. identical emissions, including multi-class mixtures (different converter types), state-dependent marks (operating-point dependent spectra), and frequency-dependent admittance variability. Field EVSE data show substantial inter-device spectral heterogeneity, supporting mixture-type mark distributions (Mariscotti, 2022). Laboratory experiments demonstrate that emission spectra shift with supply voltage, providing direct evidence for state-dependent marks (Frydlewicz et al., 2024). MV/LV studies further show that frequency-dependent grid impedance modifies effective mark contributions, implying admittance-weighted variability (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024). These enriched models typically inflate variance and increase extreme-percentile (e.g., 95th/99th) risk relative to single-class independent-mark assumptions.
________________________________________
2) Evidence Synthesis
A. Multi-Class Mixture Marks (Device Heterogeneity)
Empirical support:
	EV charger measurements show different emission magnitudes and spectral clusters across charger models and sites (Mariscotti, 2022).
Model implication:
	Replace single mark distribution with mixture:
M\sim\sum_{k} p_kF_k\bigm
where F_kcorresponds to device class k.
Effect on tail metrics:
	Heavy-tailed mixture components increase high-percentile values.
	Variance increases due to between-class dispersion.
Common finding:
	95th/99th percentile distortion rises more than mean when heterogeneity is included.
________________________________________
B. State-Dependent Marks (Operating-Point Variability)
Empirical support:
	Emission magnitude and frequency shift under different supply-voltage levels (Frydlewicz et al., 2024).
Model implication:
	Marks conditional on grid state S:
M\mid S\sim F_S\bigm
Effect on tail metrics:
	Introduces conditional variance component.
	Increases tail probability during stressed grid states.
	Leads to asymmetric risk distribution.
Common finding:
	Tail risk more sensitive to operating-state variability than mean levels.
________________________________________
C. Admittance Variability and Frequency-Dependent Effective Marks
Empirical support:
	MV/LV grid analysis shows frequency-selective impedance shaping distortion magnitude (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024).
Model implication:
	Effective mark becomes:
M_{\mathrm{eff}}(f)=M(f)\cdot Z(f)\bigm
Effect on tail metrics:
	Resonance frequencies amplify variance and percentile spread.
	Tail risk becomes frequency-localized rather than broadband.
Common finding:
	Admittance variability strongly shapes extreme distortion bands.
________________________________________
D. Combined Enriched-Mark Effects
When multi-class mixtures, state dependence, and admittance variability are combined:
	Variance decomposes into:
	Within-class variance.
	Between-class variance.
	State-dependent variance.
	Impedance amplification variance.
This layered variability significantly increases tail risk relative to homogeneous i.i.d. marks.
________________________________________
3) Summary Table
Enriched Mark Type	Empirical Basis	Modeling Change	Effect on Mean	Effect on Tail Risk
Multi-class mixture	EVSE heterogeneity (Mariscotti, 2022)	Mixture distribution	Slight mean shift	Increased high percentiles
State-dependent marks	Supply-voltage experiments (Frydlewicz et al., 2024)	Conditional distribution	Moderate	Strong tail sensitivity
Admittance variability	MV/LV resonance (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024)	Frequency-weighted marks	Frequency-selective mean shift	Strong local tail inflation
________________________________________
4) Common Findings Across Studies
	Device heterogeneity increases between-source variance.
	Operating-state dependence inflates conditional tail risk.
	Impedance weighting creates frequency-specific extreme amplification.
	Tail percentiles are more sensitive than mean values to enriched-mark effects.
________________________________________
5) Gaps and Future Research
	Few feeder-scale statistical fits of mixture models to real EVSE datasets.
	Limited joint modeling of state dependence and spatial clustering.
	Sparse extreme-value analysis for enriched-mark shot-noise fields.
	Need for standardized tail-risk reporting (e.g., 99th percentile vs mean).
	Lack of analytical closed forms for mixture-mark percentile scaling.

3.6 Beyond PPP: clustered and repulsive deployments and tail behaviour
Q1: What clustered point processes (e.g., Thomas/Neyman–Scott) and repulsive processes (e.g., Matérn hard-core, determinantal) are used to represent real spatial patterns in infrastructure/device placement?

Recent infrastructure and wireless-network studies use clustered point processes—especially Thomas and Neyman–Scott (Poisson cluster) models—to represent hotspot-style deployments such as small cells or hubs, and repulsive processes—including Matérn hard-core and determinantal point processes (DPPs)—to model planned or minimum-separation layouts. Cluster-process analyses show that Thomas/Neyman–Scott models capture spatial aggregation and elevated local intensity relative to homogeneous PPP assumptions (Afshang & Dhillon, 2021). Repulsive layouts, such as Matérn hard-core and DPP models, better represent infrastructure with enforced spacing and produce lower variance and interference tails than PPP models (Li et al., 2021). These models are widely adopted to approximate real spatial patterns in device placement and infrastructure planning.
________________________________________
2) Evidence Synthesis
A. Clustered Processes: Thomas and Neyman–Scott
Model structure:
	Parent PPP with intensity λ_p.
	Each parent generates offspring according to a spatial distribution (often Gaussian → Thomas process).
Empirical application:
	Poisson cluster (Neyman–Scott) models used to represent hotspot deployments in cellular networks and infrastructure aggregation (Afshang & Dhillon, 2021).
Key findings:
	Increased local intensity and heavier interference tails relative to homogeneous PPP.
	Better fit to real-world clustered layouts than uniform PPP.
Parameters:
	Parent intensity λ_p.
	Mean offspring per cluster.
	Cluster radius (variance parameter).
________________________________________
B. Repulsive Processes: Matérn Hard-Core
Model structure:
	Begin with PPP.
	Remove points closer than minimum distance r (Type I or II).
Application:
	Used to model infrastructure with regulatory spacing (e.g., base stations, planned facilities) (Li et al., 2021).
Key properties:
	Reduced variance compared to PPP.
	Lower interference tails due to enforced separation.
Relevance:
	Appropriate when siting constraints impose minimum spacing (e.g., substations, large charging hubs).
________________________________________
C. Determinantal Point Processes (DPPs)
Model structure:
	Repulsive process defined via kernel determinant.
	Naturally enforces soft-core repulsion.
Application:
	Used in wireless and infrastructure modeling to better match real deployments with regular spacing (Li et al., 2021).
Key findings:
	Lower variance of interference than PPP.
	Reduced extreme-event probability relative to clustered models.
________________________________________
D. Comparative Effects on Aggregate Metrics
Process Type	Mean Intensity	Variance	Tail Behavior	Spatial Pattern
Homogeneous PPP	Uniform	Moderate	Moderate	Random
Thomas/Neyman–Scott	Same average	Inflated	Heavier tails	Hotspots
Matérn Hard-Core	Reduced effective density	Lower	Lighter tails	Minimum spacing
DPP	Similar mean	Lower	Reduced extreme probability	Soft repulsion
Clustered processes increase tail risk and joint exceedance probabilities; repulsive processes dampen extremes and reduce variance relative to PPP.
________________________________________
3) Summary Table
Study	Process Modeled	Infrastructure Context	Key Finding
Afshang & Dhillon, 2021	Neyman–Scott (cluster)	Small-cell / hotspot deployments	Elevated local intensity & heavier tails
Li et al., 2021	Matérn hard-core, DPP	Cellular infrastructure	Repulsion lowers variance & extremes

4) Common Modeling Tools
	Probability Generating Functional (PGFL).
	Laplace transform of interference.
	Pair-correlation function g(r).
	Second-order product density.
	Joint exceedance probability derivations.
These tools are used to derive aggregate interference moments and tail probabilities under clustered and repulsive processes (Afshang & Dhillon, 2021); (Li et al., 2021).

	Few direct applications of repulsive processes to EVSE siting in power journals.
	Limited empirical fitting of Thomas vs. DPP models to real charging datasets.

Q2: What analytical results compare shot-noise tails under clustering vs PPP vs repulsion, and what mechanisms explain tail inflation/deflation?

Analytical results in stochastic-geometry literature show that shot-noise tails are inflated under clustered (e.g., Thomas/Neyman–Scott) processes and deflated under repulsive (e.g., Matérn hard-core, determinantal) processes relative to homogeneous PPP, when mean intensity is held constant. Cluster processes increase tail probability due to local concentration of points and positive spatial correlation, which elevate second-order product densities and joint exceedance rates (Afshang & Dhillon, 2021). Repulsive processes reduce tail mass because enforced minimum separation lowers local point density variance and decreases interference covariance (Li et al., 2021). Multi-point interference analyses confirm that positive spatial correlation inflates extreme-event probabilities, while negative correlation suppresses them (Ganti & Haenggi, 2022).
________________________________________
2) Evidence Synthesis
A. PPP Baseline: Reference Tail Behavior
For homogeneous PPP shot noise:
	Mean and variance scale linearly with intensity λ.
	Tail decay depends on kernel attenuation and mark distribution.
	No excess spatial correlation beyond Poisson randomness.
These properties are derived via Laplace functionals and second-moment measures (Ganti & Haenggi, 2022).
PPP serves as the neutral benchmark.
________________________________________
B. Clustered Processes (Thomas/Neyman–Scott): Tail Inflation
Analytical results:
	Cluster processes introduce parent–offspring structure with local point concentration.
	Interference variance includes additional intra-cluster covariance terms.
Mechanism:
Var(I)={\rm \operatorname{Var}}_{PPP}(I)+\mathrm{Cluster\ covariance\ term}\bigm
Consequences:
	Higher second-order product density.
	Elevated joint exceedance probability.
	Heavier shot-noise tails relative to PPP with equal mean intensity (Afshang & Dhillon, 2021).
Multi-point perspective:
	Spatial correlation increases with clustering, inflating tail probabilities (Ganti & Haenggi, 2022).
Intuition:
	Extreme events more likely when multiple sources concentrate near observation point.
________________________________________
C. Repulsive Processes (Matérn Hard-Core, DPP): Tail Deflation
Analytical results:
	Hard-core and determinantal processes enforce minimum spacing or negative correlation.
	Reduced local point density variance relative to PPP.
Mechanism:
	Lower second-order product density at small distances.
	Negative spatial correlation reduces covariance term in shot noise.
Consequences:
	Lower interference variance.
	Reduced high-percentile and extreme-event probability relative to PPP (Li et al., 2021).
Intuition:
	Minimum spacing limits simultaneous strong nearby contributors.
________________________________________
D. Comparative Analytical Tools
Across these studies, tail comparisons are derived using:
	Laplace transform of shot noise.
	Probability Generating Functional (PGFL).
	Second-order product density.
	Pair-correlation function g(r).
	Joint exceedance probability analysis.
Spatial correlation analysis explicitly links positive correlation to inflated tails and negative correlation to tail suppression (Ganti & Haenggi, 2022).
________________________________________
3) Summary Table
Process Type	Spatial Correlation	Variance vs PPP	Tail Behavior	Mechanism
Homogeneous PPP	None (baseline)	Reference	Baseline	Independent points
Thomas / Neyman–Scott	Positive (clustered)	Higher	Inflated tails	Local concentration & covariance
Matérn hard-core	Negative (repulsive)	Lower	Deflated tails	Minimum spacing
Determinantal (DPP)	Negative correlation	Lower	Reduced extremes	Soft repulsion via kernel determinant
________________________________________
4) Mechanisms Explaining Tail Inflation/Deflation
Tail Inflation (Clustering)
	Increased local point density near observation.
	Positive spatial covariance.
	Higher second-order moment measure.
	Enhanced joint exceedance probability.
Tail Deflation (Repulsion)
	Suppressed close-proximity interactions.
	Reduced covariance term in variance.
	Lower probability of simultaneous strong contributors.
________________________________________
5) Common Findings
	Mean interference can remain similar across processes with equal average intensity.
	Variance and tails are most sensitive to spatial correlation structure.
	Clustering primarily affects high percentiles, not just mean.
	Repulsion stabilizes extremes without significantly altering mean intensity.
________________________________________
6) Gaps and Future Research
	Limited direct comparison of tail quantiles across all three processes in power-system contexts.
	Sparse empirical validation in electrical infrastructure datasets.
	Few closed-form percentile approximations under repulsive processes.
	Limited extreme-value asymptotic analysis for clustered models in supraharmonic applications.
	Need for feeder-specific impedance-weighted tail comparisons.

Q3: In power applications, what deployment contexts plausibly induce clustering (public charging hubs, apartment complexes) or repulsion (planning constraints), and what data supports these claims?

Empirical power-system studies indicate that clustering is plausibly induced by public EV charging hubs, commercial parking facilities, and multi-dwelling residential complexes, where multiple converters co-locate on the same LV feeder. Field measurements of EV chargers show site-dependent distortion levels consistent with concentrated installations rather than uniform deployment (Mariscotti, 2022). Conversely, repulsion-like patterns arise in planned infrastructure subject to minimum spacing, capacity limits, or regulatory siting constraints, which are commonly modeled using hard-core or determinantal processes in infrastructure studies (Li et al., 2021). Cluster-process modeling shows that hotspot-style deployments better capture aggregated interference and tail behavior than homogeneous PPP assumptions (Afshang & Dhillon, 2021).
________________________________________
2) Evidence Synthesis
A. Clustering Contexts in Power Applications
1. Public Charging Hubs and Commercial Parking Facilities
Empirical evidence:
	Field measurements of EV chargers show distortion magnitudes vary by site, indicating local concentration of chargers rather than uniform distribution (Mariscotti, 2022).
Interpretation:
	Public fast-charging stations typically host multiple converters at a single PCC.
	Aggregated emissions align with hotspot-style clustering.
Model support:
	Neyman–Scott (Poisson cluster) processes are analytically used to represent hub-based deployments with parent–offspring structure (Afshang & Dhillon, 2021).
________________________________________
2. Apartment Complexes and Multi-Dwelling Units
Plausible mechanism:
	Shared LV feeders in residential blocks.
	Multiple home chargers installed within limited geographic footprint.
Supporting inference:
	Site-dependent distortion patterns and feeder variability suggest local concentration of converter loads (Mariscotti, 2022).
Analytical analog:
	Cluster models capture higher local intensity and increased variance relative to homogeneous PPP (Afshang & Dhillon, 2021).
________________________________________
B. Repulsion Contexts in Power Infrastructure
1. Planning and Regulatory Spacing Constraints
Context:
	Substations, distribution transformers, and major facilities often subject to minimum spacing or capacity constraints.
Analytical representation:
	Matérn hard-core and determinantal processes used to model infrastructure with enforced separation (Li et al., 2021).
Implication:
	Reduced local clustering.
	Lower interference variance compared to PPP.
________________________________________
2. Capacity-Limited Distribution Planning
Mechanism:
	Hosting-capacity studies and feeder constraints may limit high-density charger siting in close proximity.
Spatial analogy:
	Hard-core processes capture enforced minimum separation between large installations.
Expected outcome:
	Tail deflation relative to clustered deployments.
________________________________________
C. Data-Supported Spatial Patterns
Deployment Context	Observed Pattern	Supporting Evidence
Public EV hubs	High local density	Site-dependent distortion variability [(Mariscotti, 2022)]
Commercial parking	Multiple co-located converters	Cluster-process modeling relevance [(Afshang & Dhillon, 2021)]
Apartment complexes	Shared feeder concentration	Feeder variability patterns [(Mariscotti, 2022)]
Regulated infrastructure	Minimum spacing	Hard-core/DPP modeling of planned networks [(Li et al., 2021)]

	Grid-connection cost optimization.

3.7 Feeder-level benchmark validation
Q1: What feeder simulation tools and modelling choices are used for supraharmonic studies (frequency-domain network models, EMTP, impedance scan), and how are models validated?

Recent supraharmonic feeder studies use frequency-domain network models with impedance scans, EMTP-type time-domain simulations, and hybrid measurement-informed impedance models to capture 2–150 kHz behavior. MV/LV studies emphasize frequency-dependent transformer and line models and validate them through comparison with measured distortion spectra and impedance characteristics (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024). Field-based EVSE investigations validate models by directly comparing simulated and measured current/voltage spectra at PCCs (Mariscotti, 2022). Laboratory studies use controlled emission measurements to parameterize device models and verify simulation outputs against measured conducted emissions (Frydlewicz et al., 2024).
________________________________________
2) Evidence Synthesis
A. Frequency-Domain Network Models and Impedance Scans
Modeling approach:
	Construct feeder impedance models in frequency domain.
	Perform impedance scans (|Z(f)| vs frequency).
	Combine with device emission spectra to predict voltage distortion.
Evidence:
	MV/LV studies demonstrate the importance of modeling transformer and feeder frequency response above 2 kHz (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024).
Validation method:
	Compare simulated voltage distortion peaks with measured spectral peaks.
	Validate resonance frequencies and magnitude trends.
Common modeling choices:
	Frequency-dependent RLC equivalents.
	Detailed transformer leakage and stray capacitance representation.
	Linear network assumption for supraharmonic frequency band.
________________________________________
B. EMTP / Time-Domain Simulation
Modeling approach:
	Time-domain electromagnetic transient simulation (e.g., EMTP-type tools).
	Detailed converter models including switching behavior.
	Fourier analysis of simulated waveforms to extract 2–150 kHz spectra.
Empirical support:
	Laboratory studies measuring conducted emissions provide data to parameterize and validate time-domain models (Frydlewicz et al., 2024).
Validation method:
	Compare simulated emission spectra with measured LISN-based laboratory results.
	Assess magnitude error across frequency bins.
Common modeling choices:
	Detailed switching converter representation.
	Inclusion of control loops.
	High sampling rate to capture supraharmonic content.
________________________________________
C. Measurement-Informed Feeder Models
Approach:
	Use field measurements to estimate feeder impedance and distortion patterns.
	Fit simulation parameters to measured PCC data.
Evidence:
	EVSE field studies measure harmonic and supraharmonic emissions at PCCs and analyze feeder-dependent distortion variability (Mariscotti, 2022).
Validation method:
	Site-to-site comparison between predicted and measured distortion.
	Cross-check of short-circuit power vs distortion magnitude.
Common modeling choices:
	Simplified equivalent feeder impedance model.
	Empirical parameter calibration.
________________________________________
D. Hybrid Modeling (Frequency-Domain + Measured Emission Data)
Approach:
	Combine measured device emission spectra with modeled feeder impedance transfer functions.
Evidence:
	MV/LV distortion studies combine network modeling with measured distortion data to interpret resonance effects (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024).
Validation method:
	Compare predicted amplification bands to measured supraharmonic voltage peaks.
________________________________________

Mariscotti, 2022	Measurement-informed feeder analysis	2–150 kHz	PCC spectral comparison	Feeder equivalent impedance
Frydlewicz et al., 2024	Lab + time-domain modeling	2–150 kHz	Simulated vs measured emissions	Detailed converter model

Q2: How is the transfer-impedance kernel extracted or fitted from feeder models (impedance matrix reduction, injection tests, regression to kernel forms), and what uncertainty arises in extraction?



Recent supraharmonic feeder studies extract transfer-impedance kernels using (i) frequency-domain impedance scans of detailed feeder models, (ii) reduced nodal impedance matrices at PCCs, and (iii) measurement-based injection or spectral fitting approaches. MV/LV analyses compute frequency-dependent transfer characteristics from network models and validate them against measured distortion spectra (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024). Field EVSE studies infer effective feeder impedance by comparing measured current injections with resulting voltage distortion at PCCs (Mariscotti, 2022). Laboratory studies fit device–grid interaction models using controlled injection and regression against measured spectra (Frydlewicz et al., 2024). Uncertainty arises from parameter reduction, measurement noise, frequency discretization, and non-linear converter–grid coupling.
________________________________________
2) Evidence Synthesis
A. Impedance Matrix Reduction from Frequency-Domain Feeder Models
Method:
	Build detailed multi-node feeder model.
	Compute frequency-dependent nodal impedance matrix \mathbf{Z}(f).
	Reduce matrix to equivalent transfer impedance between injection node and PCC.
Evidence:
	MV/LV distortion study explicitly models transformer and feeder frequency response to evaluate supraharmonic propagation (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024).
Kernel form:
H(f)=Z_{\mathrm{PCC,inj}}(f)\bigm
Uncertainty sources:
	Parameter uncertainty in transformer stray capacitance.
	Cable modeling approximations.
	Linearization assumptions.
________________________________________
B. Injection Tests and Measurement-Based Extraction
Method:
	Measure injected current spectrum I(f).
	Measure resulting voltage spectrum V(f).
	Estimate transfer impedance:
\hat{H}(f)=\frac{V(f)}{I(f)}\bigm
Evidence:
	EVSE field measurements analyze current and voltage distortion at PCC to infer effective feeder impedance behavior (Mariscotti, 2022).
Validation:
	Compare extracted impedance behavior across sites with different short-circuit powers.
Uncertainty sources:
	Background distortion contamination.
	Limited signal-to-noise ratio.
	Time variability of feeder state.
________________________________________
C. Regression to Kernel Forms (Parametric Fitting)
Method:
	Assume analytical kernel form (e.g., RLC resonance structure).
	Fit parameters to measured or simulated spectra using least-squares regression.
Evidence:
	Laboratory emission studies compare measured conducted emissions to modeled supply interaction, implicitly fitting frequency response characteristics (Frydlewicz et al., 2024).
Typical parametric forms:
	RLC parallel resonance model.
	Multi-resonance transfer functions.
Uncertainty sources:
	Overfitting narrowband peaks.
	Frequency-bin resolution limitations.
	Model-structure mismatch.
________________________________________
D. Frequency Discretization and Numerical Issues
Observed practice:
	Impedance scans performed at discrete frequency bins (e.g., 200 Hz resolution in supraharmonic band).
Uncertainty effects:
	Missed narrow resonance peaks if resolution too coarse.
	Numerical interpolation error between bins.
Evidence for frequency-dependent amplification behavior underscores importance of accurate discretization (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024).
________________________________________
E. Nonlinear Converter–Grid Coupling
Issue:
	Transfer impedance extraction assumes linearity.
Evidence:
	Supply-voltage-dependent emission shifts indicate state-dependent interaction (Frydlewicz et al., 2024).
Implication:
	Extracted kernel may depend on operating condition.
	Apparent impedance varies with converter state.
________________________________________

Impedance matrix reduction	Simulated feeder model	Z(f) from nodal reduction	Compare to measured spectra	Model parameter uncertainty
Injection ratio V/I	Field PCC measurement	Empirical transfer function	Cross-site comparison	Noise & background distortion
Regression to RLC form	Lab + simulation	Parametric resonance model	Fit error minimization	Overfitting & discretization
Hybrid scan + measurement	MV/LV modeling	Frequency-domain transfer	Resonance alignment	Frequency resolution limits
________________________________________

Q3: What validation results in related literature compare analytical aggregation to feeder simulations across scenarios, and what accuracy/robustness is reported for RMS and high-percentile metrics?

Recent supraharmonic studies validate analytical aggregation concepts by comparing simplified frequency-domain aggregation (e.g., emission × feeder impedance superposition) against detailed feeder simulations and field data. MV/LV case studies report good agreement in RMS spectral trends and resonance locations, with deviations primarily near sharp impedance peaks (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024). EVSE field analyses show that simplified aggregation captures mean/RMS distortion behavior but may underpredict site-specific extreme peaks caused by local impedance or coherence effects (Mariscotti, 2022). Laboratory validation studies demonstrate that frequency-domain predictions align well with measured conducted emissions across operating conditions, though high-percentile variability increases under supply-voltage shifts (Frydlewicz et al., 2024).
________________________________________
2) Evidence Synthesis
A. Frequency-Domain Analytical Aggregation vs Feeder Modeling
Approach:
	Use linear superposition:
V(f)=Z(f)\sum I_i(f)\bigm
	Compare predicted voltage spectra to simulated or measured feeder responses.
Findings:
	Analytical impedance-based aggregation reproduces resonance amplification patterns observed in MV/LV simulations (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024).
	RMS spectral levels generally align when feeder impedance is accurately modeled.
Accuracy observations:
	Good agreement in broadband RMS distortion levels.
	Largest discrepancies near narrow resonance peaks.
________________________________________
B. Analytical Aggregation vs Field Measurements (EVSE Context)
Approach:
	Measure EV charger current emissions.
	Combine with feeder impedance assumptions to predict voltage distortion.
Findings:
	Field measurements show feeder-dependent distortion consistent with analytical aggregation logic (Mariscotti, 2022).
	RMS trends across sites match predicted dependence on short-circuit power.
Robustness observations:
	RMS metrics relatively stable across scenarios.
	Extreme peaks more sensitive to site-specific impedance.
________________________________________
C. Laboratory Validation Across Operating Scenarios
Approach:
	Controlled supply voltage variation.
	Compare modeled vs measured conducted emission spectra.
Findings:
	Frequency-domain modeling captures general spectral shape and magnitude (Frydlewicz et al., 2024).
	Operating-point shifts change spectral amplitude, affecting high-percentile metrics.
Accuracy observations:
	Mean/RMS agreement strong under nominal conditions.
	High-percentile variation increases under stressed supply conditions.
________________________________________

Across studies:
