95th percentile	Moderate agreement; sensitive to impedance peaks
99th percentile	Most sensitive to resonance and state variability
Common pattern:
	RMS metrics validate linear aggregation assumptions.
	High-percentile metrics expose sensitivity to local resonance, clustering, or state dependence.

Section 4 - Discussion
4.1 Mechanistic interpretation
Q1: What mechanistic explanations are offered in the literature for why resonance-rich impedance environments dominate supraharmonic outcomes even under random-phase cancellation?

Recent empirical and analytical supraharmonics studies explain that resonance-rich impedance environments dominate outcomes because frequency-dependent grid impedance selectively amplifies narrowband emissions near parallel/series resonance points, overwhelming random-phase cancellation between multiple converters. Field and modeling studies show that converter emissions interact with feeder impedance peaks, producing voltage distortion amplification even when current sources are phase-diverse (Mariscotti, 2022). MV/LV grid analyses further demonstrate that resonance phenomena and instrument-transfer characteristics above 2 kHz significantly shape observed supraharmonic voltages (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024). Controlled experiments also show that supply-voltage and impedance conditions shift emission spectra, reinforcing the dominance of network impedance over simple phase-aggregation assumptions (Frydlewicz et al., 2024).
________________________________________
2) Evidence Synthesis
A. Frequency-Dependent Impedance Amplification at Resonance
Mechanism:
Parallel or series resonance in LV/MV networks creates high-impedance peaks at specific frequencies, converting modest current injections into large voltage distortion.
	Field measurements of EV chargers show that supraharmonic voltage distortion depends strongly on network short-circuit power and impedance characteristics, indicating that impedance peaks—not phase coherence—govern observed voltages (Mariscotti, 2022).
	Grid-level analysis confirms that MV/LV resonance behavior and transformer frequency response significantly influence distortion propagation above 2 kHz (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024).
Implication:
Even if converter current phases are random, voltage distortion scales with |Z(f)|·|I(f)|; large |Z(f)| near resonance dominates.
________________________________________
B. Narrowband Converter Emission Coupling
Mechanism:
Modern converters emit clustered spectral components near switching frequencies and sidebands; if these align with network resonances, amplification occurs.
	EV charger measurements reveal distinct supraharmonic spectral clusters that interact differently depending on grid impedance conditions (Mariscotti, 2022).
	Experimental studies show that voltage variation shifts emission magnitudes and spectral distribution, implying sensitivity to impedance-mediated coupling (Frydlewicz et al., 2024).
Implication:
Resonant impedance “selects” and magnifies specific narrowband emissions, reducing effectiveness of broadband phase cancellation.
________________________________________
C. Reduced Effectiveness of Random-Phase Cancellation in Resonant Systems
Mechanism:
Random-phase cancellation assumes independent current phasors with similar magnitude and no frequency-selective amplification. Resonant networks violate this assumption.
	EVSE field data show that distortion magnitude varies more with network characteristics than with nominal device aggregation, suggesting impedance dominance over random aggregation effects (Mariscotti, 2022).
	MV/LV studies demonstrate that frequency-dependent transformer and line impedance modify phase relationships and amplitude, limiting simple cancellation assumptions (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024).
Implication:
At resonance, voltage distortion is dominated by impedance amplification rather than current phase statistics.
________________________________________
D. Supply-Voltage and Operating-State Sensitivity
Mechanism:
Converter emission spectra shift with supply conditions, and resonance peaks can be excited differently across operating states.
	Controlled lab experiments show conducted emission amplitude and frequency distribution vary with supply voltage changes (Frydlewicz et al., 2024).
Implication:
Resonant dominance is dynamic: small voltage or operating-state shifts can move emission peaks into resonance zones.
________________________________________
3) Summary Table
Study	Context	Method	Mechanistic Insight	Relevance to Resonance Dominance
Mariscotti, 2022	Public LV EVSE	Field measurement	Grid impedance governs distortion	Impedance > phase aggregation
Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024	MV/LV grids	Analytical + measurement	Transformer & resonance effects >2 kHz	Resonance-driven amplification
Frydlewicz et al., 2024	Lab LV lighting	Controlled experiment	Emission shifts with voltage	Impedance-emission coupling
________________________________________
4) Gaps and Future Research
	Limited feeder-scale multi-converter aggregation studies isolating resonance effects statistically.
	Few published impedance-frequency scans combined with long-term supraharmonic monitoring.
	Need for probabilistic models quantifying resonance dominance vs. random-phase aggregation.
	Sparse public datasets linking impedance spectra to distortion amplification factors.
	Greater integration of stochastic impedance modeling with field validation.

Q2: How do studies decompose contributions into “source variability vs network filtering vs spatial deployment,” and what diagnostic plots/metrics are used?

Recent supraharmonic and stochastic modeling studies decompose distortion contributions by separating (1) source variability (device emission spectra and operating states), (2) network filtering (frequency-dependent impedance and resonance), and (3) spatial deployment effects (aggregation across feeders or nodes). Empirical EVSE and grid studies isolate source spectra under controlled conditions and then compare them against field voltage distortion shaped by network impedance (Mariscotti, 2022); (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024). Controlled laboratory voltage-variation studies further isolate emission sensitivity to supply conditions, helping distinguish intrinsic source variability from network amplification (Frydlewicz et al., 2024). Variance decomposition and convergence diagnostics are used in stochastic modeling to quantify contribution shares and robustness (Saarela et al., 2024); (Sun et al., 2024).
________________________________________
2) Evidence Synthesis
A. Isolating Source Variability
Approach:
Measure device current emissions under controlled conditions and across operating states to quantify intrinsic variability.
	Field measurements of EV chargers characterize device-specific harmonic and supraharmonic current spectra, showing variability across charger types and load levels (Mariscotti, 2022).
	Controlled lab experiments vary supply voltage to isolate how intrinsic emission spectra change with operating conditions (Frydlewicz et al., 2024).
Diagnostic plots/metrics:
	Current spectrum magnitude vs. frequency.
	Stage-based emission comparison plots.
	Voltage-dependent emission shift curves.
________________________________________
B. Quantifying Network Filtering and Resonance Effects
Approach:
Compare injected current spectra with observed voltage distortion to infer frequency-dependent network transfer characteristics.
	Grid-level analysis shows that MV/LV resonance and transformer frequency response shape voltage distortion independently of source characteristics (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024).
	EV charger field studies demonstrate distortion dependence on short-circuit power and grid impedance rather than solely on device emission magnitude (Mariscotti, 2022).
Diagnostic plots/metrics:
	Frequency-dependent impedance magnitude |Z(f)|.
	Voltage-to-current transfer ratio plots (V/I vs. frequency).
	Amplification factor curves near resonance peaks.
________________________________________
C. Spatial Deployment and Aggregation Effects
Approach:
Assess distortion across nodes or under varying device aggregation to separate spatial deployment effects from single-source behavior.
	Field EVSE measurements across public LV networks show variability linked to feeder characteristics, implying spatial deployment influence (Mariscotti, 2022).
Diagnostic plots/metrics:
	Distortion vs. feeder short-circuit power plots.
	Node-to-node spectral comparison charts.
	Aggregated vs. single-device distortion comparisons.
________________________________________
D. Variance Decomposition in Stochastic Models
Approach:
Use hierarchical or separable variance decomposition to quantify contributions from structural components.
	Separable bootstrap variance estimation decomposes variance contributions in hierarchical models, offering a framework for separating source vs. structural effects (Saarela et al., 2024).
	Convergence-monitoring research recommends resolution refinement to verify that contribution estimates are numerically stable (Sun et al., 2024).
Diagnostic plots/metrics:
	Variance component bar charts.
	Sensitivity indices.
	Convergence curves for component estimates.
________________________________________

Saarela et al., 2024	Hierarchical stochastic modeling	Variance decomposition	Component variance charts	Structured contribution quantification

	Limited feeder-scale statistical decomposition combining spatial deployment and resonance modeling.
	Few studies quantify percentage contribution of each factor explicitly.
	Sparse integration of stochastic geometry with measured impedance spectra.
	Need for standardized diagnostic plot templates in supraharmonics research.
	Greater use of probabilistic sensitivity indices for resonance-dominated systems.

Q3: What evidence supports the relative importance of (i) kernel resonance, (ii) clustering, and (iii) coherence in driving extreme percentiles?

Recent supraharmonics and stochastic modelling studies indicate that (i) kernel resonance (frequency-dependent impedance peaks) exerts the strongest influence on extreme percentiles of voltage distortion, while (ii) spatial clustering and (iii) phase coherence act as secondary modifiers whose effects are conditional on the impedance environment. Field EVSE measurements show that extreme distortion levels correlate more strongly with grid impedance characteristics than with device aggregation alone (Mariscotti, 2022). MV/LV analyses confirm that resonance phenomena above 2 kHz shape voltage amplification, driving high-percentile outcomes (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024). Controlled experiments further show that source variability alone does not explain observed extremes without impedance amplification (Frydlewicz et al., 2024).
________________________________________
2) Evidence Synthesis
A. Kernel Resonance (Frequency-Dependent Impedance)
Evidence:
Extreme percentiles of voltage distortion are linked to impedance peaks rather than average current magnitudes.
	Field EV charger measurements demonstrate that voltage distortion magnitude depends strongly on short-circuit power and feeder impedance, implying resonance-dominated amplification (Mariscotti, 2022).
	MV/LV grid analysis shows that transformer and line frequency response above 2 kHz significantly modifies distortion propagation and can amplify specific bands (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024).
Mechanism for extremes:
Near resonance, voltage distortion scales with |Z(f)|·|I(f)|; if |Z(f)| peaks sharply, extreme percentiles are dominated by impedance rather than statistical averaging.
________________________________________
B. Spatial Clustering (Deployment Density and Topology)
Evidence:
Aggregation across feeders shows variability tied to network characteristics, indicating clustering influences distortion distribution but within impedance constraints.
	EVSE measurements across public LV grids show feeder-dependent distortion variation, suggesting that deployment density and topology modulate outcomes (Mariscotti, 2022).
Mechanism for extremes:
Clustering increases current magnitude statistically, but its effect on extreme percentiles becomes pronounced mainly when clusters excite resonant frequencies.
________________________________________
C. Phase Coherence (Random vs. Correlated Emission Phases)
Evidence:
Random-phase cancellation assumptions do not prevent extreme percentiles when impedance amplification dominates.
	Field studies show that distortion magnitude varies more with network impedance than with nominal device aggregation, indicating limited protective effect from random-phase diversity (Mariscotti, 2022).
	Experimental work demonstrates emission sensitivity to supply voltage and spectral shifts, suggesting that coherence effects are secondary to frequency alignment with impedance peaks (Frydlewicz et al., 2024).
Mechanism for extremes:
Even if phase coherence is low, resonance selectively amplifies aligned spectral components, driving high-percentile voltage levels.
________________________________________
3) Relative Importance Summary
Factor	Mechanism	Evidence Strength	Impact on Extreme Percentiles
Kernel resonance	Frequency-dependent impedance amplification	Strong (field + grid studies)	Dominant driver
Spatial clustering	Increased aggregate current injection	Moderate (field deployment variation)	Conditional amplifier
Phase coherence	Constructive interference of emissions	Weak-to-moderate (limited evidence)	Secondary unless resonant
________________________________________
4) Gaps and Future Research
	Lack of probabilistic decomposition quantifying percentage contribution of each factor.
	Limited multi-feeder extreme-percentile statistical studies.
	Sparse direct phase-coherence measurement campaigns at supraharmonic frequencies.
	Need for stochastic impedance modeling combined with field percentile validation.
	Few long-term datasets capturing rare-event supraharmonic extremes.
________________________________________

4.2 Engineering implications for planning, hosting capacity, and standards alignment
Q1: What planning/hosting-capacity approaches exist for converter-driven PQ issues, and how do they incorporate probabilistic criteria (percentile limits, risk constraints)?

Recent peer-reviewed studies on converter-driven power-quality (PQ) issues (harmonics and supraharmonics) show that hosting-capacity and planning approaches increasingly use probabilistic criteria, including percentile-based distortion limits, Monte Carlo risk assessment, and stochastic impedance modeling. Field-based EVSE studies demonstrate that distortion levels depend strongly on grid impedance and short-circuit power, motivating probabilistic rather than deterministic hosting thresholds (Mariscotti, 2022). MV/LV analyses highlight resonance-driven amplification, supporting risk-constrained planning under frequency-dependent impedance uncertainty (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024). Empirical emission variability studies further justify percentile-based limits rather than worst-case deterministic assumptions (Frydlewicz et al., 2024).
________________________________________
2) Evidence Synthesis
A. Deterministic vs. Probabilistic Hosting Capacity for Converter PQ
Approach:
Traditional deterministic hosting capacity assumes fixed emission levels and worst-case impedance. Recent studies show this is inadequate due to variability in converter spectra and network resonance.
	Field EVSE measurements show distortion magnitude depends on local short-circuit power and feeder impedance, implying hosting limits must reflect grid-specific variability (Mariscotti, 2022).
Probabilistic incorporation:
	Replace fixed thresholds with percentile limits (e.g., 95th percentile voltage distortion).
	Use feeder-specific impedance distributions in planning.
________________________________________
B. Percentile-Based Distortion Limits
Approach:
Instead of worst-case aggregation, planners evaluate compliance at high percentiles (e.g., 95th or 99th) of voltage distortion.
	Controlled emission studies show supply voltage and operating state shift emission magnitudes and frequencies, reinforcing the need for statistical representation of variability (Frydlewicz et al., 2024).
Implementation in planning:
	Compute distortion distributions under stochastic load and device states.
	Compare high-percentile distortion values to compatibility limits.
Metrics used:
	95th percentile total harmonic distortion (THD).
	99th percentile band-specific supraharmonic magnitude.
	Empirical coverage probability for compliance.
________________________________________
C. Risk-Constrained Planning under Resonance Uncertainty
Approach:
Incorporate frequency-dependent impedance uncertainty and resonance into hosting capacity analysis.
	MV/LV studies demonstrate that resonance behavior above 2 kHz significantly shapes voltage distortion, meaning hosting capacity depends on uncertain impedance peaks (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024).
Probabilistic incorporation:
	Treat impedance magnitude as random variable across feeders.
	Impose risk constraint:
P(\mathrm{Distortion}>\mathrm{limit})\le\alpha\bigm
where α is acceptable risk (e.g., 5%).
Metrics used:
	Exceedance probability curves.
	Amplification factor distributions.
	Sensitivity analysis vs. short-circuit power.
________________________________________
D. Stochastic Aggregation and Monte Carlo Evaluation
Approach:
Use Monte Carlo simulation of device deployment, emission variability, and feeder impedance to estimate hosting capacity.
	EVSE field variability across feeders implies that aggregation effects are stochastic and require simulation-based evaluation (Mariscotti, 2022).
Typical diagnostic outputs:
	Distribution of feeder voltage distortion vs. number of converters.
	Hosting-capacity curve (maximum number of converters vs. risk level).
	Probability of resonance excitation.
________________________________________
3) Summary Table
Study	PQ Context	Planning Implication	Probabilistic Element	Metric Used
Mariscotti, 2022	EVSE LV field study	Grid-dependent hosting limits	Feeder variability	Distortion vs. short-circuit power
Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024	MV/LV resonance study	Impedance-aware planning	Resonance uncertainty	Amplification vs. frequency
Frydlewicz et al., 2024	Controlled emission variability	Statistical emission modeling	Operating-state variability	Emission shift distributions
________________________________________
4) Gaps and Future Research
	Few explicit probabilistic hosting-capacity frameworks for supraharmonics above 2 kHz.
	Limited feeder-level extreme percentile studies.
	Sparse integration of stochastic geometry with impedance-frequency scans.
	Need for standardized percentile targets in supraharmonic planning.
	Lack of open datasets for validating probabilistic hosting models.

Q2: What standards or technical reports address supraharmonic emission limits, measurement, or compatibility considerations, and what gaps remain for aggregation under uncertainty?

Recent peer-reviewed studies indicate that supraharmonic emission and compatibility considerations are addressed indirectly through extensions of harmonic standards and technical guidance, but dedicated, harmonized limits for the 2–150 kHz range remain incomplete. Empirical work highlights gaps in standardized measurement methods above 2 kHz, including instrument transformer frequency response and traceability issues (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024). Field EVSE studies demonstrate that grid-dependent distortion variability complicates compatibility-based limit setting (Mariscotti, 2022). Controlled emission experiments further show that supply-voltage and operating-state dependence challenge fixed deterministic limits (Frydlewicz et al., 2024). Collectively, these findings suggest significant gaps in aggregation rules under uncertainty.
________________________________________
2) Evidence Synthesis
A. Measurement Standards and Instrumentation Gaps
Evidence from empirical studies:
	MV/LV grid investigations report that instrument transformers and measurement chains exhibit frequency-dependent behavior above 2 kHz, affecting observed supraharmonic levels (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024).
	Laboratory-to-field characterization of high-frequency power-quality measurement highlights calibration and traceability limitations in extended frequency ranges (Daaboul et al., 2025).
Implication for standards:
	Existing harmonic measurement frameworks do not fully specify bandwidth, binning, or uncertainty budgets for 2–150 kHz.
	Lack of harmonized instrument performance requirements above classical harmonic ranges.
________________________________________
B. Emission Limits and Compatibility Considerations
Evidence from EVSE and device studies:
	Field measurements of plug-in EV chargers show emission magnitudes depend strongly on feeder impedance and short-circuit power, complicating fixed emission limits (Mariscotti, 2022).
	Voltage variation experiments demonstrate that device emission spectra shift with operating conditions, indicating that compatibility limits may not reflect real-world variability (Frydlewicz et al., 2024).
Implication for standards:
	Fixed emission limits may not ensure compatibility across diverse feeder impedances.
	Compatibility assessment should account for statistical emission variability.
________________________________________
C. Resonance and Network-Dependent Amplification
Evidence:
	MV/LV analysis shows that supraharmonic distortion is strongly shaped by resonance phenomena and transformer frequency response (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024).
Implication for compatibility frameworks:
	Compatibility limits based solely on emission magnitude ignore impedance amplification.
	Standards lack probabilistic aggregation rules for resonance-rich feeders.
________________________________________
D. Aggregation Under Uncertainty
Evidence from field variability:
	EVSE field studies show distortion varies across feeders and operating conditions, implying that aggregation cannot be treated deterministically (Mariscotti, 2022).
Observed gaps:
	No harmonized percentile-based aggregation criteria for supraharmonics.
	Limited guidance on acceptable exceedance probability.
	Sparse integration of stochastic impedance modeling in compatibility planning.
________________________________________

	Limited percentile-based compatibility frameworks.
	Lack of aggregation rules incorporating impedance uncertainty.
	Sparse long-term feeder datasets for validating compatibility thresholds.
	Need for standardized uncertainty budgets in supraharmonic measurements.

Q3: What decision-support workflows are proposed to translate analytical exceedance probabilities into actionable screening limits (allowed density, siting constraints, mitigation requirements)?

Recent peer-reviewed work on converter-driven power-quality (PQ) issues proposes decision-support workflows that translate analytical exceedance probabilities into actionable screening rules by combining (i) field-calibrated emission models, (ii) impedance-aware network transfer analysis, and (iii) percentile-based risk constraints. EVSE field studies show that distortion depends on feeder short-circuit power and impedance, supporting screening limits expressed as allowable converter density per impedance class (Mariscotti, 2022). MV/LV resonance analyses motivate workflows that evaluate amplification factors and impose risk constraints such as P(\mathrm{distortion}>\mathrm{limit})\le\alpha(Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024). Controlled variability studies further justify incorporating operating-state distributions into percentile-based screening thresholds (Frydlewicz et al., 2024).
________________________________________
2) Evidence Synthesis
A. Step 1: Characterize Emission Variability (Source Model)
Workflow element:
Develop probabilistic emission models from field or lab data before applying network constraints.
	Field EV charger measurements demonstrate device-specific and grid-dependent supraharmonic emission spectra, implying that screening must be based on measured variability rather than nominal ratings (Mariscotti, 2022).
	Controlled voltage-variation studies show emission magnitude and spectral distribution shift with supply conditions, supporting inclusion of operating-state distributions in risk evaluation (Frydlewicz et al., 2024).
Decision-support output:
	Probability distribution of injected current spectrum.
	Stage-weighted emission model.
________________________________________
B. Step 2: Impedance-Aware Transfer and Amplification Assessment
Workflow element:
Map emission spectra to voltage distortion using frequency-dependent network impedance.
	MV/LV grid analysis shows that resonance and transformer frequency response significantly shape distortion, implying that screening must include impedance amplification factors (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024).
	EVSE field studies link distortion magnitude to short-circuit power, supporting impedance-based feeder classification (Mariscotti, 2022).
Decision-support output:
	Amplification factor curves \mid Z(f)\mid.
	Feeder impedance classes (strong vs. weak grids).
________________________________________
C. Step 3: Probabilistic Exceedance Evaluation
Workflow element:
Use Monte Carlo or analytical distribution models to compute exceedance probabilities.
	Field variability evidence shows deterministic worst-case limits are insufficient, supporting percentile-based evaluation (Mariscotti, 2022).
Typical risk formulation:
P(V_{SH}>V_{\mathrm{limit}})\le\alpha\bigm
Metrics used:
	95th or 99th percentile distortion.
	Exceedance probability curves.
	Sensitivity vs. converter density.
________________________________________
D. Step 4: Translate Risk into Screening Limits
Workflow element:
Convert allowable risk into operational constraints.
Common translations:
	Maximum allowed converter density per feeder.
	Siting restrictions in high-impedance or resonance-prone feeders.
	Mitigation requirements (filters, damping, impedance shaping) if exceedance probability > threshold.
Supporting evidence:
	EVSE distortion sensitivity to short-circuit power implies density limits should scale with feeder strength (Mariscotti, 2022).
	Resonance-driven amplification suggests mitigation in feeders with high impedance peaks (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024).
________________________________________
3) Summary Table
Workflow Stage	Evidence Source	Diagnostic Metric	Decision Output
Emission characterization	Mariscotti, 2022	Spectral variability	Probabilistic source model
Operating-state modeling	Frydlewicz et al., 2024	Voltage-dependent emission shifts	Stage-weighted distribution
Impedance transfer analysis	Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024	Amplification factor vs. frequency	Feeder classification
Risk evaluation	Mariscotti, 2022	Percentile distortion, exceedance probability	Risk-constrained threshold
Screening translation	All above	Distortion vs. density curve	Allowed density, siting rules
________________________________________
4) Gaps and Future Research
	Few fully integrated probabilistic hosting-capacity workflows for 2–150 kHz supraharmonics.
	Limited feeder-level validation of exceedance-probability thresholds.
	Sparse public datasets enabling percentile-based calibration.
	Need for standardized acceptable risk levels (α) in supraharmonic planning.
	Integration of stochastic spatial deployment models into screening tools.

4.3 Domain of validity and limitations
Q1: What known phenomena violate LTI superposition for supraharmonics (nonlinear converter interactions, control coupling, saturation), and how frequently are they observed?

Recent empirical supraharmonic studies show that several phenomena violate linear time-invariant (LTI) superposition assumptions, including nonlinear converter emission dependence on supply voltage and impedance, control-loop interactions between converters and the grid, and frequency-dependent transformer and measurement-chain saturation or resonance effects. Controlled laboratory experiments demonstrate that emission spectra change with supply voltage, indicating nonlinear source behavior (Frydlewicz et al., 2024). Field EVSE measurements show grid-dependent distortion amplification inconsistent with simple linear aggregation (Mariscotti, 2022). MV/LV studies further report transformer frequency-response and resonance effects above 2 kHz that alter distortion transfer characteristics (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024). Collectively, these effects are observed frequently in field and laboratory measurements, particularly in weak or resonance-rich grids.
________________________________________
2) Evidence Synthesis
A. Nonlinear Converter Emission Dependence
Phenomenon:
Emission magnitude and spectral distribution vary with supply voltage and operating state, violating LTI assumptions of fixed transfer behavior.
	Controlled experiments on CFL and LED devices show that conducted emission in the 2–150 kHz range shifts in amplitude and spectral location when supply voltage varies (Frydlewicz et al., 2024).
Frequency of observation:
Observed consistently across tested devices and operating conditions in laboratory studies.
Implication:
Source injection is not independent of grid state, invalidating strict superposition.
________________________________________
B. Grid–Converter Control Coupling
Phenomenon:
Converter control dynamics interact with feeder impedance and resonance, modifying emission spectra.
	Field EV charger measurements show distortion magnitude depends strongly on short-circuit power and grid impedance, implying dynamic coupling between converter control and network characteristics (Mariscotti, 2022).
Frequency of observation:
Reported across multiple field measurement sites in LV public charging networks.
Implication:
Voltage distortion cannot be modeled as simple linear sum of independent current sources.
________________________________________
C. Resonance and Frequency-Dependent Transformer Effects
Phenomenon:
Frequency-dependent impedance peaks and transformer response alter distortion propagation.
	MV/LV grid analysis shows that resonance and transformer frequency response significantly shape distortion above 2 kHz (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024).
Frequency of observation:
Identified in measured MV and LV network scenarios, particularly near resonance frequencies.
Implication:
Transfer characteristics are frequency-selective and may vary with loading or configuration, violating strict LTI invariance.
________________________________________
D. Measurement-Chain and Instrument Transformer Limitations
Phenomenon:
Measurement devices and instrument transformers exhibit frequency-dependent response, potentially saturating or distorting observed spectra.
	Experimental investigation of extended-frequency power-quality measurement reports limitations in measurement-chain behavior above traditional harmonic ranges (Daaboul et al., 2025).
Frequency of observation:
Observed in controlled measurement-chain characterization studies.
Implication:
Apparent non-superposition may arise from instrumentation nonlinearities or bandwidth limitations.
________________________________________
3) Summary Table
Phenomenon	Evidence Source	Observed Context	LTI Violation Mechanism	Reported Frequency
Voltage-dependent emission	Frydlewicz et al., 2024	Lab LV devices	Nonlinear source injection	Consistent across tests
Grid–control coupling	Mariscotti, 2022	Field EVSE	Impedance-dependent emission	Multiple feeder sites
Resonance & transformer effects	Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024	MV/LV grids	Frequency-selective amplification	Observed in measured grids
Measurement-chain limitations	Daaboul et al., 2025	Lab instrumentation	Frequency-dependent measurement distortion	Demonstrated experimentally
________________________________________
4) Gaps and Future Research
	Limited quantitative frequency-of-occurrence statistics for nonlinear coupling events.
	Few feeder-scale aggregation studies isolating control-interaction effects.
	Sparse long-term monitoring of supraharmonic control-induced instabilities.
	Need for joint modeling of nonlinear control and stochastic deployment.
	Lack of standardized criteria for identifying LTI violation thresholds.

Mariscotti, A., & Mingotti, A. (2024). The effects of supraharmonic distortion in MV and LV AC grids. Sensors, 24. https://consensus.app/papers/the-effects-of-supraharmonic-distortion-in-mv-and-lv-ac-mariscotti-mingotti/880a88b1b308517196d9f193e3b75f53/?utm_source=chatgpt

Q2: What limitations are identified for random-i.i.d. mark assumptions (device heterogeneity, correlated operating schedules, common impedance coupling), and what alternative models are suggested?

Recent supraharmonic and converter-emission studies show that random i.i.d. mark assumptions (independent, identically distributed device emissions) are often violated due to device heterogeneity, operating-state dependence, and common impedance coupling across feeders. Field EVSE measurements demonstrate substantial variability across charger types and grid conditions, contradicting identical-distribution assumptions (Mariscotti, 2022). Controlled experiments further show emission spectra depend on supply voltage and operating state, violating independence and stationarity assumptions (Frydlewicz et al., 2024). MV/LV grid studies reveal shared impedance and resonance effects that induce correlated voltage distortion across nodes (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024). Alternative modeling approaches include hierarchical variance decomposition and impedance-aware stochastic formulations (Saarela et al., 2024).
________________________________________
2) Evidence Synthesis
A. Device Heterogeneity (Non-Identical Marks)
Limitation:
Devices of the same class (e.g., EV chargers, LED drivers) exhibit diverse emission spectra and magnitudes.
	Field measurements of plug-in EV chargers show variability across charger models and grid locations, contradicting identical-distribution assumptions (Mariscotti, 2022).
Implication:
Marks should be modeled as heterogeneous distributions (mixture models) rather than identical draws.
Suggested alternative:
	Multi-class or mixture mark distributions reflecting device categories.
	Parameterized emission spectra by charger type or rating.
________________________________________
B. Correlated Operating Schedules and State Dependence
Limitation:
Emission characteristics depend on supply voltage and operating state, violating independence and stationarity.
	Laboratory experiments demonstrate that conducted emissions vary with supply voltage magnitude, showing state-dependent mark behavior (Frydlewicz et al., 2024).
Implication:
Operating states may be correlated across feeders (e.g., peak load times), invalidating i.i.d. scheduling assumptions.
Suggested alternative:
	State-conditioned marks (conditional distributions given voltage or load).
	Time-varying stochastic processes instead of static i.i.d. marks.
________________________________________
C. Common Impedance Coupling (Induced Correlation)
Limitation:
Devices share feeder impedance and resonance structures, inducing correlated voltage distortion even if current injections are independent.
	MV/LV studies show frequency-dependent impedance and resonance significantly shape distortion above 2 kHz (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024).
	EVSE field data link distortion to feeder short-circuit power, implying common-network amplification effects (Mariscotti, 2022).
Implication:
Voltage marks are spatially correlated via shared impedance, violating independence.
Suggested alternative:
	Impedance-weighted mark models.
	Spatially correlated stochastic geometry formulations.
	Feeder-class conditional distributions.
________________________________________
D. Structured Variance Decomposition as Alternative
Evidence:
Hierarchical variance decomposition frameworks separate structural sources of variability rather than assuming i.i.d. behavior.
	Separable bootstrap variance modeling decomposes variance components across hierarchical levels (Saarela et al., 2024).
Relevance to supraharmonics:
	Supports multi-level modeling (device → feeder → network).
	Quantifies contribution of heterogeneity vs. structural coupling.
________________________________________
3) Summary Table
Limitation of i.i.d. Marks	Empirical Evidence	Violation Mechanism	Suggested Alternative
Device heterogeneity	Mariscotti, 2022	Non-identical emission spectra	Mixture mark models
Operating-state dependence	Frydlewicz et al., 2024	State-dependent emission	Conditional/time-varying marks
Common impedance coupling	Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024	Spatially correlated voltage distortion	Impedance-aware spatial models
Hierarchical variability	Saarela et al., 2024	Multi-level variance structure	Hierarchical stochastic modeling
________________________________________
4) Gaps and Future Research
	Few feeder-scale empirical studies quantifying spatial correlation coefficients.
	Limited integration of time-correlated operating schedules in supraharmonic models.
	Sparse probabilistic impedance models for correlated-mark stochastic geometry.
	Need for public datasets enabling estimation of mark-dependence structures.
	Lack of standardized modeling guidance beyond i.i.d. assumptions.
________________________________________

Q3: What best practices exist for transparently bounding validity (assumption tests, sensitivity analysis, conservative envelopes), and how do reviewers typically expect them documented?
Recent empirical research on simulation, uncertainty quantification, and power-quality measurement shows that best practices for transparently bounding validity include: (i) explicit assumption testing and convergence diagnostics, (ii) systematic sensitivity analysis across parameter ranges, and (iii) conservative or envelope-based uncertainty bounds validated by empirical coverage checks. Large-scale audits of simulation studies demonstrate that reporting convergence failures, missingness, and design parameters is essential for credibility (Pawel et al., 2024). Convergence-monitoring research emphasizes documenting refinement diagnostics and stability checks (Sun et al., 2024). Finite-sample inference studies show that empirical coverage validation and calibrated bootstrap envelopes provide defensible conservative bounds (Jiang et al., 2024).
________________________________________
2) Evidence Synthesis
A. Assumption Testing and Explicit Domain-of-Validity Statements
Best practice:
Clearly state model assumptions and empirically test or justify them.
	A review of 482 simulation studies found that many failed to report handling of non-convergence and assumption violations; the authors recommend explicit documentation of assumption checks and failure rates (Pawel et al., 2024).
Reviewer expectation:
	Dedicated subsection listing assumptions.
	Quantified rates of convergence failure.
	Explicit statement of tested parameter ranges.
Documentation format typically used:
	Table of assumptions and validation checks.
	Reporting of convergence success percentage.
________________________________________
B. Convergence and Numerical Stability Diagnostics
Best practice:
Demonstrate estimator stability via resolution refinement, replication growth, or tolerance sweeps.
	Convergence-monitoring literature emphasizes plotting estimator value versus sample size or discretization resolution to demonstrate stability (Sun et al., 2024).
Reviewer expectation:
	Convergence curves (estimate vs. iteration count).
	Justification of tolerance thresholds.
	Evidence of asymptotic stabilization.
Common documentation:
	Log–log scaling plots.
	Residual error vs. refinement level graphs.
________________________________________
C. Sensitivity Analysis Across Parameter Space
Best practice:
Evaluate robustness of conclusions across plausible parameter variations.
	Simulation audits emphasize systematic exploration of input space and reporting of design parameters (Pawel et al., 2024).
	Convergence analysis research recommends stability assessment across discretization and model configurations (Sun et al., 2024).
Reviewer expectation:
	Sensitivity plots (output vs. key parameter).
	Ranked influence measures or variance contributions.
	Identification of “breakdown regions” where assumptions fail.
________________________________________
D. Conservative Envelopes and Coverage Validation
Best practice:
Provide conservative uncertainty bounds and empirically verify coverage probability.
	Calibrated bootstrap methods produce finite-sample-valid confidence sets that guarantee nominal coverage under stated assumptions (Jiang et al., 2024).
	Bootstrap comparison research highlights empirical coverage probability (CP) and interval width as primary evaluation metrics (Zrimvsek & Štrumbelj, 2024).
Reviewer expectation:
	Table of nominal vs. empirical coverage (e.g., 95%).
	Monte Carlo standard error reporting.
	Justification for conservative margin selection.
Typical documentation:
	Coverage probability tables.
	Envelope plots (analytical curve ± uncertainty band).
________________________________________
E. Transparent Reporting of Failure and Boundary Conditions
Best practice:
Explicitly report where the model breaks down or becomes unreliable.
	Only 23% of reviewed simulation studies reported non-convergence; the review recommends mandatory reporting of simulation failures (Pawel et al., 2024).
Reviewer expectation:
	Clear limitations subsection.
	Quantitative threshold where error exceeds acceptable bound.
	Discussion of conservative fallback assumptions.
________________________________________

	Few engineering journals mandate structured assumption-validation tables.
	Sparse reporting of empirical coverage in power-system modeling papers.
	Limited adoption of calibrated or finite-sample-valid uncertainty envelopes.
	Inconsistent documentation of parameter ranges and breakdown thresholds.
	Need for standardized “validity bounding checklist” in stochastic power research.

4.4 Spatial uncertainty: what matters most and how to model it
Q1: In spatial modelling literature for power systems, which uncertainty sources most affect aggregate metrics (intensity estimation, hotspot structure, geometry), and why?

In spatial aggregation studies relevant to power systems, hotspot (cluster) structure and local geometry typically affect high-percentile aggregate metrics more strongly than small errors in overall intensity, while intensity uncertainty primarily shifts mean/RMS levels. Clustered deployments inflate variance and extreme-event probability relative to homogeneous PPP assumptions because positive spatial correlation increases second-order moments (Afshang & Dhillon, 2021). Conversely, repulsive geometry reduces local covariance and tail mass compared to PPP (Li et al., 2021). Multi-point interference analyses show that spatial correlation structure (not just mean density) governs joint exceedance probabilities and extreme aggregates (Ganti & Haenggi, 2022).

2) Evidence Synthesis
A. Intensity (λ) Estimation Uncertainty
Mechanism:
	Mean aggregate ∝ λ.
	Variance under PPP ∝ λ.
Implication:
	Errors in λ primarily scale first- and second-order moments proportionally.
Evidence:
Shot-noise analysis under PPP shows linear scaling of mean and variance with intensity, indicating that misestimating λ shifts aggregate levels but does not fundamentally change tail structure (Ganti & Haenggi, 2022).
Effect on metrics:
	RMS moderately sensitive.
	High-percentile sensitivity proportional, but not structurally altered.
________________________________________
B. Hotspot / Cluster Structure
Mechanism:
	Cluster processes introduce parent–offspring concentration.
	Additional intra-cluster covariance term in variance.
{\rm \operatorname{Var}}_{cluster}(I)={\rm \operatorname{Var}}_{PPP}(I)+\mathrm{Cluster\ covariance}\bigm
Evidence:
Poisson cluster (Neyman–Scott) models show heavier interference tails and increased exceedance probability relative to PPP with equal average intensity (Afshang & Dhillon, 2021).
Impact:
	High-percentile (95th/99th) values increase disproportionately.
	Joint exceedance probability increases strongly (Ganti & Haenggi, 2022).
Conclusion:
Hotspot structure often dominates extreme-metric uncertainty.
________________________________________
C. Spatial Geometry (Repulsion vs Random vs Clustered)
Mechanism:
	Pair-correlation function g(r) modifies second-order product density.
	Repulsion reduces probability of close pairs.
Evidence:
Hard-core and determinantal processes reduce variance and tail mass compared to PPP due to negative spatial correlation (Li et al., 2021).
Impact:
	Reduced extreme values.
	Lower sensitivity of high-percentile metrics.
Conclusion:
Geometry uncertainty (clustered vs repulsive) changes tail behavior more than small λ shifts.
________________________________________
D. Multi-Point and Joint Exceedance Sensitivity
Mechanism:
	Correlation between observation points depends on spatial structure.
Evidence:
Spatial and temporal interference correlation analysis shows correlation structure governs joint exceedance probability more strongly than mean intensity alone (Ganti & Haenggi, 2022).
Implication for power applications:
	Feeder-level extreme distortion more sensitive to hotspot uncertainty than to small density misestimation.
________________________________________
3) Comparative Sensitivity Summary
Uncertainty Source	Mean Effect	RMS Effect	High-Percentile Effect	Mechanism
Intensity λ error	Linear scaling	Moderate	Proportional scaling	First-order moment shift
Hotspot structure	Minor mean shift	Increased variance	Strong tail inflation	Positive covariance
Repulsive geometry	Minor mean shift	Reduced variance	Tail deflation	Negative correlation
Geometry of observation window	Small mean impact	Moderate	Edge effects	Finite-region bias
________________________________________
4) Common Findings Across Studies
	Tail metrics more sensitive to spatial correlation than to average density.
	Cluster parameter uncertainty (cluster radius, offspring variance) heavily influences extremes.
	Repulsive modeling stabilizes aggregates and reduces extreme risk.
	Second-order statistics (pair-correlation) drive uncertainty in percentiles.
________________________________________
5) Gaps and Future Research
	Limited direct application of these sensitivity findings in feeder-level supraharmonic studies.
	Sparse quantitative decomposition of variance contributions in power-system contexts.
	Few Bayesian uncertainty propagation studies incorporating spatial-structure uncertainty.
	Limited empirical fitting of cluster vs repulsive models to EVSE datasets.
	Need for standardized sensitivity metrics (e.g., Sobol indices) in spatial power modeling.
________________________________________

IEEE Transactions on Wireless Communications. https://consensus.app/papers/poisson-cluster-process-based-analysis-of-downlink-afshang-dhillon/5e46a9e25e6f58a7bdb3c03c2eaa1b8a/?utm_source=chatgpt
Ganti, R. K., & Haenggi, M. (2022). Spatial and temporal correlation of the interference in wireless networks. IEEE Transactions on Communications. https://consensus.app/papers/spatial-and-temporal-correlation-of-the-interference-ganti-haenggi/9b3f9b7f1e3757a68c8cbb6d1dfeac4e/?utm_source=chatgpt
Li, X., Zhang, J., Andrews, J. G., & Haenggi, M. (2021). Spatial modeling and analysis of cellular networks using determinantal and hard-core point processes. IEEE Transactions on Communications. https://consensus.app/papers/spatial-modeling-and-analysis-of-cellular-networks-li-zhang/5a4e0f8b52e3567dbaacac73dbb1e8e1/?utm_source=chatgpt

Q2: What model-selection criteria are used to choose between PPP, inhomogeneous PPP, Cox, cluster, or hard-core processes (goodness-of-fit, AIC/BIC-like criteria, cross-validation with spatial data)?

Recent spatial-modeling studies relevant to infrastructure and networked systems select between PPP, inhomogeneous PPP, Cox/cluster, and hard-core processes using second-order goodness-of-fit diagnostics (pair-correlation, Ripley’s K/L functions), likelihood-based criteria (AIC/BIC), and predictive cross-validation of interference or coverage metrics. Cluster processes are favored when empirical pair-correlation exceeds unity at short distances, indicating hotspot structure (Afshang & Dhillon, 2021). Repulsive models (e.g., Matérn hard-core or determinantal) are selected when empirical data show suppressed short-range interaction and lower-than-Poisson variance (Li et al., 2021). Multi-point interference validation further evaluates models by comparing predicted joint exceedance probabilities to empirical correlations (Ganti & Haenggi, 2022).
________________________________________
2) Evidence Synthesis
A. Second-Order Goodness-of-Fit Diagnostics
Tools used:
	Pair-correlation function g(r)
	Ripley’s K/L functions
	Second-order product density
Selection logic:
	g(r)>1at small r → clustering → cluster/Cox model
	g(r)<1at small r → repulsion → hard-core/DPP
	g(r)\approx1→ PPP
Evidence:
Cluster-based modeling is motivated by spatial correlation beyond PPP baseline (Afshang & Dhillon, 2021).
Repulsive models are validated by showing reduced short-range interaction relative to PPP (Li et al., 2021).
________________________________________
B. Likelihood-Based Criteria (AIC/BIC)
Approach:
	Estimate model parameters via maximum likelihood.
	Compare candidate models using AIC/BIC-type criteria.
Rationale:
	Penalizes over-parameterized Cox/cluster models.
	Supports selection between homogeneous and inhomogeneous PPP.
Evidence context:
Spatial modeling studies comparing cluster vs PPP vs repulsive models employ parametric fitting frameworks where model complexity must be justified (Li et al., 2021).
________________________________________
C. Predictive Cross-Validation on Aggregate Metrics
Approach:
	Fit spatial model to location data.
	Predict interference or aggregate metrics.
	Compare predicted vs simulated or empirical distributions.
Evidence:
Multi-point interference analysis evaluates models by matching predicted spatial correlation and joint exceedance to empirical behavior (Ganti & Haenggi, 2022).
Application to power systems:
	Validate chosen spatial model by comparing RMS or percentile distortion under Monte Carlo simulation.
________________________________________
D. Intensity Surface Testing (Homogeneous vs Inhomogeneous PPP)
Approach:
	Estimate spatial intensity surface \lambda(x).
	Test for significant spatial trend.
Model selection rule:
	Significant spatial intensity gradient → inhomogeneous PPP.
	Residual clustering beyond intensity trend → Cox or cluster process.
Cluster modeling literature distinguishes pure inhomogeneity from second-order clustering (Afshang & Dhillon, 2021).
________________________________________
3) Comparative Criteria Summary
Criterion	PPP	Inhomogeneous PPP	Cox/Cluster	Hard-Core/DPP
g(r) ≈ 1	✔	✔ (after detrending)	✖	✖
g(r) > 1 (short r)	✖	✖	✔	✖
g(r) < 1 (short r)	✖	✖	✖	✔
AIC/BIC lower	If simplest adequate	If intensity varies	If clustering justified	If repulsion evident
Cross-validation accuracy	Baseline	Improved with trend	Improved for tails	Improved for suppressed extremes
________________________________________
4) Common Findings Across Studies
	PPP often rejected when short-range clustering evident.
	Inhomogeneous PPP adequate when variation due to large-scale density gradient, not local hotspots.
	Cox/cluster models preferred when second-order clustering persists after detrending.
	Hard-core/DPP models selected when infrastructure shows regulated spacing.
	Predictive validation of tail metrics is increasingly used as secondary model-selection criterion.
________________________________________
5) Gaps and Future Research
	Few published AIC/BIC comparisons in power-system spatial modeling contexts.
	Limited standardized spatial-diagnostic reporting in EVSE studies.
	Sparse cross-validation using percentile distortion metrics.
	Need for formal goodness-of-fit frameworks tailored to feeder-level applications.
	Limited uncertainty quantification for model-selection error.
________________________________________
6) Bibliography (APA 7)
Afshang, M., & Dhillon, H. S. (2021). Poisson cluster process-based analysis of downlink cellular networks with multi-antenna base stations. IEEE Transactions on Wireless Communications. https://consensus.app/papers/poisson-cluster-process-based-analysis-of-downlink-afshang-dhillon/5e46a9e25e6f58a7bdb3c03c2eaa1b8a/?utm_source=chatgpt
Ganti, R. K., & Haenggi, M. (2022). Spatial and temporal correlation of the interference in wireless networks. IEEE Transactions on Communications. https://consensus.app/papers/spatial-and-temporal-correlation-of-the-interference-ganti-haenggi/9b3f9b7f1e3757a68c8cbb6d1dfeac4e/?utm_source=chatgpt
Li, X., Zhang, J., Andrews, J. G., & Haenggi, M. (2021). Spatial modeling and analysis of cellular networks using determinantal and hard-core point processes. IEEE Transactions on Communications. https://consensus.app/papers/spatial-modeling-and-analysis-of-cellular-networks-li-zhang/5a4e0f8b52e3567dbaacac73dbb1e8e1/?utm_source=chatgpt
Q3: What practical guidance exists for practitioners when only limited spatial data are available (default priors, conservative assumptions, scenario envelopes)?
When spatial data are limited, recent infrastructure and aggregation studies recommend (i) starting from a homogeneous PPP baseline with conservative intensity bounds, (ii) testing cluster and repulsive envelope scenarios to bracket tail risk, and (iii) validating models against aggregate metrics (RMS or exceedance probability) rather than exact point patterns. Cluster-process analyses show that hotspot assumptions provide conservative upper-tail estimates relative to PPP (Afshang & Dhillon, 2021). Repulsive (hard-core/DPP) models offer lower-bound envelopes for interference or aggregate distortion metrics (Li et al., 2021). Multi-point interference studies further emphasize validating predicted correlation and exceedance probabilities rather than relying solely on fitted intensity (Ganti & Haenggi, 2022).
________________________________________
2) Evidence Synthesis
A. Default Baseline: Homogeneous PPP with Intensity Bounds
Practical guidance:
	Use PPP as neutral baseline when no spatial structure data exist.
	Estimate λ from limited counts or planning data.
	Apply conservative ± bounds on λ to reflect uncertainty.
Rationale:
PPP provides analytically tractable mean and variance scaling (Ganti & Haenggi, 2022).
Implication:
	Produces central estimate for RMS metrics.
	Serves as reference model for envelope comparison.
________________________________________
B. Conservative Upper Envelope: Cluster (Hotspot) Scenarios
Guidance:
	When deployment uncertainty includes possible hubs or co-location, test a Thomas/Neyman–Scott cluster model.
	Use plausible cluster radius and offspring count ranges.
Evidence:
Cluster processes inflate variance and tail probability compared to PPP with equal mean intensity (Afshang & Dhillon, 2021).
Practical implication:
	Provides conservative high-percentile (e.g., 95th/99th) envelope.
	Appropriate when hotspot formation cannot be ruled out.
________________________________________
C. Lower Envelope: Repulsive (Hard-Core or DPP) Assumptions
Guidance:
	If planning constraints imply minimum spacing, model using Matérn hard-core or DPP.
	Provides lower-bound extreme metrics.
Evidence:
Repulsive models reduce variance and tail mass relative to PPP (Li et al., 2021).
Practical implication:
	Brackets risk below PPP baseline.
	Useful when infrastructure siting rules are enforced.
________________________________________
D. Scenario Envelope Approach
Recommended workflow under limited data:
	PPP baseline (central estimate).
	Cluster scenario (upper-tail bound).
	Hard-core scenario (lower-tail bound).
	Compare RMS and percentile metrics across scenarios.
Rationale:
Spatial correlation governs extreme behavior more than mean intensity (Ganti & Haenggi, 2022).
________________________________________
E. Validation Against Aggregate Metrics
Guidance:
	When point locations unavailable, validate model by matching aggregate interference/distortion metrics.
	Compare predicted RMS and exceedance probability to measured feeder/site data.
Multi-point correlation studies highlight that matching second-order behavior (variance, correlation) is more informative than matching mean alone (Ganti & Haenggi, 2022).
________________________________________
3) Summary Table
Data Availability	Recommended Model	Purpose	Risk Orientation
Very limited (counts only)	PPP with λ bounds	Baseline	Neutral
Possible hotspots	Thomas/Neyman–Scott	Upper percentile bound	Conservative (high)
Planned spacing constraints	Hard-core/DPP	Lower percentile bound	Conservative (low)
Limited validation data	Compare RMS & exceedance metrics	Model screening	Robustness check
________________________________________
4) Common Findings Across Studies
	Spatial-structure uncertainty affects tail metrics more than intensity uncertainty.
	Cluster scenarios consistently produce heavier tails.
	Repulsive scenarios consistently reduce extreme-event probability.
	Envelope analysis is more robust than single-model selection under sparse data.
________________________________________
5) Gaps and Future Research
	Few explicit guidelines published in power-quality journals.
	Limited Bayesian priors developed specifically for EVSE spatial modeling.
	Sparse empirical datasets for fitting cluster parameters in distribution networks.
	Need for standardized “spatial scenario envelope” reporting in planning studies.
	Limited integration of impedance-aware distortion modeling with spatial uncertainty envelopes.
________________________________________

IEEE Transactions on Communications. https://consensus.app/papers/spatial-modeling-and-analysis-of-cellular-networks-li-zhang/5a4e0f8b52e3567dbaacac73dbb1e8e1/?utm_source=chatgpt

4.5 Research extensions
Q1: What work exists on multi-observation-point (multi-PCC) coupled aggregates in stochastic geometry (vector shot noise, interference correlation), and how might it translate to distribution networks?

Recent stochastic-geometry research on multi-point interference correlation and vector shot-noise processes provides analytical tools directly relevant to multi-PCC (point-of-common-coupling) distortion analysis in distribution networks. Studies on interference correlation in Poisson networks show that aggregate shot noise observed at multiple spatial locations is statistically coupled through common source realizations and shared propagation kernels (Afshang & Dhillon, 2021). Work on vector shot-noise and joint Laplace transforms characterizes joint distributions of interference at multiple receivers, quantifying correlation coefficients and joint exceedance probabilities (Ganti & Haenggi, 2022). These results translate to distribution networks by modeling converter injections as spatial point processes and feeder impedance as the propagation kernel, enabling joint percentile or exceedance-risk assessment across multiple PCCs.
________________________________________
2) Evidence Synthesis
A. Vector Shot Noise and Joint Laplace Transforms
Core idea:
Aggregate interference at multiple observation points can be modeled as a vector shot-noise process with a shared underlying point process.
	Multi-receiver interference analysis derives joint Laplace transforms and correlation metrics for shot noise observed at spatially separated receivers, explicitly quantifying cross-correlation due to shared transmitters (Ganti & Haenggi, 2022).
Relevance to distribution networks:
	Converters → spatial points.
	Impedance magnitude |Z(f,x)| → propagation kernel.
	Voltage distortion at PCCs → vector shot noise.
	Enables joint exceedance probability P(V_1>\tau_1,V_2>\tau_2).
Diagnostic metrics used:
	Joint Laplace transform.
	Interference correlation coefficient.
	Joint coverage probability.
________________________________________
B. Interference Correlation in Clustered Point Processes
Core idea:
Clustering of transmitters increases spatial correlation of aggregate interference.
	Poisson cluster process models show that interference correlation between receivers increases with clustering intensity and proximity (Afshang & Dhillon, 2021).
Translation to distribution grids:
	EVSE clusters on feeders resemble clustered point processes.
	Nearby PCCs share many upstream converters, inducing correlated distortion.
	Clustering raises probability of simultaneous extreme percentiles.
Metrics used:
	Spatial correlation coefficient vs. distance.
	Joint outage probability.
________________________________________
C. Multi-Point Interference Correlation and Joint Extremes
Core idea:
Even if marginal distributions are known, joint exceedance probabilities require explicit correlation modeling.
	Multi-point interference work shows that neglecting correlation underestimates joint outage probability and tail dependence (Ganti & Haenggi, 2022).
Distribution-network implication:
	Two PCCs on the same feeder may exhibit correlated supraharmonic voltage extremes.
	Planning based on independent percentile limits may underestimate simultaneous violations.
________________________________________
D. Mapping Propagation Kernels to Impedance Kernels
Wireless kernel: Path-loss function \ell(r).
Distribution analog: Frequency-dependent impedance magnitude \mid Z(f,x)\mid.
Translation:
	Replace radio path loss with feeder impedance transfer function.
	Use joint Laplace transform of vector distortion process.
	Compute joint percentile surfaces across PCCs.
________________________________________
3) Summary Table
Study	Model Type	Multi-Observation Feature	Key Metric	Translation to Distribution Networks
Ganti & Haenggi, 2022	Vector shot noise	Joint interference at multiple receivers	Joint Laplace transform, correlation coefficient	Multi-PCC joint distortion modeling
Afshang & Dhillon, 2021	Poisson cluster process	Correlated interference via clustering	Spatial correlation vs. distance	EVSE clustering on feeders
Ganti & Haenggi, 2022	Interference correlation	Joint exceedance probabilities	Tail dependence metrics	Simultaneous PCC violation risk
________________________________________
4) Gaps and Future Research
	Few direct applications of vector shot-noise models to electrical impedance networks.
	Limited empirical validation of multi-PCC supraharmonic correlation.
	Need for impedance-aware joint Laplace-transform derivations.
	Sparse feeder-scale datasets for estimating distortion correlation coefficients.
	Lack of joint percentile-based planning standards for multiple PCCs.
________________________________________
5) Bibliography (APA 7)
Afshang, M., & Dhillon, H. S. (2021). Poisson cluster process-based analysis of downlink cellular networks with multi-antenna base stations. IEEE Transactions on Wireless Communications. https://consensus.app/papers/poisson-cluster-process-based-analysis-of-downlink-afshang-dhillon/5e46a9e25e6f58a7bdb3c03c2eaa1b8a/?utm_source=chatgpt
Ganti, R. K., & Haenggi, M. (2022). Spatial and temporal correlation of the interference in wireless networks. IEEE Transactions on Communications. https://consensus.app/papers/spatial-and-temporal-correlation-of-the-interference-ganti-haenggi/9b3f9b7f1e3757a68c8cbb6d1dfeac4e/?utm_source=chatgpt
________________________________________
Q2: What models combine spatial point processes with temporal activity processes (doubly stochastic spatio-temporal processes, marked renewal activity), and what inference methods are used?
Recent stochastic-geometry research combines spatial point processes with temporal activity through doubly stochastic (Cox) spatio-temporal processes, Poisson cluster processes with time-varying intensities, and marked renewal or ON/OFF activity models. Wireless-network studies model transmitters as spatial PPPs with temporally correlated activity marks, deriving joint spatio-temporal interference distributions and correlation functions (Ganti & Haenggi, 2022). Cluster-based models incorporate spatial clustering with temporal activation probabilities or renewal traffic processes to capture burstiness and dependence (Afshang & Dhillon, 2021). Inference methods include Laplace-functional derivations, moment-based estimators, likelihood-based Cox-process inference, and Monte Carlo validation of spatio-temporal correlation metrics.
________________________________________
2) Evidence Synthesis
A. Doubly Stochastic (Cox) Spatio-Temporal Point Processes
Model structure:
A spatial point process whose intensity itself evolves randomly over time (Λ(x,t)).
	Spatio-temporal interference studies derive joint spatial and temporal correlation functions for networks where transmitter activity evolves over time, capturing non-independent marks (Ganti & Haenggi, 2022).
Inference methods:
	Joint Laplace functional of interference field.
	Closed-form correlation functions.
	Monte Carlo simulation for validation.
Relevance:
	Suitable for converter fleets whose activation intensity varies with load cycles.
________________________________________
B. Spatial Cluster Processes with Temporal Activity Marks
Model structure:
Poisson cluster processes (e.g., Neyman–Scott) combined with probabilistic activity or renewal processes.
	Cluster-process analyses quantify interference correlation and coverage under clustered transmitter locations (Afshang & Dhillon, 2021).
Extension with temporal marks:
	Each spatial point assigned ON/OFF activity probability or renewal-driven duty cycle.
	Temporal correlation modeled via Markov or renewal processes.
Inference methods:
	Conditional Laplace transforms.
	Pair-correlation function estimation.
	Joint exceedance probability derivations.
________________________________________
C. Marked Renewal and ON/OFF Activity Models
Model structure:
Each spatial point has a temporal mark evolving according to renewal or semi-Markov dynamics.
	Interference-correlation research models time-varying transmitter activity and derives temporal autocorrelation of aggregate shot noise (Ganti & Haenggi, 2022).
Inference methods:
	Autocorrelation function estimation.
	Spectral density of interference.
	Moment-based and Laplace-domain techniques.
Relevance:
	Models EV charging sessions or converter duty cycles as renewal processes.
________________________________________
D. Vector Shot-Noise with Spatio-Temporal Correlation
Model structure:
Aggregate field observed across space and time, forming a spatio-temporal random field.
	Multi-point interference correlation results provide analytical expressions for joint spatio-temporal distributions (Ganti & Haenggi, 2022).
Inference methods:
	Joint Laplace transform inversion.
	Correlation coefficient computation.
	Simulation-based percentile estimation.
Distribution-network analogy:
	Converters = spatial points.
	Activity = charging sessions (renewal).
	Impedance kernel = propagation function.
________________________________________
3) Summary Table
Model Class	Spatial Component	Temporal Component	Inference Methods	Example Source
Cox spatio-temporal process	PPP with random intensity	Time-varying Λ(t)	Laplace functional, correlation analysis	Ganti & Haenggi, 2022
Cluster process + activity	Poisson cluster	ON/OFF or renewal	Conditional Laplace transform	Afshang & Dhillon, 2021
Marked renewal process	PPP	Renewal/Markov marks	Autocorrelation, spectral methods	Ganti & Haenggi, 2022
Vector shot noise (space-time)	Shared spatial process	Time-dependent marks	Joint exceedance probability	Ganti & Haenggi, 2022
________________________________________
4) Gaps and Future Research
	Limited application of Cox spatio-temporal models to electrical distribution networks.
	Sparse empirical estimation of temporal mark parameters for converter fleets.
	Few feeder-scale joint space-time percentile studies.
	Lack of impedance-aware spatio-temporal Laplace-transform formulations.
	Need for integrated statistical inference pipelines using field measurement data.
________________________________________
5) Bibliography (APA 7)
Afshang, M., & Dhillon, H. S. (2021). Poisson cluster process-based analysis of downlink cellular networks with multi-antenna base stations. IEEE Transactions on Wireless Communications. https://consensus.app/papers/poisson-cluster-process-based-analysis-of-downlink-afshang-dhillon/5e46a9e25e6f58a7bdb3c03c2eaa1b8a/?utm_source=chatgpt
Ganti, R. K., & Haenggi, M. (2022). Spatial and temporal correlation of the interference in wireless networks. IEEE Transactions on Communications. https://consensus.app/papers/spatial-and-temporal-correlation-of-the-interference-ganti-haenggi/9b3f9b7f1e3757a68c8cbb6d1dfeac4e/?utm_source=chatgpt

Q3: What Bayesian or likelihood-based calibration methods have been used to infer point-process parameters and mark distributions from limited grid measurements?

Recent peer-reviewed work shows that Bayesian and likelihood-based calibration of spatial point-process and mark models from limited measurements relies on (i) maximum likelihood (ML) and composite likelihood for Cox/cluster processes, (ii) Bayesian hierarchical inference with latent intensity fields, and (iii) bootstrap- or calibration-based likelihood adjustments to ensure finite-sample validity. Spatio-temporal interference models use likelihood or Laplace-functional matching to infer point-process intensity and correlation parameters from aggregate measurements (Ganti & Haenggi, 2022). Cluster-process analyses employ parameterized Neyman–Scott models with likelihood or moment-based estimation for cluster radius and parent intensity (Afshang & Dhillon, 2021). Finite-sample-valid bootstrap calibration provides likelihood-adjusted confidence regions when direct likelihood inference is unstable under limited samples (Jiang et al., 2024).
________________________________________
2) Evidence Synthesis
A. Likelihood-Based Inference for Spatial and Cluster Point Processes
Model class:
Poisson, Cox, and Neyman–Scott (cluster) processes.
	Cluster-based cellular models parameterize parent intensity, offspring distribution, and cluster radius, with parameters inferred via likelihood or moment matching to coverage/interference statistics (Afshang & Dhillon, 2021).
Inference methods used:
	Maximum likelihood estimation (MLE) for intensity parameters.
	Composite likelihood using pair-correlation functions.
	Moment matching to empirical interference moments.
Translation to grids:
	Estimate converter spatial density and clustering radius from limited feeder measurements.
	Calibrate spatial intensity via observed distortion variance.
________________________________________
B. Bayesian Hierarchical Models for Latent Intensity Fields
Model class:
Cox processes with stochastic intensity Λ(x).
	Spatio-temporal interference work derives correlation functions and Laplace functionals that can be matched to observed interference statistics for parameter inference (Ganti & Haenggi, 2022).
Inference methods used:
	Bayesian hierarchical modeling with latent intensity field.
	Posterior inference via MCMC or variational methods.
	Matching empirical spatial–temporal correlation functions.
Relevance to limited grid data:
	Enables inference of unobserved converter density from few PCC measurements.
	Naturally incorporates uncertainty quantification.
________________________________________
C. Mark Distribution and Activity Calibration
Model class:
Marked point processes with emission marks and activity states.
	Spatio-temporal interference studies incorporate activity marks with temporal correlation, inferring activity probabilities or duty cycles from observed interference statistics (Ganti & Haenggi, 2022).
Inference methods used:
	Estimation of mark distribution parameters via likelihood of aggregate shot noise.
	Moment-based estimation from observed variance and autocorrelation.
Distribution-network analog:
	Infer emission magnitude distribution or ON/OFF probability from limited voltage distortion records.
________________________________________
D. Likelihood Calibration and Finite-Sample Adjustment
Problem:
Limited measurements → unstable ML estimates.
	Calibrated bootstrap methods provide finite-sample-valid inference when classical likelihood approximations are unreliable (Jiang et al., 2024).
Methods used:
	Bootstrap likelihood calibration.
	Finite-sample coverage correction.
	Empirical coverage validation.
Relevance:
	Useful when only a few PCCs are instrumented.
	Produces conservative but valid parameter bounds.
________________________________________
3) Summary Table
Study	Model Type	Calibration Method	Data Requirement	Relevance to Grid Inference
Afshang & Dhillon, 2021	Poisson cluster process	ML / moment matching	Spatial interference stats	Estimate clustering & density
Ganti & Haenggi, 2022	Spatio-temporal PPP	Correlation matching / likelihood	Multi-point interference samples	Infer intensity & activity parameters
Jiang et al., 2024	General parametric inference	Calibrated bootstrap	Limited samples	Finite-sample-valid bounds
________________________________________
4) Gaps and Future Research
	Few direct applications of Bayesian Cox-process inference to electrical feeder data.
	Limited open datasets enabling formal likelihood estimation for converter deployments.
	Sparse joint inference of spatial density and impedance kernel parameters.
	Need for hierarchical models combining device heterogeneity and network filtering.
	Limited benchmarking of Bayesian vs. composite-likelihood methods in power systems.

Section 5 — Conclusion
5.1 Summary of contributions and main takeaways
Q1: What “key contribution statements” are typical in analytical power-quality papers that introduce new stochastic frameworks (theory + validation + engineering interpretation)?

Analytical power-quality (PQ) papers introducing new stochastic frameworks typically structure their “key contribution statements” around three pillars: (1) a novel theoretical formulation (e.g., stochastic modeling, calibrated inference, or analytical transform derivation), (2) rigorous validation via Monte Carlo simulation, bootstrap coverage analysis, or field measurements, and (3) engineering interpretation, translating mathematical results into grid-level or device-level implications. Empirical reviews show that convergence diagnostics, parameter transparency, and uncertainty quantification are increasingly treated as part of the claimed contribution (Pawel et al., 2024). Papers that combine finite-sample-valid inference or variance decomposition with practical case studies exemplify this pattern (Jiang et al., 2024); (Saarela et al., 2024).
________________________________________
2) Evidence Synthesis
A. Theoretical Novelty (New Stochastic Formulation or Inference Framework)
Typical contribution claim:
“We propose a novel stochastic/analytical framework for modeling [phenomenon], deriving closed-form expressions or calibrated estimators.”
	Finite-sample calibrated bootstrap methods explicitly present new inferential theory ensuring valid confidence sets under complex stochastic dependence (Jiang et al., 2024).
	Separable bootstrap variance decomposition offers a structured hierarchical modeling framework with theoretical justification (Saarela et al., 2024).
In analytical PQ context, this maps to:
	Derivation of Laplace/PGFL-based distortion distribution.
	Stochastic feeder-level interference modeling.
	New correlated-mark or impedance-weighted frameworks.
________________________________________
B. Rigorous Validation (Monte Carlo, Coverage, Convergence)
Typical contribution claim:
“We validate the proposed analytical model via extensive Monte Carlo simulations and demonstrate convergence and coverage accuracy.”
	Convergence monitoring and stability assessment are emphasized as methodological requirements, not optional validation steps (Sun et al., 2024).
	Simulation audits highlight the importance of reporting replication counts, seed strategies, and handling of non-convergence as part of methodological contribution (Pawel et al., 2024).
Typical validation elements:
	Monte Carlo cross-check of analytical derivation.
	Empirical coverage probability reporting.
	Stability under parameter variation.
	Sensitivity to discretisation or truncation bounds.
________________________________________
C. Engineering Interpretation and Practical Relevance
Typical contribution claim:
“We translate theoretical findings into actionable engineering insights for grid planning, converter design, or PQ compliance.”
	Hierarchical modeling research demonstrates translation of variance components into interpretable system-level uncertainty metrics (Saarela et al., 2024).
	Output-analysis research emphasizes bias-corrected interpretation of stochastic results to ensure engineering reliability of conclusions (Vahdat & Shashaani, 2022).
In analytical PQ papers, this typically includes:
	Interpreting distribution tails as overload probability.
	Relating analytical moments to compliance thresholds.
	Quantifying risk of resonance or distortion amplification.
	Providing design margin recommendations.
________________________________________
D. Transparency and Reproducibility as an Explicit Contribution
Emerging trend:
Reproducibility itself is increasingly framed as a contribution.
	Simulation audits recommend explicit reporting of convergence, missingness, and parameter settings as part of scientific contribution (Pawel et al., 2024).
Exemplary contribution statements now often include:
	Provision of algorithmic pseudocode.
	Full parameter tables.
	Replication budgets and convergence plots.
	Sensitivity analysis documentation.
________________________________________
3) Summary Table
Study	Type of Framework	Validation Approach	Engineering Translation	Contribution Pattern
Jiang et al., 2024	Calibrated stochastic inference	Finite-sample coverage validation	Reliable CI construction	Theory + validated inference
Saarela et al., 2024	Hierarchical stochastic modeling	Monte Carlo variance validation	Interpretable variance components	Analytical + engineering interpretation
Sun et al., 2024	Convergence assessment framework	Resolution-refinement diagnostics	Stability guarantees	Validation methodology
Pawel et al., 2024	Simulation transparency audit	Reporting standards analysis	Reproducibility guidance	Transparency as contribution
Vahdat & Shashaani, 2022	Monte Carlo output stabilization	Bias/variance correction	Reliable estimator interpretation	Numerical stability emphasis
________________________________________
4) Gaps and Future Research
	Few analytical PQ papers explicitly structure contributions around reproducibility and uncertainty quantification.
	Limited integration of correlated-mark stochastic geometry with engineering compliance interpretation.
	Sparse inclusion of formal convergence diagnostics in theoretical PQ papers.
	Need for standardized “key contribution” templates in stochastic power modeling publications.
	Greater emphasis needed on tail-risk engineering interpretation (e.g., rare supraharmonic events).
