Q2: What quantitative summary metrics are most convincing for reviewers (closed-form results, scaling laws, validated percentiles), and how are they usually presented?

Across recent analytical and simulation-based studies, reviewers are most convinced by quantitative metrics that combine closed-form or theoretically derived results, scaling or asymptotic laws validated by simulation, and empirically validated uncertainty summaries (e.g., percentiles, coverage probabilities, Monte Carlo standard errors). Studies emphasize that convergence diagnostics, empirical coverage probability, and finite-sample validation are critical to demonstrate robustness (Sun et al., 2024); (Jiang et al., 2024). Large-scale audits further show that transparent reporting of replication counts, failure rates, and error bounds strengthens credibility (Pawel et al., 2024). The most persuasive papers therefore present theory + simulation agreement tables, scaling plots, and validated percentile-based risk metrics.
________________________________________
2) Evidence Synthesis
A. Closed-Form or Analytical Expressions with Explicit Assumptions
Convincing metric:
Closed-form derivations (e.g., expectation, variance, transform expressions) with clearly stated assumptions and domain of validity.
	Finite-sample-valid inference research demonstrates how calibrated analytical intervals can guarantee nominal coverage under explicit assumptions (Jiang et al., 2024).
	Hierarchical stochastic modeling shows decomposed variance expressions with analytical justification, followed by simulation confirmation (Saarela et al., 2024).
Typical presentation format:
	Theorem or Proposition with proof (Appendix).
	Explicit parameter table.
	Comparison table: Analytical vs. Monte Carlo estimate.
________________________________________
B. Scaling Laws and Convergence Behavior
Convincing metric:
Scaling relationships (e.g., error ∝ 1/√N, performance vs. parameter growth) validated numerically.
	Convergence-monitoring literature emphasizes stability plots and systematic resolution-refinement checks as evidence of estimator robustness (Sun et al., 2024).
	Monte Carlo output analysis demonstrates higher-order bias correction and variance scaling behavior under replication growth (Vahdat & Shashaani, 2022).
Typical presentation format:
	Log–log scaling plots.
	Convergence curves (estimate vs. replication count).
	Relative error tables across N values.
________________________________________
C. Validated Percentiles and Coverage Metrics
Convincing metric:
Empirical percentiles (e.g., 95th percentile risk) validated via Monte Carlo and coverage testing.
	Bootstrap comparison research highlights empirical coverage probability (CP) and interval length as primary evaluation metrics (Zrimvsek & Štrumbelj, 2024).
	Calibrated bootstrap methods provide finite-sample-valid percentile-based confidence regions (Jiang et al., 2024).
Typical presentation format:
	Table of nominal vs. empirical coverage (e.g., 95% target).
	Percentile risk curves.
	Confidence interval width comparisons.
________________________________________
D. Monte Carlo Standard Error (MCSE) and Bias Quantification
Convincing metric:
Explicit reporting of Monte Carlo standard error and bias-corrected estimates.
	Output-analysis research emphasizes reporting MCSE and bias reduction as necessary to demonstrate numerical reliability (Vahdat & Shashaani, 2022).
	Simulation audits highlight that transparent reporting of replication counts and failure handling improves reviewer confidence (Pawel et al., 2024).
Typical presentation format:
	MCSE reported alongside mean estimate.
	Bias comparison between analytical and simulated values.
	Sensitivity to random seed or tolerance changes.
________________________________________
E. Agreement Between Theory and Simulation
Convincing metric:
Close match between theoretical curve and Monte Carlo simulation with quantified error.
	Hierarchical modeling research demonstrates agreement between theoretical variance decomposition and Monte Carlo validation (Saarela et al., 2024).
	Convergence literature recommends repeated refinement to verify analytical stability (Sun et al., 2024).
Typical presentation format:
	Overlay plots (analytical vs. simulated).
	Relative error (%) annotations.
	Residual plots.
________________________________________
3) Summary Table
Study	Quantitative Metric Emphasized	Presentation Style	Reviewer-Relevant Strength
Jiang et al., 2024	Calibrated CIs, coverage	Coverage tables, proofs	Finite-sample validity
Saarela et al., 2024	Variance decomposition	Analytical + MC comparison	Theory-simulation agreement
Sun et al., 2024	Convergence diagnostics	Stability plots	Robustness evidence
Vahdat & Shashaani, 2022	MCSE & bias correction	Error tables	Numerical reliability
Zrimvsek & Štrumbelj, 2024	Percentile & CP metrics	Coverage tables	Validated uncertainty
________________________________________
4) Gaps and Future Research
	Limited use of explicit MCSE reporting in engineering journals.
	Rare formal tail-error quantification in rare-event PQ modeling.
	Few studies provide analytical + scaling + percentile validation together.
	Need for standardized presentation templates for stochastic power research.
	Greater emphasis needed on sensitivity-to-assumption analysis.

Q3: What common pitfalls in conclusion sections are cited by reviewers/editors (overreach, no limits, no actionable insight), and what best-practice alternatives exist?
Recent empirical audits of simulation and modeling studies show that reviewers frequently flag conclusion sections for overstating generality, failing to report methodological limits, ignoring non-convergence or uncertainty, and lacking actionable engineering interpretation. A large review of 482 simulation studies found widespread underreporting of missingness, convergence failures, and design constraints—issues that often propagate into overly confident conclusions (Pawel et al., 2024). Convergence-assessment research emphasizes that conclusions should reflect estimator stability and resolution sensitivity rather than assume robustness (Sun et al., 2024). Best-practice alternatives include explicitly stating domain-of-validity limits, reporting uncertainty and coverage performance, and translating quantitative findings into calibrated, conditional engineering recommendations (Jiang et al., 2024).
________________________________________
2) Evidence Synthesis
A. Overreach Beyond Model Assumptions
Pitfall:
Conclusions generalize findings beyond the parameter space, model structure, or validation regime.
	Convergence-monitoring research highlights that estimator behavior can change under refinement or structural variation, implying that conclusions must be conditional on tested settings (Sun et al., 2024).
Best-practice alternative:
	Explicitly state parameter ranges and tested configurations.
	Frame conclusions as conditional (e.g., “under tested impedance and sample sizes…”).
	Include sensitivity findings directly in concluding statements.
________________________________________
B. Failure to Report Limits or Non-Convergence
Pitfall:
Ignoring failed simulations, calibration instability, or tolerance sensitivity while claiming robustness.
	Only 23% of simulation studies reported missingness or non-convergence, suggesting that many conclusions omit methodological fragility (Pawel et al., 2024).
Best-practice alternative:
	Include a limitations paragraph quantifying convergence failures or numerical sensitivity.
	Report replication counts and stability checks.
	Summarize robustness diagnostics in the conclusion.
________________________________________
C. No Quantified Uncertainty or Coverage Reporting
Pitfall:
Presenting point estimates as definitive without confidence intervals or coverage validation.
	Calibrated bootstrap research demonstrates the importance of finite-sample-valid inference and empirical coverage validation (Jiang et al., 2024).
	Bootstrap comparison studies emphasize reporting empirical coverage probability and interval length to substantiate claims (Zrimvsek & Štrumbelj, 2024).
Best-practice alternative:
	Report confidence intervals alongside main findings.
	State empirical coverage or Monte Carlo standard error.
	Clarify uncertainty magnitude relative to engineering thresholds.
________________________________________
D. Lack of Actionable Engineering Insight
Pitfall:
Conclusions restate statistical results without translating them into practical implications.
	Hierarchical modeling research shows how variance decomposition can be interpreted in system-level uncertainty terms, linking statistical outputs to operational insight (Saarela et al., 2024).
	Monte Carlo output-analysis research highlights bias-corrected interpretation as necessary for reliable engineering conclusions (Vahdat & Shashaani, 2022).
Best-practice alternative:
	Translate percentiles into risk probabilities or design margins.
	Connect scaling laws to system capacity planning.
	Provide conditional design recommendations.
________________________________________
E. Absence of Reproducibility Framing
Pitfall:
Conclusions ignore replication, parameter disclosure, or validation reproducibility.
	Simulation transparency audits recommend integrating reporting of design parameters and validation results into scientific conclusions (Pawel et al., 2024).
Best-practice alternative:
	Conclude with explicit reference to validated convergence and robustness checks.
	Mention replication strategy and sensitivity confirmation.
________________________________________
3) Summary Table
Study	Pitfall Identified	Recommended Alternative	Reviewer-Relevant Strength

Sun et al., 2024	Unverified robustness	Convergence diagnostics in conclusion	Stability evidence
Jiang et al., 2024	No uncertainty validation	Coverage-validated CIs	Statistical reliability
Zrimvsek & Štrumbelj, 2024	No empirical percentile validation	Coverage + interval width reporting	Robust uncertainty quantification
Saarela et al., 2024	Purely statistical interpretation	System-level interpretation	Actionable insight
Vahdat & Shashaani, 2022	Ignored bias/MC error	MCSE & bias correction disclosure	Numerical reliability
________________________________________
4) Gaps and Future Research
	Few engineering journals enforce structured “limitations + uncertainty” conclusion templates.
	Rare explicit reporting of convergence failure rates in conclusion sections.
	Limited guidance on conditional generalization boundaries.
	Need for standardized checklist for conclusion robustness statements.
	Greater emphasis needed on linking uncertainty magnitude to engineering risk tolerance.

5.2 Future work
Q1: What open problems are most frequently identified in supraharmonics research (measurement standardisation, limit setting, modelling converter interactions, spatial-temporal uncertainty)?

Recent empirical supraharmonics (2–150 kHz) research consistently identifies four major open problems: (1) lack of standardized measurement methods and frequency-band definitions, (2) absence of harmonized emission and compatibility limits, (3) insufficient modeling of multi-converter interactions under realistic grid impedance, and (4) poor characterization of spatial–temporal variability and uncertainty. Field and laboratory studies show strong dependence of supraharmonic emissions on supply voltage, grid impedance, and operating stage, yet reporting practices and limit frameworks remain inconsistent (Frydlewicz et al., 2024); (Mariscotti, 2022). Measurement-chain uncertainty above 2 kHz and limited grid-level validation further complicate reproducibility and limit-setting (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024); (Daaboul et al., 2025).
________________________________________
2) Evidence Synthesis
A. Measurement Standardisation and Frequency Segmentation
Key finding: Supraharmonic measurements lack harmonized frequency binning, bandwidth, and instrumentation standards.
	Conducted emission measurements (up to 150 kHz) show that spectral content shifts with supply voltage and device type, yet frequency segmentation (e.g., ≤2 kHz vs. 9–150 kHz) varies between studies (Frydlewicz et al., 2024).
	MV/LV grid studies highlight uncertainty in instrument transformer behavior above 2 kHz and the lack of validated measurement infrastructure for supraharmonics (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024).
	High-frequency measurement-chain characterization emphasizes calibration limits and traceability challenges beyond classical harmonic ranges (Daaboul et al., 2025).
Open problem:
No universally adopted frequency bins, uncertainty budgets, or calibration standards for 2–150 kHz grid measurements.
________________________________________
B. Limit Setting and Compatibility Levels
Key finding: Empirical emission studies reveal wide variability, complicating development of compatibility or emission limits.
	EV charger measurements show device- and grid-dependent supraharmonic distortion, indicating that uniform limit-setting may not reflect real operating diversity (Mariscotti, 2022).
	Fast-charging station studies demonstrate stage-dependent waveform distortion, implying that static emission limits may not capture transient operating states (Giri & Rönnberg, 2025).
Open problem:
Absence of harmonized emission or planning limits accounting for operating states, impedance variation, and aggregation effects.
________________________________________
C. Modeling Converter–Converter and Converter–Grid Interactions
Key finding: Multi-converter environments exhibit interaction effects not predictable from single-device tests.
	EV charger studies show that distortion depends on grid short-circuit power and background distortion, highlighting interaction sensitivity (Mariscotti, 2022).
	BESS-integrated fast chargers exhibit spectral changes and even-harmonic amplification depending on operating stage and system configuration (Giri & Rönnberg, 2025).
	Grid-level analyses emphasize that supraharmonic impact depends on MV/LV topology and impedance characteristics (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024).
Open problem:
Need for validated frequency-dependent impedance models and multi-device interaction frameworks beyond single-emitter testing.
________________________________________
D. Spatial–Temporal Variability and Uncertainty
Key finding: Emissions vary with supply voltage, operating stage, and grid context, but long-term and spatial datasets remain limited.
	Voltage variation significantly changes supraharmonic emission magnitude and frequency distribution in lighting devices (Frydlewicz et al., 2024).
	Fast-charging studies classify multiple operating stages with distinct distortion signatures (Giri & Rönnberg, 2025).
Open problem:
Limited multi-site, long-duration datasets capturing temporal variability, aggregation uncertainty, and feeder-level diversity.
________________________________________

________________________________________
4) Gaps and Future Research
	Standardized 2–150 kHz frequency bin definitions and uncertainty budgets.
	Harmonized emission and compatibility limits reflecting dynamic operation.
	Frequency-dependent impedance modeling for multi-converter networks.
	Aggregation studies across feeders (urban vs. rural).
	Long-term spatial–temporal monitoring campaigns.
	Public supraharmonic measurement datasets for benchmarking.

Q2: What methodological frontiers are emerging in stochastic-geometry power modelling (correlated marks, network-aware kernels, hybrid data-driven + analytical models)?

Recent peer-reviewed work shows three clear methodological frontiers in stochastic-geometry power modelling: (1) correlated and state-dependent marks to capture dependence between device ratings, operating states, and spatial context; (2) network-aware spatial kernels and graph-constrained point processes that move beyond homogeneous Poisson models; and (3) hybrid analytical–data-driven frameworks combining stochastic geometry with Monte Carlo, bootstrap, or learning-based calibration. Convergence monitoring and finite-sample validation are increasingly emphasized to ensure that analytical Laplace/PGFL results remain numerically and statistically stable under complex mark or kernel structures (Sun et al., 2024); (Jiang et al., 2024). Simulation transparency audits further reinforce the need for reproducible handling of stochastic marks, replication budgets, and convergence diagnostics in these advanced models (Pawel et al., 2024).
________________________________________
2) Evidence Synthesis
A. Correlated and State-Dependent Marks
Frontier: Moving from independent marks (e.g., iid power ratings) to correlated, state-dependent, or hierarchical marks.
	Hierarchical model-based inference using separable bootstrap variance estimation demonstrates how dependence between spatial units and marks can be modeled and decomposed systematically (Saarela et al., 2024).
	Finite-sample calibrated bootstrap methods allow inference under complex dependence structures, supporting correlated-mark extensions (Jiang et al., 2024).
Implication for power modelling:
DER ratings, EV charging states, or inverter switching regimes can be modeled as correlated marks influenced by feeder impedance, voltage, or clustering.
________________________________________
B. Network-Aware Kernels and Graph-Constrained Geometry
Frontier: Embedding spatial processes in electrical network topology rather than Euclidean space alone.
	Convergence-assessment research in global sensitivity analysis highlights the importance of structural dependence and model stability under increasing resolution or complexity (Sun et al., 2024).
	Monte Carlo output-analysis research stresses bias correction and structural variance decomposition when model topology influences estimator stability (Vahdat & Shashaani, 2022).
Implication for stochastic-geometry power models:
	Replace homogeneous Poisson Point Processes (PPP) with graph-restricted kernels.
	Incorporate impedance-weighted distance metrics.
	Use topology-aware thinning or clustering rules.
________________________________________
C. Hybrid Analytical + Data-Driven Calibration
Frontier: Combining closed-form PGFL/Laplace results with empirical calibration via simulation or bootstrap.
	Calibrated bootstrap inference provides finite-sample correction to analytical approximations, enabling hybrid inference frameworks (Jiang et al., 2024).
	Simulation methodology audits recommend explicit validation of analytical outputs using Monte Carlo cross-checks and convergence tracking (Pawel et al., 2024).
Implication for power systems:
	Use PGFL-derived interference distributions combined with data-driven mark calibration.
	Employ machine learning to estimate mark distributions, while retaining analytical tractability.
	Validate hybrid models via Monte Carlo and empirical coverage analysis.
________________________________________
D. Uncertainty Quantification and Rare-Event Stability
Frontier: Formal tail-error and coverage validation for rare-event or heavy-tail regimes.
	Bootstrap coverage comparison research highlights empirical coverage probability as essential validation metric (Zrimvsek & Štrumbelj, 2024).
	Output-analysis research emphasizes higher-order bias correction and variance reduction in Monte Carlo-driven models (Vahdat & Shashaani, 2022).
Implication:
	Tail probability bounds for overload or resonance events require both analytical derivation and simulation validation.
	Rare-event stability is becoming a formal validation frontier.
________________________________________
3) Summary Table

Jiang et al., 2024	Statistical inference	Hybrid calibration	Finite-sample calibrated bootstrap	Analytical + data-driven fusion
Sun et al., 2024	Sensitivity analysis	Convergence diagnostics	Structural convergence assessment	Stability under complex kernels
Vahdat & Shashaani, 2022	MC output analysis	Bias & variance correction	Higher-order MC stabilization	Rare-event validation
Zrimvsek & Štrumbelj, 2024	Bootstrap methods	Coverage validation	Empirical CP comparison	Tail error quantification
________________________________________
4) Gaps and Future Research
	Limited published power-specific implementations of correlated-mark PGFL models.
	Few graph-constrained stochastic-geometry frameworks tailored to electrical feeders.
	Lack of standardized validation protocols for hybrid analytical–ML models.
	Sparse treatment of tail-probability error bounds in power reliability contexts.
	Need for benchmark stochastic-geometry test cases in power distribution networks.
________________________________________

Q3: What datasets or field trials are being used to support next-generation supraharmonic modelling and validation (EVSE monitoring campaigns, feeder measurement repositories)?
Next-generation supraharmonic (2–150 kHz) modelling is increasingly supported by (i) field EVSE monitoring campaigns in public LV grids, (ii) fast-charging station trials including BESS-integrated systems, (iii) laboratory-to-field measurement-chain validation datasets, and (iv) grid-level MV/LV monitoring studies assessing instrument and impedance effects. Empirical EV charger measurements in real distribution networks provide device- and grid-dependent distortion datasets (Mariscotti, 2022), while fast-charging station trials capture stage-dependent waveform distortion under operational conditions (Giri & Rönnberg, 2025). Measurement-chain validation and grid-level monitoring studies provide traceable datasets and instrument-performance insights needed for model calibration (Daaboul et al., 2025); (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024).
________________________________________
2) Evidence Synthesis
A. EVSE Monitoring Campaigns in Public LV Networks
Key finding: Field measurements of plug-in EV chargers in public grids provide real-world supraharmonic datasets reflecting grid impedance and background distortion.
	Empirical measurements of plug-in EV chargers in public LV networks show harmonic and supraharmonic emissions varying with charger type, load conditions, and network characteristics (Mariscotti, 2022).
Dataset characteristics:
	Real distribution feeder measurements
	Device diversity (multiple charger types)
	Grid-context sensitivity (short-circuit power, background distortion)
Modelling relevance:
Supports impedance-aware stochastic and deterministic supraharmonic emission models.
________________________________________
B. Fast-Charging Station Field Trials (Including BESS Integration)
Key finding: High-power EV fast-charging stations, especially those integrated with BESS, generate stage-dependent distortion datasets suitable for dynamic modelling.
	Field evaluation of a BESS-integrated fast-charging station classified five operating stages and reported waveform distortion variations across stages (Giri & Rönnberg, 2025).
Dataset characteristics:
	Stage-based measurement segmentation
	Transformer-side and charger-terminal recordings
	Even-harmonic amplification cases
Modelling relevance:
Enables validation of dynamic supraharmonic emission models under realistic load transitions.
________________________________________
C. Laboratory-to-Field Measurement-Chain Validation Datasets
Key finding: Traceable high-frequency measurement-chain datasets provide calibration benchmarks for supraharmonic modelling.
	Measurement and characterization studies up to 150–500 kHz analyze uncertainty budgets, calibration traceability, and sensor bandwidth limits (Daaboul et al., 2025).
Dataset characteristics:
	Laboratory and field measurements
	Defined uncertainty ranges
	Instrument performance above 2 kHz
Modelling relevance:
Supports uncertainty-aware supraharmonic propagation models and measurement validation.
________________________________________
D. MV/LV Grid-Level Monitoring Studies
Key finding: Grid-level analyses provide datasets capturing supraharmonic distortion propagation and instrument transformer behavior.
	MV/LV grid studies investigate supraharmonic distortion impact and instrument transformer performance above classical harmonic ranges (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024).
Dataset characteristics:
	Multi-voltage-level measurements
	Transformer accuracy assessment
	Grid-topology sensitivity
Modelling relevance:
Essential for validating feeder-level impedance and propagation models.
________________________________________
E. Voltage-Variation and Device-Specific Laboratory Campaigns
Key finding: Controlled voltage-variation experiments produce structured supraharmonic datasets for emission modeling.
	Conducted emission measurements of CFL and LED lamps up to 150 kHz under voltage variation demonstrate frequency shifts and amplitude sensitivity (Frydlewicz et al., 2024).
Dataset characteristics:
	Controlled supply variation
	Device-class comparison
	Band-segmented spectral analysis
Modelling relevance:
Supports voltage-dependent supraharmonic emission parameterization.

Section 6 — Declarations and Reproducibility Assets
6.1 Data/code availability statement
Q1: What data/code availability policies are common in Q1 power systems and power-quality journals, and what wording patterns are recommended?

Empirical studies of research reporting practices show that high-impact journals increasingly require a dedicated Data Availability Statement (DAS) and, where applicable, a Code Availability Statement, typically specifying repository location, access conditions, or reasons for restriction. Reviews of simulation and methodological literature indicate that transparent reporting of data, code, and computational settings remains inconsistent, with explicit recommendations to share datasets, simulation scripts, and processing details to enable reproducibility (Pawel et al., 2024). Empirical modeling studies that adopt structured bootstrap and Monte Carlo validation frequently describe algorithmic steps and data-generation procedures in sufficient detail to allow replication (Saarela et al., 2024). In Q1 power systems and power-quality journals, this typically translates into standardized sections titled “Data Availability,” “Code Availability,” or “Availability of Data and Materials,” often with repository DOIs or conditional access statements.
________________________________________
2) Evidence Synthesis
A. Dedicated Data Availability Statements (DAS)
Key finding: Empirical reviews show that structured reporting of data availability improves transparency and is increasingly expected.
	In a review of 482 simulation studies, only a minority fully reported methodological details needed for replication, leading authors to recommend explicit sharing of data, simulation code, and handling of missing or failed runs (Pawel et al., 2024).
	Transparent reporting—including availability of input data and processing steps—was identified as necessary to ensure reproducibility of computational results (Pawel et al., 2024).
Typical wording patterns observed in Q1 engineering journals:
	“The datasets generated and/or analyzed during the current study are available in the [repository name] repository, [DOI/link].”
	“The data that support the findings of this study are available from the corresponding author upon reasonable request.”
	“Publicly available datasets were analyzed in this study. These data can be found at: [URL/DOI].”
________________________________________
B. Code Availability and Algorithm Transparency
Key finding: For computational or Monte Carlo–based studies, detailed methodological description and sharing of scripts are recommended to ensure reproducibility.
	Bootstrap-based variance estimation research explicitly documents algorithmic steps and simulation procedures, demonstrating reproducible methodology and encouraging transparency in computational implementation (Saarela et al., 2024).
	Reviews of simulation practices recommend that code, seeds, and convergence handling procedures be reported or shared to prevent irreproducible findings (Pawel et al., 2024).
Common wording patterns:
	“The code used to generate the results in this study is available at [GitHub/Zenodo DOI].”
	“Custom scripts are available from the corresponding author upon reasonable request.”
	“Simulation code and parameter settings are provided in the Supplementary Materials.”
________________________________________
C. Restricted or Industry-Linked Data
Key finding: When data cannot be publicly shared (e.g., proprietary grid measurements or industry-linked EVSE datasets), journals expect justification.
	Transparency literature emphasizes disclosure of data restrictions and explanation of access limitations to avoid ambiguity (Pawel et al., 2024).
Typical wording:
	“The data are not publicly available due to confidentiality agreements with [industry partner].”
	“Data sharing is restricted under institutional policy but may be available upon reasonable request subject to approval.”
In power-quality research, this is common when field measurements are obtained from utilities or equipment manufacturers.
________________________________________
D. Increasing Expectation of Reproducibility Documentation
Key finding: Empirical methodological research highlights underreporting of simulation failures and convergence issues, recommending structured reporting standards.
	Only 23% of reviewed simulation studies reported missingness or non-convergence, indicating that stronger policy enforcement is needed (Pawel et al., 2024).
Implication for Q1 power systems journals:
	Data and code availability sections are increasingly mandatory.
	Supplementary materials often include parameter sets, simulation settings, and algorithm descriptions.
	Clear separation between data availability, funding disclosure, and COI statement is standard formatting.

Q2: What repositories and artefact formats are most used for analytical+simulation power research (GitHub/Zenodo, parameter files, notebooks), and what metadata is expected?

Empirical research on computational and simulation reporting shows that analytical and simulation-based studies most commonly use public code repositories (e.g., GitHub) linked to archival repositories (e.g., Zenodo with DOI minting), alongside supplementary materials hosted by journals. Typical artefacts include parameter/configuration files, simulation scripts, Jupyter notebooks, Monte Carlo seeds, and processed datasets, with metadata documenting software versions, input data provenance, and execution instructions. Reviews of simulation studies emphasize that reproducibility requires structured metadata (algorithm description, parameter settings, random seeds, hardware/software environment, and convergence handling) (Pawel et al., 2024). Empirical modeling studies that implement bootstrap or Monte Carlo procedures demonstrate detailed algorithmic documentation and parameter disclosure as a reproducibility benchmark (Saarela et al., 2024).
________________________________________
2) Evidence Synthesis
A. Repository Platforms: GitHub + Archival DOI Repositories
Key finding: Empirical reviews show that reproducible simulation research increasingly links executable code repositories with long-term archival storage.
	A large review of 482 simulation studies identified underreporting of computational artifacts and recommended explicit sharing of code, data, and failure-handling procedures to improve reproducibility (Pawel et al., 2024).
	Empirical bootstrap-based modeling research demonstrates structured reporting of algorithmic steps, implying that sharing structured scripts and computational artifacts is expected for replication (Saarela et al., 2024).
Observed common practice in analytical/simulation power research:
	GitHub (version-controlled development repository)
	Zenodo (DOI-minted snapshot of GitHub release)
	Journal supplementary files (PDF + ZIP archive)
________________________________________
B. Artefact Formats Commonly Shared
Key finding: Reproducibility literature stresses that executable scripts and parameter configurations must accompany publications.
	Simulation reporting analysis shows that missing reporting of convergence, simulation seeds, or excluded runs undermines reproducibility (Pawel et al., 2024).
	Empirical hierarchical modeling studies include algorithm descriptions sufficient to reconstruct Monte Carlo variance estimation, reflecting the importance of detailed parameter and procedure disclosure (Saarela et al., 2024).
Typical artefacts in analytical + simulation power research:
	Source code files (.m, .py, .jl, .cpp)
	Jupyter notebooks (.ipynb) or MATLAB live scripts
	Parameter/configuration files (.json, .yaml, .csv)
	Simulation input datasets (processed measurement data)
	Monte Carlo seed specifications
	README documentation with execution steps
	Environment specification files (requirements.txt, conda.yml, Dockerfile)
________________________________________
C. Metadata Expectations
Key finding: Empirical reproducibility research identifies specific metadata elements required to replicate simulation-based results.
	Reviews highlight the need to report number of simulation replications, convergence criteria, and treatment of failed runs (Pawel et al., 2024).
	Monte Carlo and bootstrap modeling research includes documentation of algorithm steps and computational parameters, demonstrating expected metadata completeness (Saarela et al., 2024).
Common metadata fields expected:
	Software name and version
	Programming language and interpreter version
	Random seed values
	Parameter ranges and default settings
	Number of Monte Carlo iterations
	Hardware/CPU details (when performance-sensitive)
	Data preprocessing steps
	License (MIT/GPL/etc.)
	Contact information
________________________________________
D. Archival and Citation Practices
Key finding: Long-term reproducibility requires persistent identifiers and citation-ready artefacts.
	Simulation transparency literature emphasizes that replication requires durable access to materials, not just informal sharing (Pawel et al., 2024).
Typical best-practice workflow in power research:
	Develop code on GitHub.
	Create a tagged release.
	Archive release on Zenodo (DOI assigned).
	Cite DOI in Data/Code Availability section.
	Include parameter files and notebooks in release bundle.

Q3: What examples exist of “reproducible stochastic-geometry power papers” with strong artefact sharing, and what elements made them reviewer-proof?
Directly documented examples of “reproducible stochastic-geometry power papers” with explicit artefact-sharing audits are rare, but several recent peer-reviewed stochastic or Monte Carlo–based power and infrastructure modeling studies demonstrate strong reproducibility practices. These studies are considered reviewer-proof when they provide: (i) full mathematical specification of point processes or stochastic inputs, (ii) explicit simulation parameters and random seed handling, (iii) algorithmic pseudocode or stepwise workflow, (iv) Monte Carlo replication counts and convergence diagnostics, and (v) transparent handling of failed runs or uncertainty intervals (Pawel et al., 2024); (Saarela et al., 2024). Papers that combine analytical derivations with Monte Carlo validation and publish structured computational procedures are closest to reproducible best practice.
________________________________________
2) Evidence Synthesis
A. Explicit Stochastic Model Specification + Monte Carlo Validation
Key finding: Reproducible stochastic studies clearly define the stochastic structure (distributional assumptions, dependence models) and validate via Monte Carlo replication.
	A hierarchical model-based inference study used structured Monte Carlo simulation with bootstrap-based variance estimation and reported algorithmic steps and variance decomposition, enabling reconstruction of stochastic procedures (Saarela et al., 2024).
	Transparent Monte Carlo output analysis emphasizes reporting bias correction, replication budgets, and variance reduction methods as core reproducibility elements (Vahdat & Shashaani, 2022).
Reviewer-proof elements observed:
	Closed-form derivation + stochastic simulation cross-check
	Explicit number of replications
	Convergence or stability justification
	Variance/bias reporting
________________________________________
B. Transparent Handling of Non-Convergence and Simulation Failures
Key finding: Strong reproducibility requires reporting of missing or failed simulation runs.
	A review of 482 simulation studies found that only 23% reported missingness and only 14% reported how non-convergence was handled; the authors recommend mandatory reporting of failures and seeds to ensure replicability (Pawel et al., 2024).
Reviewer-proof elements:
	Explicit count of failed simulations
	Reproducible seed strategy
	Sensitivity to sample size or replication budget
	Clear stopping criteria
In stochastic-geometry power studies (e.g., random DER placement, spatial EV clustering), this would translate to full disclosure of point-process generation parameters and Monte Carlo iteration counts.
________________________________________
C. Bootstrap and Confidence Interval Validation
Key finding: Robust stochastic studies validate results via bootstrap or calibrated confidence intervals.
	Finite-sample-valid calibrated bootstrap methods explicitly document algorithm steps and calibration parameters, demonstrating replicable inferential procedures (Jiang et al., 2024).
	Comparative bootstrap research evaluates empirical coverage probability across scenarios, illustrating how to present stochastic validation transparently (Zrimvsek & Štrumbelj, 2024).
Reviewer-proof elements:
	Empirical coverage probability tables
	Interval length reporting
	Replication count disclosure
	Finite-sample justification
________________________________________
D. Algorithmic Transparency and Workflow Disclosure
Key finding: Studies that provide stepwise computational decomposition and separable algorithm components are easier to replicate.
	The separable bootstrap variance estimation algorithm decomposed variance components explicitly and demonstrated unbiasedness via Monte Carlo study, offering a clear computational workflow (Saarela et al., 2024).
Reviewer-proof elements:
	Step-by-step algorithm
	Defined inputs/outputs
	Parameter table
	Sensitivity analysis
For stochastic-geometry power papers (e.g., random grid node placement, PPP-based EV arrival modeling), this means publishing:
	Distribution parameters
	Spatial domain assumptions
	Seed and RNG type
	Sample size and replication logic
	Analytical + simulation consistency checks

6.2 Reproducibility checklist (parameters, seeds, tolerances)
Q1: What reproducibility checklists are recommended for Monte Carlo studies (sample size justification, convergence checks, random seed reporting, tail error bounds)?

Recent peer-reviewed research recommends that Monte Carlo (MC) studies include a structured reproducibility checklist covering: (1) justification of simulation sample size (number of replications), (2) explicit convergence diagnostics and stability analysis, (3) reporting of random seed strategy and stochastic inputs, (4) transparent handling of non-convergence or failed runs, and (5) uncertainty quantification with tail error or coverage validation. Empirical audits show that many studies underreport missingness and convergence issues, prompting calls for mandatory disclosure of replication counts and failure rates (Pawel et al., 2024). Methodological research further emphasizes convergence assessment for global sensitivity metrics and MC estimators (Sun et al., 2024). Best practice also includes validated confidence intervals or calibrated bootstrap procedures to ensure nominal coverage (Jiang et al., 2024).
________________________________________
2) Evidence Synthesis
A. Sample Size (Replication Count) Justification
Key finding: MC studies should justify the number of replications based on estimator stability or coverage targets.
	A review of simulation studies found inconsistent reporting of replication size and recommended explicit disclosure and justification of simulation repetitions (Pawel et al., 2024).
	Convergence assessment literature highlights that sensitivity or MC metrics require formal evaluation of convergence stability rather than arbitrary replication counts (Sun et al., 2024).
Checklist item:
	State number of MC replications.
	Provide rationale (e.g., stabilization of mean/variance, CI width threshold).
	Demonstrate stability via incremental replication plots or error bounds.
________________________________________
B. Convergence Diagnostics and Stability Checks
Key finding: Proper convergence monitoring is often deficient and should be explicitly documented.
	A comprehensive review identified multiple methods for assessing convergence of MC-based sensitivity measures and emphasized systematic convergence testing (Sun et al., 2024).
	Simulation audit research found underreporting of non-convergence and recommended mandatory quantification of failed runs (Pawel et al., 2024).
Checklist item:
	Define convergence criterion (e.g., relative error < ε).
	Report convergence diagnostics (trace plots, stability curves).
	Report frequency and handling of failed/non-convergent simulations.
________________________________________
C. Random Seed Reporting and Stochastic Input Transparency
Key finding: Reproducibility requires reporting RNG strategy and stochastic input distributions.
	Simulation methodology reviews recommend sharing seed handling procedures and stochastic input definitions to prevent irreproducibility (Pawel et al., 2024).
Checklist item:
	State RNG type and software version.
	Report seed(s) or seed-generation protocol.
	Fully specify probability distributions and parameters.
________________________________________
D. Tail Error Bounds and Confidence/Coverage Validation
Key finding: MC inference should quantify uncertainty with validated coverage or finite-sample guarantees.
	Calibrated bootstrap procedures ensure finite-sample-valid confidence sets and explicitly describe calibration algorithms (Jiang et al., 2024).
	Comparative bootstrap research shows the importance of evaluating empirical coverage probability and interval length across simulations (Zrimvsek & Štrumbelj, 2024).
Checklist item:
	Report nominal confidence level (e.g., 95%).
	Provide empirical coverage probability.
	Report interval length or Monte Carlo standard error.
	Quantify tail error or rare-event estimation accuracy where relevant.
________________________________________
E. Reporting of Missingness and Simulation Failures
Key finding: Explicit reporting of simulation failures is recommended but often absent.
	Only 23% of reviewed simulation studies reported missingness; authors recommend quantifying and reporting even when no failures occur (Pawel et al., 2024).
Checklist item:
	Report number and percentage of failed runs.
	Describe exclusion criteria.
	Provide sensitivity analysis including/excluding failed cases.
________________________________________

4) Gaps and Future Research
	No standardized Monte Carlo reproducibility checklist adopted across engineering journals.
	Limited enforcement of seed reporting in applied power research.
	Few studies quantify tail error bounds explicitly in rare-event power reliability simulations.
	Need for harmonized reporting standards across simulation-heavy disciplines.
	Greater adoption of automated convergence diagnostics reporting tools.

Q2: What numerical stability/tolerance reporting is standard for Laplace/PGFL-based computations (integration tolerances, truncation bounds, discretisation error)?

Recent peer-reviewed computational and Monte Carlo studies indicate that standard numerical-stability reporting for Laplace- or PGFL-based computations includes: (i) explicit absolute/relative integration tolerances, (ii) truncation limits for infinite integrals or series with sensitivity checks, (iii) discretisation resolution (step size or quadrature nodes), (iv) convergence diagnostics under refinement, and (v) Monte Carlo cross-validation with reported standard errors or coverage metrics. Empirical audits show that convergence and failure handling are frequently underreported, prompting recommendations for mandatory tolerance disclosure (Pawel et al., 2024). Convergence-assessment research further recommends systematic monitoring of estimator stability under increasing resolution or replication budgets (Sun et al., 2024). When analytical approximations feed inferential outputs, finite-sample coverage validation or calibrated bootstrap procedures are advised (Jiang et al., 2024).
________________________________________
2) Evidence Synthesis
A. Integration Tolerances and Stopping Criteria
Key finding: Explicit reporting of numerical tolerances and convergence thresholds is recommended but inconsistently applied.
	A large audit of simulation studies found widespread underreporting of convergence diagnostics and recommended explicit disclosure of stopping criteria and numerical settings (Pawel et al., 2024).
	Convergence-monitoring research emphasizes systematic evaluation of estimator stability under increasing sample size or refinement, rather than assuming convergence (Sun et al., 2024).
Standard elements reported:
	Absolute and/or relative integration tolerance (e.g., ε_abs, ε_rel).
	Maximum iteration counts or adaptive quadrature criteria.
	Sensitivity test using stricter tolerances to demonstrate numerical stability.
________________________________________
B. Truncation Bounds for Infinite Integrals or Series
Key finding: Infinite-domain integrals (common in Laplace/PGFL formulations) require explicit truncation bounds with bias assessment.
	Robust Monte Carlo output analysis highlights the need for bias correction and higher-order error control when approximations truncate infinite sums or integrals (Vahdat & Shashaani, 2022).
	Calibrated bootstrap methods demonstrate how finite-sample inference can compensate for approximation or truncation error in downstream estimators (Jiang et al., 2024).
Standard elements reported:
	Upper/lower integration bounds (e.g., R_max).
	Justification via integrand decay threshold (e.g., |f(x)| < δ).
	Sensitivity comparison with expanded bounds.
	Quantitative estimate of truncation error impact.
________________________________________
C. Discretisation Error and Grid Refinement
Key finding: Discretisation resolution must be reported and validated via refinement analysis.
	Convergence assessment research recommends resolution-refinement checks to confirm stability of computed metrics (Sun et al., 2024).
	Simulation audits recommend explicit reporting of discretisation levels and sensitivity to grid density (Pawel et al., 2024).
Standard elements reported:
	Step size (Δx) or number of quadrature nodes (N).
	Quadrature rule (e.g., Gauss–Legendre, adaptive Simpson).
	Relative difference between coarse and refined grids.
	Stopping condition based on discretisation error threshold.
________________________________________
D. Monte Carlo Cross-Validation and Error Quantification
Key finding: Analytical Laplace/PGFL expressions are frequently validated via Monte Carlo simulation with reported standard errors.
	Robust output-analysis methodology emphasizes reporting Monte Carlo standard error (MCSE), bias correction, and variance reduction techniques (Vahdat & Shashaani, 2022).
	Simulation transparency research recommends reporting replication counts, seed strategy, and failure frequency (Pawel et al., 2024).
Standard elements reported:
	Number of Monte Carlo replications.
	Monte Carlo standard error (MCSE).
	Relative error between analytical and simulated results.
	Stability under alternative seeds.
________________________________________
E. Inferential Stability and Coverage Validation
Key finding: When numerical integration feeds inferential metrics (e.g., outage probability CIs), coverage validation is expected.
	Calibrated bootstrap approaches provide finite-sample-valid confidence sets under complex numerical approximations (Jiang et al., 2024).
Standard elements reported:
	Nominal confidence level (e.g., 95%).
	Empirical coverage probability.
	Interval width and stability under tolerance refinement.

Q3: What common reproducibility failures are flagged in peer review (missing parameter tables, unspecified kernels, hidden calibration), and how do exemplary papers avoid them?

Peer-reviewed audits of simulation and computational studies (2021–present) consistently flag reproducibility failures such as: (i) missing or incomplete parameter tables, (ii) unspecified stochastic kernels or distributional assumptions, (iii) hidden calibration steps or unreported convergence failures, and (iv) absent replication counts or seed strategies. A large review of 482 simulation studies found widespread underreporting of missingness, non-convergence, and simulation design details (Pawel et al., 2024). Convergence-assessment research further emphasizes that failure to report discretisation, refinement, or stability diagnostics undermines credibility (Sun et al., 2024). Exemplary papers avoid these issues by providing explicit algorithmic workflows, parameter tables, convergence criteria, and empirical coverage validation (Jiang et al., 2024).
________________________________________
2) Evidence Synthesis
A. Missing Parameter Tables and Simulation Design Details
Failure flagged: Studies often omit complete parameter listings (e.g., distribution parameters, sample sizes, tolerance thresholds).
	A cross-disciplinary audit found that many simulation studies failed to report core design elements such as replication counts, handling of failures, or complete simulation settings (Pawel et al., 2024).
How exemplary papers avoid this:
	Provide full parameter tables (distribution parameters, iteration counts, tolerances).
	Report simulation replication budgets explicitly.
	Document inclusion/exclusion criteria for runs.
________________________________________
B. Unspecified Kernels or Distributional Assumptions
Failure flagged: Analytical and stochastic studies sometimes assume spatial kernels, prior distributions, or mark structures without full specification.
	Convergence-assessment literature stresses that estimator stability depends on structural assumptions and refinement levels, requiring explicit reporting of model structure and discretisation scheme (Sun et al., 2024).
How exemplary papers avoid this:
	Clearly define stochastic kernels, distributions, and dependence structure.
	Provide mathematical expressions and parameter values.
	Demonstrate sensitivity to alternative structural assumptions.
________________________________________
C. Hidden Calibration or Post-hoc Adjustment
Failure flagged: Calibration steps (e.g., tuning parameters to match outcomes) are often not fully disclosed.
	Finite-sample-valid inference research shows that calibration procedures must be explicitly described and algorithmically specified to ensure replicability (Jiang et al., 2024).
How exemplary papers avoid this:
	Publish calibration algorithms step-by-step.
	Distinguish between training and validation phases.
	Quantify calibration error or bias correction impact.
________________________________________
D. Unreported Non-Convergence and Simulation Failures
Failure flagged: Simulation failures or convergence issues are often omitted.
	Only 23% of reviewed simulation studies reported missingness or non-convergence; authors recommend mandatory quantification and reporting of such events (Pawel et al., 2024).
How exemplary papers avoid this:
	Report frequency of failed runs.
	Provide convergence criteria and stopping rules.
	Include robustness checks with alternative seeds or tolerances.
________________________________________
E. Absence of Error Bounds or Coverage Validation
Failure flagged: Analytical results often lack empirical validation (e.g., no confidence interval or Monte Carlo error estimate).
	Convergence and uncertainty literature recommends empirical coverage validation and stability testing (Sun et al., 2024); (Jiang et al., 2024).
How exemplary papers avoid this:
	Provide Monte Carlo cross-validation.
	Report confidence intervals with empirical coverage.
	Include bias correction and sensitivity to sample size.
________________________________________

4) Gaps and Future Research
	Limited auditing of reproducibility failures specifically in power systems journals.
	No standardized parameter-table template for stochastic-geometry power papers.
	Sparse enforcement of seed-reporting requirements.
	Inconsistent reporting of calibration and validation separation.
	Need for structured reproducibility checklists in engineering peer review.

6.3 Conflict of interest / funding statement
Q1: What conflict-of-interest disclosure requirements are typical for power engineering journals, especially for industry-linked EVSE/converter studies?

Empirical analyses of journal reporting practices show that peer-reviewed journals—including engineering and applied sciences outlets—typically require: (1) a dedicated Conflict of Interest (COI) statement, (2) a funding disclosure section, and (3) clarification of sponsor involvement in study design, data analysis, and manuscript preparation. Studies of reporting quality demonstrate that incomplete or mislocated COI disclosures are common and can affect interpretation of results, especially in industry-linked research (García et al., 2024). Empirical evaluations of simulation and modeling research further show that journals increasingly expect transparency about funding, methodological failures, and reproducibility practices (Pawel et al., 2024). For industry-linked EVSE or converter studies, this typically translates into explicit declarations of corporate funding, author employment/consultancy ties, and statements on sponsor influence.
________________________________________
2) Evidence Synthesis
A. Dedicated COI Disclosure Sections Are Expected
Key finding: Empirical assessment shows that journals require explicit COI declarations, but reporting is often incomplete or misplaced.
	In a peer-reviewed assessment of 40 clinical trials, 33 studies had identifiable industry conflicts, yet 17 failed to declare COIs in the correct disclosure section, indicating that journals expect structured declarations and that deviations affect transparency (García et al., 2024).
	High prevalence of sponsor-linked favorable conclusions underscores why journals mandate clear COI sections to contextualize findings (García et al., 2024).
Implication for power engineering journals:
Applied engineering journals (e.g., IEEE, MDPI, IET, Springer) commonly mirror this structure—requiring a distinct “Conflicts of Interest” section separate from acknowledgments.
________________________________________
B. Funding Disclosure and Sponsor Role Transparency
Key finding: Empirical studies emphasize that funding source alone is insufficient; journals increasingly expect clarification of sponsor involvement.
	Industry-funded studies were frequently associated with favorable conclusions and elevated risk of bias, suggesting that journals require transparent funding statements to contextualize interpretation (García et al., 2024).
Typical expectations in engineering journals (observed practice in empirical articles):
	Identification of funding agency or corporate sponsor
	Statement of whether sponsor influenced experimental design or analysis
	Disclosure of employment, equity, patents, or consultancy relationships
For EVSE/converter studies—often supported by manufacturers or grid operators—this commonly includes explicit statements such as: “The funder had no role in data collection, analysis, or manuscript preparation.”
________________________________________
C. Reproducibility and Transparency in Industry-Linked Simulation Studies
Key finding: Reporting transparency—including failed runs, non-convergence, and data exclusions—is frequently underreported, and journals increasingly expect explicit disclosure.
	Among 482 simulation studies, only 23% reported missingness and only 14% described handling of non-convergence, leading authors to recommend mandatory transparency practices (Pawel et al., 2024).
Implication for power engineering journals:
For converter performance benchmarking, harmonic mitigation studies, or AI-based EVSE validation:
	Journals expect disclosure of excluded data or failed hardware tests
	Clear separation of sponsor-provided datasets vs. independently collected data
	Increasing expectation of code/data availability statements

	No empirical study specifically analyzing COI reporting practices in power engineering journals.
	Limited evidence on whether industry funding biases reported PQ or EVSE performance metrics.
	Need for comparative analysis of industry-funded vs. independent EVSE/converter studies.
	Development of engineering-specific transparency checklists (analogous to CONSORT in medicine).
	Greater adoption of open benchmarking datasets in EVSE and converter validation research.

Q2: What funding acknowledgement conventions are standard (grant identifiers, institutional support, equipment funding), and how are they formatted?

Empirical studies of journal reporting practices show that standard funding acknowledgements typically include: (1) the full name of the funding agency, (2) specific grant or project identifiers, (3) institutional or governmental support statements, and (4) clarification of the funder’s role in study design and analysis. Research examining disclosure quality finds that incomplete placement of funding/COI statements reduces transparency and interpretability (García et al., 2024). Empirical reviews of simulation and methodological studies further recommend explicit reporting of financial support, equipment provision, and computational resources to support reproducibility (Pawel et al., 2024). Standard formatting uses a dedicated “Funding” section separate from “Conflicts of Interest,” often with structured sentences specifying grant numbers and sponsor roles.
________________________________________
2) Evidence Synthesis
A. Inclusion of Funding Agency Names and Grant Identifiers
Key finding: Peer-reviewed journals expect explicit identification of funding bodies and associated grant numbers.
	An empirical assessment of disclosure practices found that although many studies had industry-linked funding, funding and COI information were sometimes misplaced or incompletely reported, indicating journal requirements for clearly labeled funding sections (García et al., 2024).
	Structured reporting was associated with clearer interpretation of study sponsorship and potential bias (García et al., 2024).
Observed formatting convention (across empirical articles):
“This work was supported by the [Funding Agency Name] under Grant No. XXXXX.”
Grant identifiers are typically presented in parentheses or following “Grant No.” or “Project No.”
________________________________________
B. Institutional and Governmental Support Statements
Key finding: Journals expect acknowledgment of institutional backing, including universities, laboratories, or national research programs.
	Empirical evaluation of reporting quality shows that transparent funding disclosure supports assessment of methodological rigor and potential bias (García et al., 2024).
	Reviews of simulation studies emphasize reporting institutional computational resources or research infrastructure where relevant to reproducibility (Pawel et al., 2024).
Typical format:
	Separate “Funding” section
	Optional “Acknowledgments” section for non-financial institutional support
	Clear distinction between financial support and general assistance
________________________________________
C. Equipment, Infrastructure, and In-Kind Contributions
Key finding: Transparency recommendations include disclosure of material or equipment support, particularly in technical and simulation-heavy fields.
	Empirical analysis of simulation reporting shows widespread underreporting of methodological limitations and resources, leading authors to recommend fuller transparency regarding tools, computational resources, and procedural details (Pawel et al., 2024).
Common conventions observed in peer-reviewed engineering and applied science journals:
	Disclosure of equipment funding (e.g., “Measurement equipment was funded by Project XXXXX.”)
	Statement of computational platform support
	Identification of industry-provided prototypes or datasets
	Separation of “Funding” from “Conflicts of Interest”
________________________________________
D. Clarification of Funder Role
Key finding: Transparent reporting includes explicit statements about sponsor involvement.
	Studies show that when funding is industry-linked, clear statements about the funder’s role are necessary to contextualize findings (García et al., 2024).
Standard formatting pattern:
“The funders had no role in study design, data collection, analysis, decision to publish, or preparation of the manuscript.”
This statement is typically placed at the end of the Funding section or before the COI declaration.

Q3: What examples exist where COI/funding disclosures affected interpretation of power-quality results, and what transparency practices are recommended?
Direct empirical evidence (2021–present) showing that conflict-of-interest (COI) or funding disclosures altered the interpretation of power-quality (PQ) results is limited. However, broader empirical research demonstrates that industry-funded studies are more likely to report favorable conclusions and exhibit higher risk of bias, especially when COIs are incompletely disclosed. These findings imply that, in PQ research—particularly studies evaluating mitigation devices, AI tools, or proprietary control systems—transparent COI and funding reporting is critical to avoid biased interpretation. Journals increasingly expect explicit funding statements, author COI declarations, methodological transparency, and data reproducibility disclosures.
________________________________________
2) Evidence Synthesis
A. Empirical Evidence that COI Influences Study Conclusions
Key finding: Industry-associated COIs are linked with favorable conclusions and higher bias risk in empirical studies.
	A systematic empirical assessment of clinical trials found that 33 of 40 studies had industry-related COIs, many with high risk of bias; 29 reported conclusions favorable to the sponsor’s product, and several contained conclusions inconsistent with results (García et al., 2024).
	The same study documented that 17 studies failed to declare COIs in the appropriate section, highlighting transparency gaps that could affect interpretation (García et al., 2024).
Implication for power-quality research:
While not PQ-specific, this empirical evidence suggests that studies evaluating proprietary PQ mitigation systems (e.g., semiconductor devices, AI classifiers, or inverter control platforms) may require careful scrutiny when industry-funded.
________________________________________
B. Transparency and Reporting Expectations in Empirical Research
Key finding: Empirical reviews show widespread underreporting of methodological limitations and simulation failures, reinforcing the importance of transparency.
	A review of 482 simulation studies found that only 23% reported simulation missingness, and only 14% described how non-convergence was handled, recommending explicit reporting and code sharing (Pawel et al., 2024).
	The authors recommend quantifying and reporting methodological limitations—even when none occur—to improve reproducibility (Pawel et al., 2024).
Implication for power-quality research:
For PQ studies involving field trials, hardware validation, or AI benchmarking, journals increasingly expect:
	Explicit funding statements
	COI declarations per author
	Clear separation of sponsor involvement from analysis
	Disclosure of failed runs or excluded data
	Data and code availability statements
________________________________________
C. Current Practice in Power-Quality Journals (Empirical Observation)
Recent PQ empirical studies (e.g., inverter control experiments, AI-based disturbance detection) commonly include:
	Dedicated “Funding” and “Conflicts of Interest” sections
	Statements declaring no sponsor role in study design or analysis
	Disclosure of experimental platforms or proprietary hardware
Although these papers do not empirically test COI effects, the presence of standardized disclosure sections reflects journal-level transparency expectations (e.g., Applied Sciences, Scientific Reports, Energies).
